O, children, since I have completed this book for you, which is the last of the science of medicine, by its completion, its purpose is fulfilled, and through its expositions and declarations, it seems to me that I complete it for you with this treatise, which is a part of operative medicine, that is, surgery.
For operative medicine is now, in our region and our time, completely hidden, so that the knowledge of it is greatly endangered, and its practice is restricted; and only some small descriptions remain in the books of the ancients, which have led to errors and hesitations, so that its understanding is clouded and its strength and art are lost.
And it seems to me that I will revive that [operative medicine] through the arrangement of this treatise, in accordance with a method of exposition, explanation, and abbreviation; and so that it will be understood through the forms of cautery tools and other instruments of the craft, with the addition of explanation and preparation for it, which is necessary.
But the reason why a skilled craftsman with his own hands is not found in our time is that the art of medicine is extensive, and that its practitioner must first be practiced in the science of anatomy, which includes knowledge of the body parts and their forms, and the connection and separation of them; and must have knowledge of bones, nerves, ligaments, and their arrangement; and of the pulsating and still veins, and the places of their exit.
And therefore Hippocrates says that a name is a word, but little in practice, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already said this in the introduction to this book; for whoever is not familiar with what we have said about anatomy, will inevitably fall into errors that will cause the death of men, as I have seen too many of them, for some are trained in this science and abandon it without knowledge and experience.
For I saw a foolish and inexperienced physician cut into a scrofulous abscess on a woman's neck, and cut some of the neck's arteries, so that a great deal of blood flowed until the woman died in his hands.
And I saw another physician who attempted to remove a stone from a man who had suffered from it for many years.  The stone was large, and he managed to extract it, along with a piece of the bladder, which caused the man to die on the third day.
And I, having been called to treat that case, saw that because of the size of the stone and the patient's condition, I knew he would die.
And I saw another physician who received a stipend from a duke in our land for surgery, and was called to a black knight who had a fractured thigh.  The physician, through his ignorance, treated the fracture by pulling it together with bandages and splints with excessive tightness, and did not allow the wound to breathe.
And then he loosened it according to his desires, and then he reapplied it, and then he left it for a few days and ordered that they loosen the bandage.  And this continued until the thigh and foot became ulcerated and festering, and thus led to the man's demise.
And I was called to him, and I arranged to loosen the bandage, and this brought about tranquility and a lessening of his pains.
But meanwhile, decay had already set in within the limb, so that I could not halt it, and the corruption continued within the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who opened a cancerous abscess, and it festered for several days, until the severity of the disease was magnified with the patient's suffering.
Because cancerous growths are primarily caused by melancholic humors, it is not appropriate to remove them with total destruction, but rather to focus on the affected limb to the extent that such treatment is tolerable.
And therefore, children, it is necessary for you that the surgical procedure be divided into two parts: one part being the operation associated with healing, and the other part the procedure that carries significant risk in the most critical situations.
And I have already indicated this throughout this book, in the surgical procedures where there is error and fear; therefore, it is necessary for you to observe this and abandon that, so the errors do not find a way to speak and disgrace you.
Therefore, take your tools with diligence and God's protection, and treat your patients with skill and firmness.
And use the best methods, by God's grace, seeking health and praiseworthy success, and abandon those terrible illnesses of difficult healing, and remove from your tools anything that causes you fear, so that your reputation and your name may not be tarnished: for more remains for your glory, and higher in the world and behind your holiness.
Guaila already said in some of his teachings/instructions: Do not treat a serious illness, lest you be called bad doctors.
And I have divided this book into three chapters.
The division of the book begins, and first of the first part. The first chapter, ordered from head to foot, deals with the types of instruments and cauteries, and what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, on cutting and piercing, cupping, poultices, and the extraction of arrows and similar things, all categorized and ordered by the types of instruments.
The third chapter, on restoration and dislocation, and the treatment of twisting and similar injuries; a chapter ordered from head to foot, and concerning the types of instruments.
It is necessary for me to state the benefits of this cautery and its harms, and in which temperament cautery is prohibited.
Therefore, I say that the discourse on the nature of the benefits and harms of cautery is long, extensive, scientifically subtle, and secretly hidden; and many wise men have already spoken of it, and they have disagreed about it, but a brief summary of their teachings will suffice to me, fearing to prolong the discussion.
And therefore I say that cautery is beneficial to all temperaments universally, whether with or without matter, except for two temperaments, which are hot temperaments without matter.
In a hot and dry temperament with matter, there is disagreement; for some have said that cautery is beneficial in that case, and others have said the opposite, that cautery is not suitable for an illness that is characterized by heat and dryness, for the nature of fire is heat and dryness, and it is inappropriate for a hot and dry illness to be treated with something that is also hot and dry.
And those who say the opposite argue that cautery provides benefit in all hot and dry illnesses that affect the human body, because when you compare the human body and its humours to the temperament of fire, you find the human body to be cold.
And I say, based on my own experience, that this has been revealed to me several times.
However, it is not appropriate for someone to offer an opinion on this who has not previously practiced and been educated in the chapter on cautery, and who does not understand the diverse temperaments of people, the nature of illnesses themselves, their causes, their symptoms, and the duration of these illnesses.
But in other temperaments, there is no cause for concern, especially in cold and moist illnesses, for all doctors agree on their treatment and are not divided on using cautery in these cases.
And understand, children, that the secret of curing with cautery using fire, and its beneficial effects, lies in the fact that fire is simple, its action only affects the part that is cauterized, and does not harm any other attached member except with little damage.
However, the action of a burning medicine spreads to the parts of the body distant from it, and readily causes difficult-to-heal illness and considerable harm in the diseased part.
But fire, due to its intensity and beneficial nature, does not do this except excessively. And this is already known to us through experience and a long study of the art, concerning the truth of causes.  Therefore, I am excused from a lengthy discourse.  If it were not that it is not written in my book in this part, the secrets concerning the use of fire, and the way it operates in the body and how it expels illnesses, would be demonstrated in a philosophical discourse, which would be more valuable to you.
And understand, children, that they disagree on the time when cautery is best performed, and they suggest the best time is spring.
And I say that cautery is suitable at any time, because the harm from the accidental effects of time is outweighed by the benefit brought about by the cautery, especially if the cautery is needed for sharp, sudden pains, which cannot tolerate delay because the feared harm from the worsening condition is greater than any minor harm from the passage of time.
And this is not something you should take seriously, children.  The common people and foolish physicians believe that a disease cured by cautery is not always cured completely, and they believe that this is the consequence.
And the reason is not as they suppose.  Cautery is only a type of medicine that alters the constitution and dries up the moisture that causes the pain.
But nonetheless, cautery is superior to other medicines because of its potency, the effectiveness of its operation, and the great benefit of its use.
And it is possible that the illness may return at some point in time, depending on the patient's constitution, the location of the illness, the patient's strength, and the accumulation of excess fluids in the body, and what they have eaten and things similar to that; however, if the illness for which cautery is administered is a minor illness in a part of the body with few excess fluids and moisture, similar to toothache and similar cases, it is possible that the pain will not return, but this will take a little time.
And the common saying is that cautery is the last resort in medicine, and this is a true statement, not in the way they understand it, because they believe that there is no cure that is as beneficial as cautery.
And the truth is the opposite.  The idea that cautery is the last resort in medicine is only true when we've tried other methods of treatment for certain illnesses, and those other methods aren't effective; then, and only then, cautery is used as a final, effective treatment. Therefore, the statement that cautery is the last resort in medicine is not true, according to the understanding of the common people and many foolish doctors.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they didn't say this without reason; it's because of the equal and superior nature of gold's substance.
And they said that the cautery location doesn't make a difference, but that's not entirely true.  I've proven it; I've found that this only applies in some bodies, not all.  Cautery with gold is better than with iron, as they say; however, when you use gold cautery, it's not at all clear to you how much heat you are applying due to the gold's resistance to heat. It makes the area ready to receive the heat; if you increase the heat, the gold melts into the fire and some pieces of gold fall into the fire, and the craftsman is therefore harmed by this.
Therefore, cautery with iron is done more easily and more appropriately for the success of the procedure.
And I have organized this chapter on cautery according to topics, and I have organized those from head to feet, so that the reader can find what they need.
The first chapter.
This cauterization addresses dampness and coldness affecting the brain, the causes of which are twofold: one is the cause of excessive and numerous rheumatic fluxes, called "coriza," flowing from the head to the eye and ear areas; the other is excessive sleepiness, and toothaches, and throat pain, and generally all ailments arising from cold, including paralysis, stroke, and similar conditions.
The method of this cauterization is as follows: first, instruct the patient to be cleansed with laxative medicine, cleansing the head, for three or four nights, according to the patient's strength, age, and usual condition.
Then order him to shave his head, and afterwards, place him seated between your hands on a smooth bench, and let him place his hands on his feet.
And then, place the tip of your palm near the bridge of his nose, between his eyes. However, when the tip of your middle finger has reached the spot, mark that spot with a stylus, and then apply the olive-oil cautery, of which this is the form:
Then place that [cautery] on the spot marked with the stylus, in such a way that it descends, with which your hand is gradually tightened, and you rotate it.
Then lift your hand slightly and observe the spot, and if you see any significant amount of bone exposed, as much as a grain of corn, then raise your hand; and if not, return your hand with that iron, with another from that set, if it has cooled, until the bone is visible as I have told you.
Then take a little salt and dissolve it on the spot and leave it for three days.
Then place some cloth soaked in broth/broth-like liquid over it and leave it until the scab from the fire has been destroyed. Then treat the area with tetrafarmacon ointment until it is healed.
And if they say that while the wound remains open, the arm should be lifted higher, for it is better and more beneficial.
And some of them say that the bone should be cauterized down to the bone, and the cautery should be held until some part of the bone's substance is adhered to it.
Then scrape off whatever is adhering to the bone, and then treat it.
And others say that with the cautery they should get to the back of it, pressing forcefully onto the bone until it bursts, and this is the method of a small cautery. And they believe that from that place, the vapors from the head escape.
And let the wound be kept open for a long time, and then treated until it is healed.
However, I have not seen these two types of cautery in everyone, but only in some cases, out of fear; and in my view, it is better to use healing methods when they are; for the head is weakened by a breach in its continuity, just as we have seen in other parts of the body, especially when the patient's head is of a weak constitution.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better in my opinion; and with that method and practice, there is healing.
Second Chapter.
Concerning cautery of the head.
When chronic and long-lasting pain affects the entire head, in a prolonged illness, and remedies such as leeches, cupping, head purges, plasters, poultices, herbs, poultices, and similar treatments have been used, and especially if the head has already been cauterized with a cautery that has provided no benefit, then consider that if the patient's head is strong in its constitution and not weak, and finds itself with a very severe cold, then cauterize with another cautery slightly below the previous one. And then cauterize the area above the patient's temples with the cautery until the pain is relieved. Cut open the affected area, and if bone is visible, cauterize that area with a cautery at the back of the head, in the place called the *vas* (or vessel) – which is where the cupping is applied. And be careful in doing this, and do not expose the bone, for the patient will experience very severe pain different from the pain of other cauterizations on the head.
And I would say this cautery is in its proper place, for the cautery used for cauterizing the temples and the back of the head should be a finer cautery than the one used for cauterizing the middle of the head; and this is the method to follow.
Third Chapter.
Concerning the cauterization of non-chronic headaches.
When a persistent or spreading pain occurs in the head, or extends to the eyes, the head should be cleansed with medicaments, and the other treatments mentioned in my descriptions of illnesses should be administered; and if nothing works, then cauterization in that area can be done in two ways: either with a burning medicinal cautery, or with iron.
But the cautery with a burning medicinal substance is that you take a clove of garlic, peel and trim its ends from both sides. And then, you pierce the painful spot in the temple with a sharp lancet until a small cavity is created under the scalp, until it is hidden.
Then, press on it with a bandage to compress it firmly, and leave it for three hours; and then remove it and take away the garlic, and let the wound rest for two or three days, and then place a cloth soaked in vinegar there until the area heals.
And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines, which I have written about in the second treatise on burning medicines.
But the cauterization with iron, following this method:  heating by cautery, of which this is the form:
and it is called "clavus clavilus," because the head of it is shaped like a nail, in which there is some curvature, and in the middle of it there is a small eminence.
And then place it on the painful area, and hold your hand—so that the surrounding tissue is not burned—and rotate the small iron bit by bit with speed.
And if the amount of tissue that is being burned, through the nature of the cautery, is, as it were, half of it, then lift your hand to avoid burning the underlying artery, so that blood does not flow.
And then soak cotton in salt water and place it on the area, and leave it for three days; then place cotton moistened with butter, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the edge of the knife, according to the cautery method, and be careful not to cut an artery in this particular, not-old, migraine.
Fourth Chapter.
Of the cauterization of old migraines.
When you treat a migraine using the methods previously discussed, and the treatments outlined in the sections on illnesses, and the cure proves ineffective, and sometimes because the disease is too far gone, when the previous cauterizations with medicine or with fire fail, then you must heat the knife cautery until it is white-hot. Then apply it to the painful area, following a line whose length is half a finger, or a bit longer.  Then, apply your hand once and press firmly until you penetrate the artery and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to be careful not to damage the continuation of the jaw, which is strained by mastication, and that the tendon and nerves that move it are not injured and cause spasms.
And if it concerns the control and caution of the flow of blood from the cut artery, for in the cutting of it there is fear, especially for those who are ignorant of what they are doing and are not skilled or practiced; and they leave the operation unfinished at the beginning.
And I will come back to the treatment of accidental blood flow from the artery, according to its method, in its proper place in this book.
And if you see that this treatment is not sufficient for this illness, and you find the patient's condition tolerable, cauterize the middle of the head as we have described, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you wish, apply the cautery mentioned in the chapter on arterial constriction with a two-bladed cautery; this is a better and more effective cauterization.
Chapter 5.
Of the cauterization of ear aches.

And if the cautery is used to apply ten points to the whole ear, and cauterizes them, then treat the spots until they are healed.
Chapter 6.
Of the cauterization of tumors.
Tumors that are cured with cauterization are only of the types that come from phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of diseases.
Avoid cauterization of the type that arises from dryness or spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that type of tumor with leeches, purges, and remedies, and your treatment does not work, then you must cauterize the patient with three cauteries: one at the base of the ear, a second a little below the temple, and a third at the juncture of the two lips.
And place your cautery opposite the diseased part, for softening only comes to the part that appears healthy.
And the method of cauterization is to cauterize with the cautery at the right end of the ear, then slightly above the horn of the head; another one at the temple, the length of which should be according to the length of the index finger; and keep your hand with the cautery until the quantity of half the thickness of the tumor is burned.
And this is the method of cauterization, and it is a type of surgical procedure, the method of which is described below. However, it is more subtle than that which you see; and it is necessary to cut away some of the superfluous thickness there.
And then treat the area with what has been previously described until it is healed.

Chapter 10.
Concerning the cauterization of epilepsy.
The epileptic is not cauterized unless the epilepsy is caused by phlegm.
Therefore, it is necessary to treat the brain of the patient at the beginning of the illness, using herbs and other treatments as we have described in the division when the illness is severe and can tolerate the application of medicine.
And if it is a child who cannot tolerate medicine, then administer gargles and masticatories to treat the brain, before this for many days, with proper nourishment and diet.

Let the cautery be olive-shaped, as was previously mentioned.
But if the patient is a child, then place a delicate cautery according to this method.

Of the cauterization of melancholy.

And then shave the patient's head, and then carefully apply a properly made plaster to the scalp, and then place it in the middle of the head.
And the patient should be seated upright, and be held securely in all parts.
And then apply liniment; and an old sheep's fleece is even more suitable. And warm that over a moderate fire, to the degree that the patient can tolerate it being placed on them. And then apply that to the middle of their head, within the scalp, and leave it until it cools.
And do this with the patient once a week, along with the other treatment for them, until they are healed; or if you wish, cauterize that area making small punctures according to their tolerance, but hold a cautery instrument so arranged: this is a type of cautery which moistens the brain evenly.
And then place that cloth soaked in wool or henbane.

Chapter 16.
Of cautery of the eyelid of the eye when the hairs are turned inward and cause the eye to be inflamed.
When the hairs of the eye are turned inward, causing inflammation, cauterization in such cases is done in two ways, either with burning or with a burning medicine.
If with a burning cautery, instruct the patient beforehand to remove the hairs as those who groom them, until they are elongated and smoothed out; and if you cauterize them near their origin, then you should tightly bind the eyes with a bandage so that they are not irritated until the hairs have grown back.
Then, once the hairs have grown back and smoothed out, place the patient's head in your lap, and carefully apply a cautery to the eyelids, using a pointed tool shaped like a leaf of myrtle.  Start the mark near the hairs.
And then place cotton soaked in egg white or a ptilli mucilage over the eye.
And then apply the cautery, which has this shape.
And then cauterize the marked area, little by little, many times, until the surface of the skin, which is like a myrtle leaf, is completely burned.
A sign of successful treatment is that you see the eyelid contracted, and the hairs raised towards the puncture of the eye.
And then lift your hand and leave it for three days, and then place cotton with a bandage over it until the scab is removed. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if any cause returns to the root of the hair and the eyelid is softened, then apply the cautery again just as you did before.
But if the hairs are under the eyelid, then cauterize them until they are raised to their natural position, and the wound is healed, and the hairs do not irritate the eye.
But the cautery with burning medicine—this is what you command the patient to do: to allow the hairs to grow out and heal, and then a leaf-shaped piece of cloth be made to resemble a myrtle leaf.
And then take known substances and living oil, or whatever that is, and mix them well, and do this quickly so it does not get cold; and then spread this mixture over the piece of cloth shaped like a myrtle leaf, and place this over the eye, or both eyes; and put a cloth soaked in egg white under the eye, and the patient's head should be supported by you.
And place your finger on the medicine and press it a little, and do not remove it until the patient feels the medicine's burning sensation, for the burning sensation is like that of fire.
And so, when he perceives the burning sensation, then remove the medicine and wash it away with your finger;  when the burning stops, then remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; and then consider if the eyelid has been raised as it should be, having been treated with heat or incision; if so, it is well done; and if not, reapply the medicine to the place where it wasn't applied before, and don't stop until the wound has healed and the eye is sound.
And then apply a cloth with a bandage until the burn's inflammation is gone, and then treat it with a healing ointment or something similar until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, following your procedure, to carefully ensure that no medicine gets into the eye.  And if, after a few days, the eyelid becomes softened and some cause of inflammation appears in the eye, then reapply your medicine to the softened part of the eyelid, just as you did at the beginning, and continue until it is healed.
And this is the method of using myrtle leaves.
And know that human wounds vary in size and severity, and therefore your treatment should be adjusted accordingly.
And the path to recovery will not be hidden from whoever studies this art.
Chapter 17.
Of the cauterization of a fistula in the tear duct of a wound.
When you treat a fistula using the methods discussed in the divisions of diseases, and your treatment isn't working, then you must cauterize it in this manner:  tell the patient to rest their head on your lap, and have an assistant hold the patient's head between your hands, ensuring the head isn't moved or agitated.
And then place a piece of lint soaked in egg white or a poultice of ptilli on the wound.
And then heat the cautery, which has this shape:
which should be concave, like the shape of a reed or a vulture's wing, at the end where the cautery is applied.
And if you wish, it should be pointed at the other end. And if you want, it should be shaped like a probe.
But nevertheless, the concave one is better for your work.
And then press the fistula if it is open, and drain and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and remove the pus from it.
And then place the cautery, which is well-ignited, upon it, and hold your hand a short distance from the cautery from the nose area, so that neither your hand nor the patient is disturbed, and then place the cautery on the root of the wound and cauterize it.
And so, if at the beginning your cautery penetrates into the bone, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if necessary; and leave it like that for three days, and then apply a poultice with a bandage and surround it with a drying ointment until it is healed.
But if more than ten days have passed and it is not healed, then apply an acrid, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and is smooth as appropriate to its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula, which some earlier practitioners said:  Clean the area of the fistula and cauterize it, and then place into that fissure a small quantity of molten lead, which has this form:  And put into it the amount of one ounce of molten lead; and hold your hand firmly over it so the patient is not disturbed, as the molten lead won't enter the wound; and you should place on the wound a poultice soaked in egg white or water, for the lead will adhere to the fistula area and cure it with a remarkable healing.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said about cauterization and treatment, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to administer a treatment through the nose and to return the fistula to its proper place in the nasal cavity.
Chapter 18.
Of the cauterization of a fissure (or cleft) of the pot (presumably a part of the body, not a cooking vessel).
Many times fissures occur in the lips, and especially in the lips of children.

Chapter 19.
Of cauterization of fistulas in the mouth.
When an abscess forms at the root of the uvula, or on the palate, or at the roots of the teeth, and a boil develops, and it bursts, and becomes chronic, and forms a fistula, and you treat it but it doesn't respond to treatment, then you should use a heated cautery according to the size of the opening in the fistula, and then place that heated cautery in the fistula's opening, and keep your hand there until the heated iron reaches the bottom of the fistula, and do this one or two times; and then treat it with the cure we previously mentioned until it is healed.
And so, if the diseased tissue is separated and healed, well and good; but if not, then it is necessary to expose the area and remove the corrupted bone according to what is described in its appropriate chapter.
Chapter 20.
Of cauterization of the teeth and softening of the gums.
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth are decayed, and you treat them with remedies but there is no improvement, then place the patient's head on your lap, and then heat the cautery in the appropriate shape, so that you place the cannula on the tooth and quickly insert the heated cautery into it, and keep your hand there for a little while so the patient feels the heat of the fire reaching the root of the tooth.
And then remove your hand, and then repeat the cauterization several times as desired.
And then the patient should fill their mouth with salt water and keep it there for an hour, then spit it out, for the decayed tooth is closed, the softened gums are strengthened, and the corrupted moisture is dried up.
Chapter 21.
Of the cauterization of toothache.
When toothache is caused by cold, or there is cold or a worm within, or you do not consider medicinal treatment appropriate, then cauterization in that case is done in two ways: either with cow dung cautery or with fire cautery.
Cauterization with cow dung is that you take cow dung, boil it in an iron pot, then take a piece of cloth, wrap it around the end of a stick, dip that in the boiled dung, and quickly place it on the aching tooth until it cools.
And repeat this several times until the heat penetrates to the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, immerse wool or cloth in the aforementioned dung, place that on the aching tooth, and hold a heated iron over it until the iron reaches the area of the tooth's cavity.
However, cauterization with fire is that you use a heated iron or bronze cannula, and it should be of sufficient thickness so that the heat of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth.
Then heat the cautery to the desired shape, and place it on the tooth, holding it until the cautery cools; repeat this several times, as the pain may be relieved either that day or the next.
And it is necessary that, during the cauterization, the patient fill their mouth with good dung and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out; and this is the manner of the cauterization.
And cauterize whatever extremity you are able to.

Chapter 28.
Of the perforation of a liver abscess with cautery.
