And then treat the cauterized area for three days with a bandage until the scar from the burn has healed.
And then treat it with other ointments until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on their back for 40 days until the area and the wound are sealed.
And it is necessary to regulate the patient's diet according to the length of the healing time, so that it does not strain their stomach and cause the intestines to protrude.
And then, after approximately 40 days, administer a properly made bandage, and have them wear it for another 40 days.
And be wary of exertion, overeating, drinking, and loud noises, for by observing this regimen of healing, complete recovery will be achieved.
And I shall describe the treatment for ruptures in other parts of the abdomen, and if, at the beginning, you want to prevent them from recurring, cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size, and remove that portion which is protruding from the surrounding flesh, like one-third of it; then treat it with the methods already described, and do not deviate from them.
And some earlier practitioners might say that a rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery, following this shape, after having been marked with a probe.
And a line is placed across the widest part of the rupture, and another line above it; and then you place your hand with a wedge-shaped cautery, but the first, simpler cautery is better/easier.
Chapter 46.
Of the cauterization of torsion.
When, in some limb, pain arises from a blow or a forceful impact and that pain persists for a long time, and medicinal cures are not proving effective, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And the cautery should be applied to each limb according to its size and smallness, its strength and weakness, the location of the torsion, and the pain in it.
Therefore, if the first cauterization heals, well and good; but if not, then repeat the cauterization; for it is the custom of those pains to spread from one limb to another nearby limb, so that those pains must be treated with cauterization until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
Of cauterization of leprosy.
Some lepers are greatly helped by cauterization, especially if the type of leprosy is caused by the putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And therefore, when you wish to cauterize that person, consider if the leprosy is superficial and can be cured with what we have said in the division and not persistent, and you fear that the corruption will spread throughout the patient's entire body and constitution, then cauterize the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, a second below it, between the forehead and the hairline; and two more on the temples; and one at the back of the head, on the neck or nape.
And lastly, press the cautery slightly into the bone to create a small indentation, and from there, peel off the scabs so that the expulsion of thick vapors is eased; and cauterize the spleen as is necessary.
And if the leprosy has already spread over the patient and is evident, then you must cauterize them with those cauteries we have mentioned for the head; and with one cautery on the tip of the nose, and two on each cheekbone, and two on each vertebra of the neck, and six on the vertebrae of the back, and one large one on the vertebrae near the hips, and one on the hips, and two on each hip joint, one on each hip joint, and two on the shins, and two on the muscles, and two on the tendons of the feet; and cauterize on all the joints of the fingers and toes, and on each heel of their feet, and on the soles of their hands.
Be careful of the nerves that are near the heel so you do not burn them.
And sometimes, it is cauterized with one cautery on the hip bone, another on the mouth of the stomach, and another on the liver.
And know that the more cauteries you apply, the more helpful and beneficial it is; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery, as they do from lancets, because the body is already numb.
And the cauteries should be large or small, according to the limbs and joints, as dictated by the variety of instruments used.
And then treat the cautery with flour of eryngo mixed with honey and the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Of cauterization for numbness.
When a limb is numb or asleep and is treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then you cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the size of the limb.
And let your cautery penetrate the skin slightly. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes numbness, affecting the hands and feet, and the vertebrae of the back and the nerves that supply that limb, is cauterized, and the numbness is removed.
But do not presume upon this unless you are knowledgeable in the anatomy of the limbs and the pathways of the nerves throughout the moving body.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the cauterization of whitlows (which is morphea).
When a whitlow is old and you cannot help it with any medicinal ointments, then you should cauterize over it with a cautery, pressing it slightly according to the extent of the whitlow's severity until the whiteness and its color are removed, and its color is changed.
Then treat it with flour of rye and lentils mixed with roasted oil and arnica leaves, and with a portion of pigeon's blood or swallow's blood, all mixed together.  Then, apply a bandage to the area and keep it there until healed.
Chapter 50.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
When cancer is beginning and you wish to remove it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, in a circle.
And the learned say that it should be cauterized with the final cautery in the middle; but I do not see this, for I fear it will ulcerate, and I have seen that happen several times.
And it is more proper that it be cauterized in a circle around it, as we have said, or with many cauteries.
Chapter 51.
Of the cauterization of tumors.
When a tumor is delayed in maturation, and this is due to the excess causing it, or due to the patient's age, as they are old and frail, with little blood, or due to time, and you wish to hasten the tumor's maturation, then cauterize in a circle around it with many small, puncturing cauteries; and then leave it, for it will hasten in maturation.
But if you want to pierce it with a cautery, then you heat the cautery, of which this is the form: and place it in the middle of the tumor until it penetrates the skin.
And if the cautery is in a deeper location, so that the arm's flesh is relieved; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
Concerning the cauterization of a corrosive (or, a corrosive condition).
A corrosive is a wandering corruption in a limb, which corrodes it as if with a fire consuming dry wood.
Therefore, if this corrosive is in a location suitable for cauterization with fire, then you use small, pointed cauteries, or larger ones, depending on the size of the area affected by the corrosive.
And then cauterize that area thoroughly until you eradicate all the corrosion and no trace of it remains.
And then leave it for three days, and apply a dressing of oil mixed with herbs to the cauterized areas until all the scabs are gone and all the corruption is eliminated.
And then treat it with a healing ointment that promotes the growth of flesh.
But if after three days, healthy flesh grows where there was no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cauterization on the remaining damaged areas.
And sometimes, corrosive medicine is used as a substitute for cauterization. However, cauterization with fire is a more immediate remedy.
And I have already described the treatment with sharp medicine in the division, so use it when needed.
Chapter 53.
Concerning the cauterization of wounds and similar conditions.
Often in the parts below the sick foot, a disease called a “claw” occurs, a large, jagged wound that afflicts the foot.  Cauterization in such cases is done in two ways: with fire and with hot water.
However, cauterization with fire involves heating a concave cautery, somewhat like a small scoop, made of iron, proportionate to the size of the wound's entirety, and made of fine/thin metal.
Then, place that heated cautery on the wound, and carefully rotate your hand with the cautery in a circular motion around the wound until the cautery reaches the depth of the wound. And leave it there for three days until it begins to heal/discharge. Then, apply a poultice of crushed wild mallows with salt, and leave the poultice on it overnight, for this will eradicate the disease from its roots.
And then treat the area with a healing ointment until it is healed.
And if this doesn't work, and often this happens on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet, then you should apply cauterization with water.  Take a cannula made of bronze, iron, or a bird's feather, and place it over the wound.  Pour a small amount of hot water into the cannula, hold your hand steady, and rotate the cannula with pressure around the wound, pressing the cannula's edge into the root of the wound.  The hot water will penetrate the root of the wound.  Allow the patient to endure the hot water's effect for about an hour, then remove it, for the wound and its roots will be eradicated.
And do this with these [wounds] and then with the others until all those of their kind in the body are gone.
And then treat the pus, since the roots of the wounds are eradicated with this, with ointments which regenerate the flesh.
Chapter 44.
Of the cauterization of tremor.
When tremor is caused by cold in the nerve, or by a cold fever, or by another similar cause, then it is necessary for you to cauterize it with four or five cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, one cautery between each vertebra; and with a cautery on the soles of their feet, and on their stomach, with an olive cautery.
And the tremor of the patient will subside, and hasten the recovery from the cold illness.
Chapter 45.
Of cauterization of white spots/accidents on the body.
When white spots appear on the body, and pustules or fissures are formed from corrupted, thick matter; therefore, at the beginning when it first appears, it should be cauterized over the entire head of the fistula with a thin cautery with a myrtle stick, the end of which is heated; or with long roots of erestology; or with a lens-shaped cautery.
And sometimes, at the beginning of its expulsion, cauterization is applied in this manner; and if it is not believed (or trusted?), and the superfluity is broken up by doing these things, it is healed by these actions.
However, this should be done close to evacuation with bloodletting.
Chapter LVI.
Of cauterization of an accidental blood flow by archer's incision.
Often, an archer's incision leads to a flow of blood that is directed towards the wound, outwards, or towards the incision of abscesses or cauterization of a limb, and similar situations, and it is difficult to stop that flow.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, you should apply your hand to the opening of the archer's incision and place your index finger over it, pressing repeatedly until the flow of blood beneath your finger is stopped, and it no longer escapes.
And then place the small and large olive-shaped cauteries, many in number, in the fire, and blow on them until they are very hot.
Then, take one of those cauteries, small or large depending on the location and the wound where the artery is broken,  quickly placing it over that vein after you have removed your finger, and hold the cautery until the blood is abated and constricted.
And if the blood is expelled from the opening of the artery when you remove your finger and apply the cautery, then quickly take another cautery from the ones prepared in the fire, and continue doing this repeatedly until the blood is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is here, and that another harm does not come upon the patient.
And understand that when blood flows from the artery, it is not possible to stop it, especially when the artery is large, except by one of four methods: either with cauterization as we have described, or with extraction if it has not already been extracted, for when it is extracted the patient's extremities are more likely to be burned and the blood is stopped, or by tying it with a ligature or a bandage, firmly tightened, or by placing medicines on it whose virtue is to constrict the blood; and these should be tightened with a compress with appropriate constriction.
However, whoever applies constriction using bandages or by applying burning substances, does not achieve success quickly.
But if this happens to someone and the physician is not present, nor any medicine, then, using the guiding finger on the opening of that vein as we described, apply great pressure until the blood is stopped from its source, and do not remove the finger from the artery; and over the artery, sprinkle with extremely cold water until the blood is congealed and thickened; and if there is a need for cauterization or medication, use that method.
Of incisions, perforations, cupping, phlebotomy, sores, and similar conditions.
We have already discussed in the first chapter every illness in which cauterization with fire, or with burning medicines, is appropriate, along with the occasions and causes for it, and the necessary instruments and forms of cauteries; and I have ordered the chapters from the supremacy of the head to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that the seeker of this knowledge will be assisted regarding whatever they have inquired about.
And before I begin this, children, it is necessary to understand that this chapter is about the terror that is in the first chapter of cauterization.
And for this reason, great caution is required, for in the procedures described in this chapter, very often there is a discharge of blood, from which life depends, whether by incision of a vein, or incision upon an abscess, or opening of a fistula, or curing of a plague, or extraction of an arrow, or incision of a stone, and similar procedures. And all of those are accompanied by fear and trembling, and in their effects lies death.
And I warn you that this should not fall upon you, in which there is hesitation, for sometimes the ways of men with their many diseases come upon you. And some of them are angered by their illness, and death is near for them, both because of the severity of their illness and the length of their suffering, and with the illness comes fear, which signifies death.
And those are the ones who give you their possessions and say, "You are hoping for health," and their illness is harmful.
Therefore, you should not consent to those who come to you in this manner; and your caution should be greater than your greed and your rashness; and do not presume any cause for it without certain knowledge that verifies it for you, and for which a praiseworthy outcome will follow.
Use prognostication and indication of the path to health in the treatment of all your patients, for in that lies aid for you in gaining fame and memory, praise and glory.
Oh, children, may God inspire your understanding and not withhold direction and protection from you, for that is in His hand.
And I have ordered this chapter, distinguishing it by sections, as preceded in the chapter on cauteries, from the treatment of the head to the feet, so that what you seek and need concerning this may be made easier for you.
First Chapter.
Of the treatment of water contained in the heads of children.
Know that this illness often occurs in children after birth and when the wet-nurse squeezes the child's head without gentleness.
And sometimes it arises from a hidden cause that is unknown.
However, I have seen this illness only in children; and all those whom I have seen died from it, and thus it seems to me a hopeless case in those.
And I have seen a child whose head was filled with water, and his head was always so swollen that he could not support himself due to the size of his head, and that the moisture grew until he died.
And this moisture is either collected between the spinal column and the bone, or collected below the bone on the spine.
When the operation in that case, if the moisture is between the spinal column and the bone and the abscess is small, then you should make an incision in the middle of the head, with a slit along its width, and the length of the slit should be about two inches, until the moisture flows out.  This is the method for a spatum.
And therefore, if the moisture is more extensive and the abscess larger, then there are two incisions in this manner:
And if the moisture is beneath the bone, a sign of that is that you see the seams of the head opened on all sides and the water is squeezed out when you press it with your hand in different spots, and this is not hidden from you. You should make an incision in the middle of the head with three slits in this manner:
And then extract all the moisture from the incision; then compress the slits with cloths and lint; then cover it with wine and oil until the fifth day, and then loosen the bandage and treat the wound with dressings and ointments, and bandage the head evenly; and let the patient eat dry foods with little moisture until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of incision, which considers where the size of the abscess and the collection of water appear, for sometimes it is more in the occipital area, or in the front, or on the right, or on the left; and therefore, make the incision where the abscess and the accumulation of water appear, and do it in the way that seems best to you.
And be careful not to cut the artery, lest blood flow and the patient die from that blood loss with the evacuation of moisture.
Second Chapter.
When there is a persistent cold, or inflammation in the eyes or foot, and previous treatments with medicines have not worked, then the final cure in this case is the incision of those two arteries.
And therefore, when you wish to incise those [arteries] that the sick person's ailment has affected their head, first rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries appear, and then secure the sick person's neck with a cloth; and then consider where the vein pulses; and [make] incisions on the areas that are near the ears of the affected person, as they are hidden in some men.
And then, probe the artery with a probe, and then incise each one with a sharp instrument (or scalpel) in each area, cutting right through to the bone, and this should be done according to the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the instrument under the artery and cut it through at the points above.
And the length of the incision should be approximately two fingers wide, for when any vein is incised, the blood flows out with a pulsating exit, which leaps out in frequent spurts.
But if the artery does not appear on the surface, then you must measure from the ear the quantity of three fingers' width, and then probe with a probe, and penetrate to the bone.
And the amount of blood that needs to be drawn is approximately one ounce, though it may be more or less, and all this is according to the apparent strength of the patient and the fullness of the artery.
And then consider the wound, and if any cause of suppuration remains on the bone, then separate that, so that a painful abscess does not develop later.
And then bind the wound with strips and bandages of linen, and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Of the extraction of the arteries that are in the temples.
When someone experiences a recurring headache or sudden pain, due to acrid humors and a condition in the temples, or a severe, long-lasting headache resembling those, and is treated with the standard methods of medicine but finds no relief, then in these maladies we have proven effective the extraction of the arteries in the temples, or the cauterization of those arteries, as we have demonstrated.
And the method of the operation and extraction of those arteries is that the patient shaves the hair from the temples; and locate the artery visible in the temple because it is shown to you by its pulsation, and is hidden from but a few men due to the severity of the cold.
And if it is hidden from you, then the patient's neck should be tightened with a cloth; and then rub the area with a rough cloth or apply hot water until the artery becomes clearly visible. And then you take the instrument, of which this is the shape: And then you gently scrape the temple with that instrument until you reach the artery; and then, you gently push on it, and extract that artery from its surrounding tissue until you free it from the membranes beneath, from all sides.
But if the artery is delicate, then wrap it with the tip of the instrument, and then carefully cut off a part in accordance with the amount that its ends need to be shortened or brought together, and no blood will flow, because if not cut, the blood will not fully recede.
And then drain the blood from one to three ounces.
And if the artery is large, then you must tie it in two places with strong double flax thread or long cord, and do not cut it before the wound heals, lest blood flow.
And then cut away the excess tissue that lies between the two ligaments; and do this at this time or at another time.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery iron, or with a scalpel cautery, until the edges are cut, and it will remain in the same state as before.
And it is a better course of action if the patient does not have a fever or is not of a hot temperament; for cautery is among the things that aid in disposing of moist humors, and thus is better in youth.
And it is necessary, after the removal of the arteries, to fill the area with old cotton and place powders on it, and then to heal it with drying medicines that promote flesh growth, and with compresses, until it is healed.
And if, while working, a flow of blood from the artery occurs again, then you should attempt to stop the blood, either with cautery, or by filling the area with cotton and pressing your hand firmly until the bleeding stops. And if none of those things are available to you, then place your finger on the area until the blood has clotted, and apply water of intense coldness until the sharpness subsides, and it is firmly constricted as is needed.
And the easiest way to remove an artery is to cauterize the vein with this cautery, having two blades, after you have marked the two locations with a probe, and placing each, very hot, until they reach the bone and sever the vein in two places, so that the area between the extremities is lengthened, as it is not entirely consolidated with this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And the sharpness of the two blades be equal. However, if the sharpness is less than that of two blades, it is better, for if it is sharp like two blades, it approaches each part gently and does not cut the flesh quickly.
Therefore, since there is some thickness in those areas, the heat of the fire is held in them, and the flesh is cut/separated slightly.
And this method is better than any other method and more gentle.
And let the space between the two blades be the thickness of the finger.
Fourth Chapter.
Of the treatment of bodies afflicted with acute and acrid humors of the oils.
When the humors are acrid, and the vessels in the area above the head's forehead are affected, and you find no medical cause for treatment, and sometimes the patient's face begins to redden and feels like ants are crawling on their forehead, and the oils of the area are weakened, moist, so much so that their skin is visibly thin, and the eyelids are irritated by the acridity of the tears, the ancient masters in the treatment of such conditions, used a specific treatment, and called that treatment 'spassa'.
And you command the patient to shave the hairs on their forehead, and then make three evenly spaced incisions on the forehead along its length. And if the incision is two fingers long, the first incision should be the same distance from the nose as it is to the middle of the forehead, and the second should be slightly further along the line of the tear ducts, and the third the same distance from the other side.
And be careful not to injure the arteries that run along both sides, and keep your hand away from the continuation of both jaws. And if the space between each incision is the length of three fingers.
And if you have a piece of prepared sponge or very dry cloths with which to absorb the blood.
And then insert the spatula with two sharp ends, of which this is the form of the incision: that is from the temple area to the second incision. Scrape away with that all the flesh that is between the two incisions, along with the periosteum (the membrane covering the bone), and then do this from the middle incision to the second incision. And then remove this spatula and insert into the first incision again another instrument called a scalpel, sharp on one side and blunt on the other.
And place the sharp edge of that along the flesh adjacent to the flesh, and the blunt edge against the bone, and push it until it reaches the middle fissure, and cut with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the feet.
But continue the incision along this portion of the flesh, and then do this in the middle fissure until the other fissure.
And when a large quantity of blood flows, squeeze the places of the congealed blood clots.
And then place a piece of old cloth in each fissure, and place on those powders mixed with wine, oil, and vinegar, and make sure that a hot abscess does not develop.
And on the third day, loosen the ligament and administer a large infusion of warm or lukewarm water, and then treat it with basil ointment mixed with rose oil and other ingredients until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
Concerning the treatment of sores (or ulcers) and descending wounds from parts within the head.
When a patient has sharp and persistent sores, and the wounds on the affected area are small and weakened, and the care for them is failing, and the eyelids are ulcerated and the surrounding skin is affected, and in the forehead there is a severe aching pain and consequent exhaustion, then understand from these symptoms that the matter and sores come from numerous internal veins.
And the best treatment in these cases is this:  tell the patient to lie down, and then make a small incision in the middle of their forehead, extending from the left temple to the right temple, until the bone is exposed.  (Begin the incision at the temple, working toward the other, and be careful not to damage the muscles involved in chewing).
Dry all the blood with a sponge or a dry cloth, then fill the gap between the edges of the incision with old cloth or linen bandages. And then apply a powder mixed with wine and oil over the top to prevent a painful abscess from forming.
And when you see that the abscess has begun to subside, then you should cleanse the bone until new flesh begins to grow, and then treat it with a drying regimen that promotes flesh growth.  For example, take two parts wheat flour and four parts colophony, mix them, and make a plaster from this mixture.  Apply this plaster to these wounds to aid in flesh regeneration.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the treatment of what affects the ears.
Every ailment affecting the ears falls into one of four categories: either a mineral, or something like a mineral, such as iron or glass; or from a plant, such as a thorn or a bean; or from a liquid substance, like water, sour wine, or similar things; or an animal.
Therefore, if a stone or a similar substance that does not grow or adhere to the ear, is placed in the sunlight, and the stone is loosened, then apply some kind of violet or cedar oil to the ear.  Then, extract it, and make the person sneeze with a remedy, and then close their nostrils after the sneezing has occurred. Then place some pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and stretch the ear in different directions, for often it is expelled by these methods.
And if it is not expelled, then administer extraction with a tool called a *gesti epicicarios* (a subtle, small surgical instrument), of which this is the form:
And if it is expelled with the instrument, good.  Otherwise, administer its extraction with a subtle, thin, small spoon.
And if it is still not expelled by that, then make a brass cannula and insert the end of the cannula into the hole in the ear, and seal around the cannula with softened wax and oil so that only the cannula is open. And then extract with a strong pulling motion, for often this works as we have described.
But if it is not expelled in this manner, then take a heated cauldrons or a suitable device with which birds are caught, a small amount, and place it on the end of the wound, and then wrap it properly over that placed cloth. And then insert that into the ear's hole with ease, and then dry the ear of moisture.
And therefore, if it is not expelled by all the methods we have described, then you should consider cutting it out before it becomes a painful abscess or spasm.
And the way to cut it is that you first bleed the patient from the cephalic vein and extract blood according to its virtue, and then make that incision between your hands and turn the ear towards the upper parts, and make a small incision at the base of the ear towards its fat, in the location firmly pressed by you. Let the incision be crescent-shaped until it reaches the stone, and once the stone is found, remove it if you can with appropriate instruments; then close the incision at that time quickly, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the cause of the affliction in the ear is one of the fats that grows and swells, then administer its cure and extraction; but if you do not obey this prescription, then you should take a subtle spatum in this way: And administer with that the excision of that fat which has fallen into the ear.
And do not do this when you think that it has already been softened by the ear's moisture, and break it into small pieces, and then extract them with tweezers or by skillful manipulation, or with an incision as we have said, for this will ease its extraction.
But if water has entered the ear, the patient should use sneezing with caution at first, and fill their ear with some old cloth, and lie on their side with the affected ear down. If the water is thereby expelled, well and good; but if not, then take smooth, round stones the length of one's finger, and warm them slightly in the fire. The patient should insert one into the ear canal, and place a foot on that side, and strike the stone with another stone on the outside of the ear. So do not stop with the stone until all the water is expelled.
So do not stop with the stone until all the water is expelled. And when the water is expelled in this way, take a piece of paper or linen, put one end into the ear, and hold the other end in a fire until most of it is burned.
And put in more linen, and do this many times until all the water is expelled, and extract it with a tube, as described above concerning the stone used for extraction.
However, if a small creature, such as an insect or similar, has entered the ear, treat it as described in the section on the various kinds of ear maladies; but if it is a large creature causing an obvious obstruction, then remove it with forceps and hooks, and its removal is easier than any other thing that might be lodged in the ear.
But in the removal of a worm that has grown in the ear, when you treat it with what we have said in the section on ear problems and in the treatise on distillations, and your treatment does not work, then you should expose the ear to sunlight.
And if any worm-like object is visible, then remove it with delicate forceps or hooks; but if no object is visible, then take a narrow-mouthed, wide-bellied tube, of which this is the shape: And insert the narrow end into the ear according to the amount the patient can tolerate; then, forcefully suck with it, repeating this many times, until all the worms have been removed.
But if it does not yield to this treatment, then seal the area around the tube with wax, as we have described; then administer distillations, which the ancients have already proven effective in treating worms, and you will find this in the treatise on distillations.
And let your application be one in which you pour medicinal oils into the ear using this instrument, of which this is the form: Make it of silver or brass, narrow-ended, with a small hole and a flat top.
And thus it should push against whatever is in the cavity of the tube, continually, wherever necessary. And if you wish, take the reed and wrap its end properly with wool, then pour oil into the concavity of the instrument, or the juice of any herb, or whatever medicine you want into the tube—and that is in the ear—and then insert the reed with wool on top, and press it with your hand gently until the oil is expelled into the cavity of the instrument, and the patient feels the oil inside.
And whatever is put into the ear should be heated a little with fire. And be careful that the cause which you distill into the ear is not too cold, for ear medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
On the treatment of ear obstructions.
Sometimes children inherit this from their mothers' wombs, and it happens in some men too; obstruction in the ear canal due to a plague or flesh growing there; and this obstruction is sometimes in the membrane of the ear canal which does not allow hearing, and sometimes it is in the outer parts.
And that which occurs in the membrane of the ear, preventing hearing, is in the most difficult situations to heal.
And in that which allows hearing, you should place the patient's ear in the sun and observe it. And therefore, if obstruction appears, open it with a subtle instrument, of which this is the form:
If its tip, where the width is small, has one sharp edge and the other not; and the instrument have two pointed ends so as not to injure the ear.
And therefore, if the obstruction is due to flesh grown in the ear or in its passage, then remove it with delicate hooks, and cut away the flesh forcefully until all the flesh is removed.
And if the obstruction is in the front of the ear, then take a fine, pointed reed and heat it slightly with fire, and then place it in the ear canal.
And therefore, if you have detected obstruction causing the patient difficulty in hearing, gently relieve the pressure; and be careful not to harm the nerves of the ear.
And then place a cloth over the ear, fitting the size of the opening you have previously applied Egyptian ointment to; and do this for a few days until you are sure of the wound's healing, or that you take some and grind it with water and sprinkle it on the affected area, using it as a poultice; and if the ear appears to be already festering, then you must remove the poultice and replace it with another poultice wrapped in a cerate made with rose oil until the festering subsides; and then treat it until it heals.
However, if bleeding occurs, then soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear, and use other treatments that constrict the flow of blood, as described in the treatise on bleeding.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the treatment of a swelling that forms on the eyelids.
Swellings that appear on the eyelids of the eyes sometimes are moist, sometimes are dry.
Therefore, you should hold them with forceps or tweezers, and cut them away from their roots with a knife, then apply a poultice to the affected areas.
But if pain with blood flow occurs, then you cauterize them with a fine, lens-shaped cautery.
And the best cauterization is done near the incisions, and the swellings often return if any cause remains from their roots; but when cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that comes to the eyelids of the eyes.
Often, a cause resembling a swelling in its strength and hardness is found in the eyelids of the eyes, and for that reason it is called a swelling.
And it is a collection of thick moisture on the eyelid above and below.
Therefore, the operation for them is to consider whether the swelling arises from something visible on the eyelid and is spreading in every direction; and the cause of it is slight.
Therefore, make a slit over that along its width, and then cut it away from all sides easily until it gives way, and then suspend it with forceps and excise it.
And if the swelling is large, then you should close it with sutures; and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, then you will suffer no harm from it, because the ointment will restore it and make it flesh again.
And if the swelling is extending inwards from the eyelid's surface, then raise the eyelid, suspend the swelling with forceps, aside from any incision you might make, and press on it from all sides.  If the eyelid is opened by incision, this is of no consequence.
And after the incision, wash the wound with salt water; and treat the area with something that promotes healing until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Of the treatment of a boil on the eyelid above the eye.
A boil is a painful swelling that develops in the tissues of the eyelid above the eye, and it is more common in children, irritating the eyes of those affected and hindering their vision in the morning; and they cannot bear the light of the sun because of this, as tears accumulate in their eyes.
And because of this, you see them lying on their faces all the time, and they sleep on their faces; and the eyelids below their eyebrows are damp, having already been covered by swelling, and the presence of a boil is apparent in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the boil in them.
And a method of treatment is that the patient rests their head on your lap, and you put a cloth on them and over that, make a circle, according to the size required to contain the boil on all sides, and then place this on top of it, and press it with your fingers, so that the boil is enclosed within the circle.  Then make a small incision in the center of this moistness, with a slit along the width, and the slit should not be larger than the one made in a lancing procedure.
However, in the process, you must cut through the skin completely until you reach the boil, for in most cases the boil spreads through a fissure, resembling a white pus.
Then extract it with cloths which you have wrapped between your fingers around the outside parts, and rotate it between your right and left hand until it is gone.
And be careful not to press too hard on the slit, so that with the incision you do not pierce the boil and damage it.
However, if the boil does not appear at the beginning of the incision, but you gently press on the fissure until the boil appears; then extract it as we have said; and then submerge cloths in warm water and vinegar and place them on the area and compress them with powders.
And some people sprinkle salt and place it in the cavity of the fissure to absorb any remaining moisture.
And then treat it until it is healed.
However, if there is a hot abscess in the area, then treat it with cooling plasters until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
Methods of treating the eye.
When in the eyelids of the eyes there are growths underneath the natural skin and they become inflamed, this affects the eye and causes various ailments such as constant tears, softening of the eyelids and tissue, eventually leading to the destruction of the eye, which is called an eye ailment (or tactus of the eye).  It is treated in four ways: either by cautery with fire; or with a sharp medicine, as previously described in the chapter on cautery; or it is treated with incisions and sutures; or with probes, as I will explain.
And therefore you should place the patient's head on your lap; then gently hold the eyelid with your left hand, and if it is turned outwards, it is good; and if not, insert a needle into the part where the flesh is closest to the eyelid underneath,  and push the needle through with a thread above, right beside the edge of the eyelid where the eyelashes grow — close to the eyelashes —  and pull the thread outward with the eyelid, and turn the eyelid with the tool.
Then, using a sharp instrument (spatum) cut through the inflamed area within the eyelid, underneath the skin, from the larger lacrimal duct to the smaller one; and then remove the thread and place small cotton or cloth powders under the eyelid. Then, bandage the eyelid in the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, the bandage shape should correspond to the extent of the eyelid's swelling, as this varies from person to person. Some individuals require a larger portion of the eyelid to be incised depending on the degree of softening.
Then, using the spatula, make incisions along the two lines you have marked, starting from the larger lacrimal duct and extending to the smaller one. And if the incision is close to the natural eyelashes, just as wide as the king's finger.
Then, place a ligature at one of the two corners of the wound, and then scrape the entire area.  Then, using thread, or fine wool, bring the two lips of the wound together.  Clean away the blood.  If you wish, use other substances to help the extra threads adhere to the two eyebrows; otherwise, don't worry about it.
And leave the stitches of thread in place for three or four days, then treat the wound.
And if you wish, leave the wound without stitches, and treat it with something that dries and constricts, because the eyelid is lifted by the sealing of the wound and its adhesion; however, stitching is better.  Thus, the ancients said this.
But nonetheless, be solicitous towards the patient, and this is good practice;  there should be no fear in you.
And there is another method in treating this, which is to shape a figure on the eyelid like a myrtle leaf, and then raise the eyelid with three separate and joined hooks in this way:
But if this is not possible, holding the hooks, and this is not convenient, then take a needle with thread and place it in the middle of the figure, and insert the other thread near the larger tear duct, and the second thread near the smaller tear duct, and adjust the threads equally between your fingers, and then with those raised fingers, lift the whole eyelid, which is marked as we described.
And then close the two edges of the wound with stitches, and treat it until it is healed.
However, if an abscess forms near your incision or fissure, treat that abscess with plasters and similar remedies, and with ointments.
And sometimes, the lashes of the eyelid may need to be cut or removed, therefore you should use what we have discussed about incisions in that case, but stitching in each case is more skillful/artificial/refined.
To treat with canes is according to this method: and it is that you turn the eyelid and cut the figure within as I have recounted; and then, skillfully, make two thin canes or sticks, whose length should be according to the length of the eyelid and their width smaller than the width of the space. And you have already incised at the ends of each part, where you have the threads.
Then carefully attach each one to the excess eyelid tissue, and tightly bind the canes at each section. Leave it for a few days, for the flesh will die and wither because of the tightness and rot until it falls off on its own.
But if it has lingered, then break it with forceps, and then treat the wound until it is healed.
Thus, when it is stitched, the eyelid is raised and the skin doesn't protrude.
And this is the method for two canes which you will make according to this form: and have a length and width neither more nor less.
Chapter 12.
Of raising protruding hairs in the wound with a needle when the skin is hard.
When there are unnatural, hard, rough, and protruding hairs in the wounds, then the procedure for them is that you take a fine needle and place it on a fine silken thread, and then carefully adjust the ends of it so that each one is held very subtly. And the length of the threads should be about the length of a palm.
And then you should add another fine thread underneath it quickly, and use the ends of that one if you want.
And then place the patient's head on your lap, and be close to the light so that your work is clear, for it is a subtle and delicate operation.
Then, carefully insert the needle into the root of the adhering hair, which requires skill, and quickly push it through the eyelid until you pull the hair out from above the natural hair.
Then pull the hair out completely and place it in the container, and there should be one, two, or three hairs, and no more.
Then, pull your hand with that until the hair is completely removed from the eyelid with the natural hair.
However, if the container is pulled but the hair is not removed, you will pull the container in different directions with the liquid you have prepared, until the container is removed from the hole completely.
And you will observe this with the eye's sight, for then you will remove the container and the liquid, and each person will cleanse and close the eye, and leave it closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and healthy flesh grows over it.
And if the hair is shorter, then you should attach a longer hair from the natural ones, and lift with that, or you can leave it until it is lengthened by a small amount, and then lift it.
Chapter 13.
Of the treatment of a wound that occurs on the upper eyelid.
When a wound occurs in the eye, and there is a tightening of the eyelid, whether on the upper, right, or left side, due to poor healing from a wound, incision, or cauterization, and it is called "leporine," this wound, or ulcer, is either natural or accidental, and is caused by poor healing from a wound, incision, or cauterization, or similar ill-treated injuries.
The method of treatment in this case is to cut through the affected area of scar tissue, separate its edges, and place a piece of linen cloth between them, and bind them together until healed.
And you should not use treatments that dry or constrict, for if you do this, the wound will return worse than it ever was before.
Use remedies that soften, such as fenugreek, and apply a poultice of water in which marshmallow and linseed are soaked, or a diachylon ointment, which has been liquefied with some oil.  Dip the cloth into the mixture, and treat with that.
And the key to your treatment is that you should perforate any part that is not healed to the original form.
And this operation, which we have described, is nothing other than a correction of the wound that does not restore the area to its original form.
Chapter 14.
Of the treatment of a wound that is located on the lower eyelid.
This wound on the lower eyelid, properly called a wound, may be either natural or accidental.
However, an accidental wound is caused by a blow, a cut, cautery, or similar causes.
And the method of operating on it is that you take a needle with double thread, and insert it into the flesh, and make it pass from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread reaches the two extremities of the flesh.
And then gently pull the flesh apart with the needle, and stitch it together with the needle, and cut away excess flesh with a broad scalpel. And then, if the eyelid returns to its proper form, it is good; and if not, take the thread and place it in the slit, and with that close the eyelid, and close the two slits at the edge of the lower eyelid.
And let the extremities of the two slits of the incision be two angles that meet, and let their joining form an angle so that when they meet, they form a figure from their meeting.
And this figure is the Greek letter: Lambda.
And then, remove the flesh to the extent that the edge of the wound beneath the eyelid is sharp.
And then, bring together the separated parts and stitch them with a wool thread; and then treat them with the softening medicines and poultices mentioned previously until healed.
And if, instead, a cut or stitch or cautery is required, then you should make a flat incision on the eyelid skin, as described earlier. And then separate with a poultice between the two lips, as previously described.
In summary, the treatment for a protrusion, whether above or below, should follow the operation, according to the type of protrusion, which presents various forms.
And the skilled artisan always prepares instruments in every possible way until the figure returns to its natural form, or close to it, and the patient, according to what is appropriate for them, is prepared for the operation and the instruments in the best positions.
Chapter 15.
Of the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this adhesion occurs in such a way that the eyelid is firmly attached to the white of the eye, preventing its natural movement and function, then you should introduce the tip of a probe underneath the eyelid, and elevate or extend it with forceps.
And then you carefully cut the adhesion with a sharp but not excessively sharp instrument, to avoid unsettling the patient during the procedure, and then remove the instrument so that it does not injure the eye with its sharpness.
And your incision should cut through the adhesion easily until the eyelid returns to its natural shape and the entire adhesion is healed.
And then you apply salt water to the eye, with which you wash it; or, alternatively, a decoction of chamomile dissolved in water.
And then, gently separate the eyelid from the eye, and place wool soaked in egg white over the eye.  Then, on the third day, use healing remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Of incision of the nail and the protrusion of flesh from the tear ducts.
Know that a nail is of two types:  either nervous, and resembling a hard, fine chip; or non-nervous, resembling frozen white moisture, which when touched by an iron, or forcibly taken with forceps, is detached and the forceps do not become embedded in it.
And both of these types arise from the major tear duct, gradually growing to cover the eye and obstructing the light and movement of the eye.
But the method of operating on the incision is that the patient places their head on your lap, then open their eyes, and lift the eyelid with your hand, and then use forceps with little bending, and pull the nail away from the surrounding tissue. Then take a needle, thread it with a horse, ox, cow, or similar hair, or strong thread, gradually increase the tension on the needle, insert it into the nail, and pierce the nail with the needle, tie the nail with the thread, and pull the nail away from the surrounding tissue, scraping the nail bed with the hair, which is covering the pupil. And thus you pull it (the nail) with the thread to the back of it.
Then you cut the remaining part at the root of the tear duct with a thin scalpel or small forceps, and leave the natural tear duct tissue intact, so that the incision does not cause continuous tears.
And sometimes the nail, using only forceps or thread, as we have said, is then scraped with a lion's tooth scalpel, of which this is the form:
And be careful not to touch the cornea with the scalpel, lest it be damaged, and the pupil be pierced.
Then place a little powdered salt or a prepared astringent into the wound, and leave it there until the next day; and then treat it with whatever is necessary until it is healed.
And if the nail is not sensitive and you cannot insert a needle, or it is not embedded in the flesh with forceps, then frequently this type of nail is treated with medications, and is healed accordingly as I have recounted in the division.
And if you wish to incise it, then you open the patient's wound and take a sharp, fine lion's tooth scalpel, shaped like this: with subtlety. And if it is often thin, and inflamed, and spreads to the surrounding area, then apply a preparation from your pharmacy (or something made from a similar substance), or with red-hot cautery or with powdered salt, and cover the wound until the next day.
And then repeat the operation until all of that which is there from it is removed, unless a suppurating boil appears in the wound; in that case, leave it alone, and treat the boil initially until it heals.
And then repeat your operation on it with care until it is healed.
If the tear gland protrudes significantly into the wound, it should be suspended with a thread; cut a portion of it off, but do not completely sever it to prevent a flow of tears.
And then apply a red-hot cautery, or something similar, to the tear duct until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Concerning the incision of a boil and what is born from adhered flesh in the wound.
Sometimes, in some wounds, red flesh grows and overspreads the pupil, or it approaches or presses upon the eyelids, forcibly squeezing the eyelids together at the edges, resembling the rough surface of a balustrade.
However, the procedure for this requires the patient to rest their head on your knee; then, open the wounds and collect all that flesh with suitable forceps or tweezers, and hold it with a pair of forceps. Then, you should carefully cut through the flesh with the scalpel until it is fully finished, and be careful not to damage the wound during your work.
And your incision should be made with the same technique as when you incised the nail, or with small forceps, gathering the material as described in this section's procedures for this type of wound.
So, once your incision is complete and all the flesh has been removed, then fill the wound with coarse salt, or apply a red, corrosive-like powder, and place a bandage made of cloth and egg white over the wound to prevent further inflammation.
And if any pus remains and there's a risk of inflammation, leave the wound and treat that inflammation with something that will resolve it, and then repeat the operation until it is healed.  Similarly, do the same for any adhered flesh that occurs in a wound of this type.
However, you should avoid any treatment of the wound through natural or accidental means using iron, and do not put your hand to any of these operations until you see that the limb can withstand the operation.
Chapter 18.
Of the collection of pus from the wound.
Pus that has formed, red, hard lumps over the wound, and prevents the healing process, and delays the healing of the wound by days.
Therefore, it is necessary at the beginning that you consider if the wound in which there is pus is strong and there is no other illness besides pus, then you collect that pus.
And you command the patient to rest their head on your lap, and then to suspend those veins with an ounce or two, according to your judgment; and that ounce be of a fine, double-folded type in this manner:
or two ounces gradually in one piece in this manner:
And then carefully collect those veins with gentle force, and wipe the blood away bit by bit until you see those veins drawn away from the wound and curled up.
And be careful that you do not damage the wound with the edges of the spatula; and if you perform your operation in the middle of the day before the sun, and be very firm in your operation so that you only cut the veins.
And when you have finished, then you distill green or red vinegar into the wound so that with its sharpness it corrodes that which remains of the clot.
But if this is not possible for you to collect at that time, then you should plaster everything with whatever relieves his pain, and leave him for a few days until the pain subsides and you are certain of a warm abscess.
And then repeat this procedure according to the same method until he is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps:
Chapter 19.
Of the reduction of arizati (or foreign body) in the nose.
When you treat a foreign body with a cautery or a sharp burning medicine, according to the narrative of the case, and it is not cured, then there is no use in remaining there; instead, cut open the abscess, once it is mature, and remove all the moisture and pus until the bone is exposed.
And therefore, when the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then you scrape it with an instrument of which this is the form:
And it is called a sharp head, and it is made of hard iron, and the head is round, like a knob, which is made pointed by the piercing of a file or pumice stone through subtle piercing (as is in the following figure).
And place that upon the corrupted part of the bone, and then rotate it between your fingers, and gently press your hand little by little until you know that the corruption is scraped away.
And this is done several times, and then restore the area with drying and astringent substances.
Therefore, if the area is consolidated and good flesh has grown there, and the body is free from pus and remains for 40 days, and it is not renewed and not suppurated and no accident occurs there, then you know that it is healed.
And if not, then it is not a true healing, but reduce the fracture to the shape of the nose, and in this way:  you must expose the second bone with iron or sharp medicine, and once the bone is exposed, then you take a sliver (or strip) in this form:
Let the end of the iron be triangular, and the shaft be thin and tapered to the tip, like this.
Then place that (the iron/sliver) on the bone, and let it be near the lacrimal bone; and move your hand slightly from the hole, and then rotate your hand with the sliver until it penetrates the entire bone, and the patient will feel air escaping when he feels your hand.
Therefore, then restore the area with astringent, drying medicines, as you have been taught.
So, when the flesh grows and the area hardens, then the matter which had spread outward returns to the nose, and thus it is easier on the patient.
Chapter 20.
Of the reduction of the eminence of the hole/opening.
When the opening protrudes completely and you do not encounter any harmful or diminishing swelling, then you should release the patient, and then follow them, and then apply leeches to the neck without incision, and obtain good suction.  Then, apply a liniment made of acatra, aloe, incense, and sarcocolla to the opening, and then firmly bandage the poultice over the opening with many powders.
And the method for the powders is to take fine linen cloths, and each cloth should be folded in four; and make many of those.
And then place those one upon another according to the quantity the opening needs. And then bind them together with a strong, hard cloth; and repeat the leech application with suction without incision; and then remove the leeches and leave the opening constricted day and night.
But if meanwhile the swelling is softened, then it should be very tight.
And then remove the bandage day and night as we have said.
And if the opening has already been reduced, good; and if not, then repeat the poultice with the powders, and the constriction and the leeches until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Of the incision of the eminence of the uvea tunic.
When a rupture occurs in the uvea tunic, and it is prominent and protrudes beyond the eyelids like a cluster of grapes, and the shape of a man is deformed because of it, and you want to incise it, then the operation there is as we have recounted: you insert a needle into the root of the uvea from the lower to the upper part, and then insert another needle with a double thread from the lacrimal side, and push that in and withdraw the first needle according to its placement. Then, cut the place where the double thread is and remove the part of the uvea in the area around the needle, and firmly hold that area.
And then withdraw the needle and place over the opening wool soaked in egg white and let it remain until the thread with the grape-like protrusion comes away.
And then treat the wound with something that strengthens it until the ulcer is healed.
And sometimes, gather the area around with a cloth circle, to hold a large protruding wound, and if it has been perforated with a thin spatula that reaches the edge of the wound, and the albuminous moisture comes out, the wound will descend and close quickly.
And then hold it firmly until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
Of the treatment of  falconers (or, perhaps more literally, "birds of prey").
This ailment, called "alconati," is nothing but a suppuration that has collected in the wound, resembling descending water, but is not the same.
But the method of treatment is that you should have the patient sit on a prepared and straight-backed chair, and then take their head in your hands at two points and hold it thus until you see the pus coming to the lower parts, and then let it dry and not descend, and stop the light, and the patient should see the causes this way.
And if it does not descend further, know that it is water (or, perhaps, a watery substance).
But if the descent of the pus is not prepared with what we have said, then have the patient sit between your hands, and then take a fine, light-colored substance, and apply it over the panniculus, dabbing a little near the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the corona (into the hollow place), until the pus is expelled.
Therefore, when it is expelled, then you instill warm water into the wound, in which you have mixed honey or water in which you have boiled fenugreek and honey.
And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
Of the treatment of water descending from the eye.
We have already discussed the types of water in the division, and which ones are appropriate to pierce with a lance, declaring the condition, then according to the health of the patient.
Then you should place the patient between your hands on a bed, and tightly bind their eye socket.
And then place your left hand near the eyelid of their affected eye, if the eye with the water is the left eye; or with your right hand, if the eye is the right eye.
And then with your right hand take an almond, if the eye is the left one; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right one.
Then place the almond's tip near the brow, along the width of the ray (or beam) on the white part of the eye, closer to the smaller tear duct, and then gently press the almond, and rotate your hand with it.
And then penetrate into the white of the eye and feel that the almond has reached the empty space (or cavity).
And it is necessary that the distance of the almond's path to the point be the measure of the space between the pupil and the edge of the dark part, which is the brow of the eye; for you will see through that pupil with the eye's vision, by the clarity of the cornea.
And then place the almond into the area where the fluid is, and pull it down repeatedly, and if the fluid descends immediately, then you will see the patient's gaze turn towards it and the almond in their eye.
And then rest a little while; if the fluid has descended, then you rest it again before the almond is thrown out.
And then, when it is dried and does not descend, then you extract the almond with ease, and gently move your hand around it, and then dissolve a little clear rock salt in the fluid and wash the inside of the eye with it; and then apply a cloth or stopper or wool infused in warmed oil and egg white, and bind the eye with this.
But we and those of our time use a bruised (or crushed) compress with egg white on this.
And if you cannot get your almond into the eye's interior due to its hardness—for in some people, the eye is very hard—then it is necessary to take the preparation called "alberid," of which this is the form:
And therefore, pierce the conjunctiva with this, and do not press your hand into the hole, but merely make a subtle passageway for the almond.
And then introduce the almond, as we have said.
And to complete your work, prepare the patient's sleeping quarters properly, where they sleep on their back in a dark room, and prohibit all movement.
And place this on their body so that their nature may be soothed, and do not allow their head to turn to either the right or left at all.
And let the ligament remain according to its disposition until the third day.
And then, remove that from the dark room, and shield the patient's vision, and show them some things, and then return the bandage by the seventh day.
And you should not do this during the time of the treatments or immediately after the infusion. Having done this, you should remove it because the water can easily cause great and vehement damage from watching (or observing).
But if an acute abscess occurs, then you should remove the dressing before the seventh day, and treat it with something that will resolve the abscess.
Then you blindfold the patient and place something over their face through which their gaze is controlled from below for several days, while the patient remains in the darkened room.
And then gradually they are released from that room and guided back to their normal activities.
And know that in a serious case, the student is not excused from watching several times, and then you will be skilled in the procedure.
And it has come to our knowledge from some of those who are at Artach, and some said that a fire was made in the Ayrac, a large hole having been pierced through which the water was drawn out.
