Chapter 24.
Of the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.
When various types of flesh grow in the nose, from some cause resembling a very heavy scorpion, or a large polyp; and this disease is called polyps because it resembles that depicted fish. And from it is cancerous flesh, ulcerous, of dark color; and from it is that which is lion flesh, not of dark color.
Therefore, in those flesh growths that are not fraudulent or cancerous, you should have the patient sit between your hands and expose them to the sun, and dry their nostrils, and prick the growths with your lancet. And then, remove it to the outside, and then cut what you have grasped from those growths with a sharp, thin spathe from one side until you know that all the flesh has been removed; because if any part from which it is possible to excise remains, then you should cut it with one of the thin instruments easily until there remains nothing of it. But if it is inflamed with blood or a hot abscess, then you apply what is appropriate; or if it is a malignant abscess, then you cauterize and burn it until the blood is removed and all the cavities are gone.
And then, put a mixture of vinegar and water or wine in the nose near the incision. But if the nose is open and moisture proceeds from it to the palate, then you know that it is already healed.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you know that inside there is flesh or excess pus on the bones of the nostrils to which the excision has not reached.
Therefore, then it is necessary to insert a piece of lint (or similar material) of some thickness, and put many small pieces, placing the amount of a finger or less between each piece, and placing the patient in a position where one end of the lint can be inserted into the nostrils with a probe or other suitable instrument.
And after you have done that, cause him to draw his breath in until it reaches the nasal passages, and it emerges over his throat, for many times children in school demonstrate a similar action, and it is an easy thing for one who wishes to do it.
And then, place the two ends of the leech, one end extending over the nose and the other remaining inside the nose, and then administer a piece of meat with the nuts contained within the leech, and do this until you know that the meat is now inside the leech's nuts.
And then, remove the leech, and place a cautery near the extraction of blood in the nose, and do this for three days or more until the unguent has completely dissolved all remaining flesh.
And finally, place a lead cannula in the nose for several days until it is healed.
But if you require a more complete cure, use this one.
And this is the form of the instrument for nasal purging, with which oils and medicines are distilled into the nose:
And make this of silver or brass, which should resemble a small, open lamp, like a small shell, and similarly shaped at the opening.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula like a tube, and the cannula of the purging instrument should be flat, uncovered; at the rear of which there should be a handle, with which you hold it when heating the oil or water you wish, or juices and subtle substances.
Chapter 25.
Of the oils applied to the nostrils.
Many times oil is applied to the nostrils, and it is magnified and increased over several days until the condition is greatly improved.
And therefore, you must address it at the beginning of its appearance, and eradicate the entire thing. And then place a cautery or a burning medicine on the affected area.
However, if its treatment is delayed until it has become aggravated, then consider if it is hardened, tough, dark-colored, and insensitive.  If so, do not touch it with iron, for it is a deep-seated abscess; for often, the one who cuts into this abscess risks inflicting great harm upon the afflicted person.
And if the abscess is soft to the touch and not dark-colored, and incision is possible throughout it, then drain the affected area and make the incision without fear, and treat the area with something that dries and tightens until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Of the suturing of the nose, lips, and ear when their continuity is broken by plague or similar illnesses.
Know that when you encounter a break in continuity in one of these cartilages, you accomplish little in that operation except for a few skilled individuals.
Therefore, when you encounter such a condition, consider whether it's fresh plague with its own blood.  If so, you should join the two sides of the wound with stitches and then treat it until it heals.
And if the edges of the broken skin are already separated, and each is healthy, then it is necessary for you to scrape away each portion of their visible scabs until blood comes out. Then join the two edges with stitches, tightening them, and sprinkle dragon's blood and crushed asafoetida over them.  Place a piece of diaphanous plaster, or another of the consolidating plasters, over the powder, and leave it bound for two or three days.  Then remove it and change the plaster, leaving it until the pus is completely gone, and then treat with ointment until healed.
But the method of stitching is that you should either join the broken skin with needles as we have described in the suturing of the belly, or you should join it with glue, as we have explained to you.
Chapter 27.
Of the extraction of those things that appear between the two lips in the interior parts.
Often, in the inner parts of the lips of men, some hard abscesses appear, resembling the grains of millet, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore, you must turn the lips over and cut away each of those hardnesses from all sides. Then, with sharp instruments, you should fill the area with pressed, dried sawdust until the bleeding is stopped. Then, the area should be washed with sharp wine and treated with something astringent until it is healed.
Chapter 28.
Of the incision of the flesh adhered to the gums.
Often, flesh adhered to the gums, which the ancients called "albulus," is present.  Then you should detach it with a forceps or scissors, and cut it away from its roots, allowing the pus or blood to drain.
And then apply pressed, dried sawdust, or other astringent or drying powders to the area.
And if that flesh returns, then cut it away again, and cauterize it, for the cautery will now prevent its return.
Chapter 29.
Concerning the extraction of teeth with an iron instrument.
When on the surface of the teeth, both on the inner and outer parts, and between the gums, rough, hard, and sometimes black or yellowish crusts form, and the gums become inflamed by this, and the teeth become loose.
Therefore, you should position the patient between your hands, placing their head on your lap, and carefully extract those teeth on which such crusts and similar, gritty substances appear, until no trace of them remains, thus removing the blackness, yellowness, and other impurities.
If you have removed what was present at the beginning of the procedure, then good; if not, then repeat the procedure on those teeth the next day, and the following day, and a third time, until they reach the desired state.
And know that teeth require many tools of various shapes and forms, depending on what is needed for your procedure. And therefore, the file used to file the tooth from the inside is different from the file used to file between the teeth, in other shapes, and these files should be prepared by you, which you will see shortly.
Chapter 30.
Of the removal of teeth.
You must treat the tooth's pain with all diligence and take your time in any tooth extraction, because there is no remedy that surpasses the time spent on it when it is extracted, because it is a subtle substance until the extraction is completed.
However, when the patient requires that tooth to be extracted, you must be firmly certain which tooth is the painful one, for many times the pain deceives the patient and indicates that it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one, and the pain does not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is extracted, for we have already seen this through the operations of barbers.
Therefore, how must you be certain which tooth is painful, and that you know it well? Then you must scrape around the tooth with a substance having some virtue until it is detached from the gum on every side, and then gently remove it with your fingers, or with very fine forceps, little by little, until you have removed it completely.
And then, if it requires strong forceps for good grip or sturdy structure, place the patient's head between your knees so you hold it securely and then pull out the tooth straight so you do not break it, and do not cause further pain to the patient.
And therefore, if it is not easily removed, then take one of those instruments and place it under it from every side with ease and exert the removal just as you did at first.
But if the tooth is cracked or decayed, then you must cover the crack with cloth and compress it with a fine pointed instrument so that it does not break during the process of removing it with forceps.
And you must carefully scrape around the entire gum area, and keep a strong grip so that you do not break it and leave a piece, and return the patient to the harm that could be worse than the initial pain.
And beware that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their eagerness and boldness in tooth extraction, without administering what we have described; for often they cause serious illnesses in men that are more difficult to treat, leaving the roots all, or some of them, remaining, or even pulling out some jawbone with the tooth, as we have witnessed many times.
And after the extraction, the mouth should be rinsed with warm wine or vinegar with salt.
And if there is excessive bleeding, as often happens, then apply a bit of wool and sprinkle it over the wound, and if that doesn't stop it, cauterize it.
The shape of the forceps and delicate pliers used to begin removing the tooth should be long on the end, with a short, thick handle, so that they do not bend when using them.
And they should be like a thick handle, so that when you squeeze with them, they don't bend; have short ends.
And they should be made of Indian steel, with straight ends, and on those ends there should be teeth which interlock and grip tightly with strong constriction; and sometimes the ends of those have the shape of a stork's beak, that is, a hook, and are very constricting.
Chapter 31.
Of the removal of teeth and extraction of bones from fractured jaws.
When you must remove a broken tooth which remains in place, you should apply a cloth soaked in hot water for a day or two until the area is softened; then, grasp the tooth with forceps, whose tips are shaped like the beak of a stork (or a hook), the shape of a pair of forceps:
The tips of the forceps should be made with a file or emery.
And if you cannot extract the tooth with these forceps or pliers, then you must carefully dig around the root and expose all the flesh, and insert an instrument called a small lever, which is shaped like this:
And it should have a short, thick end, a little bit, so that it is not broken.
Therefore, if the root is thus pulled out, good; and if not, help yourself with these other instruments, of which these are the shapes: the shape of the first instrument is triangular-ended, of some thickness:
The shape of the second instrument is that it should be triangular and have a more slender tip.
And having already been helped with this instrument with two ramps, the shape of which is approximately as follows: and with other instruments, which I will describe in detail later; for assistance is given with this instrument, which resembles a large hook, and this is the form:
of a triangular, bent or short tip, which has some thickness, and which should not be too sharp so that it does not break.
And you should know that the instruments for the teeth are very similar to the other instruments.
However, a skilled and clever craftsman in the art will discover for himself, according to what the operation and the various illnesses indicate to him; for there are illnesses for which the ancients did not prescribe or create a specific instrument, due to the diverse forms of the conditions.
However, if the bone of the jaw or other bones in the mouth are fractured or diseased, then it should be treated in its place with whatever is appropriate from the instruments and forceps we have described for the extraction of tooth roots, and it will be helpful with a technique, whose form is this:
And it should be gradually reduced in size, and held or squeezed with the mouth so that it does not escape or loosen until the entire bone is fully repositioned; then restore the area with appropriate remedies for this.
However, if there is a small swelling on the bone, then remove that swelling and its blackness, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
Of the treatment of teeth protruding beyond the others.
When teeth protrude beyond their natural position, causing an uneven shape, especially in women, and particularly in young women, you must consider: if a tooth is close to another tooth, and separation or a ligature is not possible, then extract that tooth; but if it is not connected to another tooth, then you should cut it with this instrument, the shape of which is like a pig's snout, made of Indian iron with a very sharp tip.
And make your incision with the instrument, cutting into the tooth over several days due to its hardness, so that it does not damage other teeth.
And if the protruding tooth is exposed for filing, then file it with an Indian iron file of this shape:
And it should be entirely made of Indian iron, and easily tempered; and it should have many small points, just like the file used to make needles, with which the tooth can be filed little by little over many days with ease, so that no other tooth is damaged or broken. And then carefully line it up at the end, and cut it (the tooth) out using a few razors.
And if a part of the tooth is fractured and hurts the tongue, then you must file it down until the roughness of that fracture is removed and smoothed, and it is made whole so that the tongue is not injured and speech is not impaired.
Chapter 33.
Of restoring damaged teeth with gold or silver fillings.
When teeth are fractured or dislodged by impact or a blow, and the patient cannot properly bite down, and you have treated it with astringent medicines, and your treatment has not been successful, then, in order to prevent it from rotting, the gaps should be filled with gold, not silver, because silver turns green and may rot after a few days, but gold never changes.
And the filling should be of such a thinness and thickness according to the amount that can fit between the teeth; and the method of insertion is to take the filling and place double that amount between two healthy teeth, then gradually increase the amount with two curves of the filling between the damaged teeth, whether one or many, until the material reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. And then repeat the insertion until the starting point, and gently and skillfully compress it until it is no longer damaged.
And your grip, with which you compress the filling, must be on the roots of the teeth so that it does not come loose.
Then you trim the excess filling with forceps or nippers, and adjust them in each place with skill, and shape and hide them between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth, so as not to injure the tongue. And then leave it thus tightly fitted as it remains.
But if it is loose or broken, compress it or them with more filling in such a way at all times.
And this is the method for teeth and the method of repairing two healthy and two damaged teeth, thus when these are joined:
And when one or two teeth are returned to the fitting of each in their places, and they have grown back as we have recounted, and remain.
And only skilled and knowledgeable craftsmen can do this.
And sometimes a substitute is made from the bones of a cow, shaped like a tooth, and placed in the gap where the tooth is missing, and compressed as we said, and it remains and serves as a replacement for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Of the binding of the ligament which is in the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament, which sometimes lies under the tongue, is either natural, present from birth, or accidental and caused by a wound that has healed.
And the operation on it involves opening the patient's mouth, their head held securely by you; lifting their tongue, and then carefully cutting that nerve ligament along its width until the tongue is released from its confinement.
However, if there is any hardness or knotting there, resulting from the healing of a wound, then apply a cauterizing agent and cut along its width until you have severed the ligament and the knot has dissolved.
And be careful not to create a tear in the flesh and that the cut doesn't go too deep, causing excessive bleeding.
And then wash the cut with water or vinegar or cold water; and then place a linen pad under the tongue; this is a bandage the patient will wear under the tongue every night to prevent it from sticking again.
But if bleeding occurs, place powdered vitriol on the area; but if the bleeding persists, then cauterize the area with a blunt cautery suitable for this. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
Of the removal of a ranula (a cyst) under the tongue.
Sometimes, an abscess forms under the tongue, resembling a small tumor, hindering the tongue's natural function; and it grows significantly until it fills the mouth.
And the treatment for it is to open the patient's mouth, sitting facing the sun, and examine the abscess.
And if you see it [the abscess] of a dark color, or black, hard, to which the patient does not feel sensitivity, then do not touch that which is cancer.
And if it has turned to a whitish color, in which there is moisture, then you insert your forceps there, and make an incision with a thin scalpel, and cut it from every side; and if blood comes out during your operation, then you place over it crushed egg white, i.e. vitriol, until the bleeding is controlled.
And then return to your work until you have extracted it completely, until it is washed with vinegar and salt; and treat it with other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 36.
Of the extraction of abscesses in the two ears, and that which is on the neck, and other abscesses.
When an abscess develops on the neck, it's often glands that are swollen and inflamed.  These become external and are called 'the two ears'; if you treat them with what we've said in the discussion and they don't heal, then you must consider if the abscess is hard, dark-colored, and insensitive.  If so, do not touch it with an iron.
And if it is reddish and the root is thick, then do not touch it with iron for fear of blood flow. Instead, let it mature; then either you will lance it or it will break open on its own.
And if it is white, soft, and its root is thin, then it is suitable to be incised.
And the operation on it requires you to consider, before your operation, whether the abscess has already been softened by complete rest or if it has diminished with any reduction. Then, have the patient sit in the sun, with their head in your lap, and open their mouth. The attendant should grasp and press down the patient's tongue with a very fine instrument, which has this form:
And it should be made of silver or bronze, and be thin like a knife.
So, as you press the tongue with it, and the abscess is shown to you, and you have placed your gaze upon it, then take a lancet and place it on the abscess, and draw it outwards to the sides as much as you can. After you have drawn some of the tissues, then cut that with an instrument of this form:
After you have drawn some of the tissues, then cut that with an instrument of this form: and it resembles forceps, but its ends are curved, so that the opening of each is facing the other, very sharp, which should be made of tempered iron.
But if this instrument is not present, then cut it with a scalpel, of which this is the form: And it should be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you have incised one incision, cut the other following this same method of incision, and then the patient will recover from cold water.
However, if there is bleeding, treat with water decoction of pomegranate bark or myrtle leaves, or similar astringents, until the bleeding is reduced, and then treat it until healed.
And sometimes other abscesses arise in the throat or near the two ears, and incise them as I have described in the incision of two incisions above.
And I once treated a woman with an abscess that had formed in her throat, an abscess that was tending towards a dark color, with little sensation, which had already narrowed her throat, and the woman was breathing with a constricted airway, and had been forbidden from eating and drinking water, and she would have died if it had lasted just one or two more days.
And the abscess had grown so much that it had formed two channels before bursting through two openings.
Therefore, I treated it fistularly, and placed a probe into one of the openings, and pulled it out, and a large portion came out with it, and then I cut that from where it connected to the other opening, and then I opened her mouth, and held her tongue down, and then I placed the probe into that abscess.
And then I excised a large portion of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Therefore, the woman's throat was completely healed, and she immediately began drinking water, and then eating food.  Thus, I did not cease to cut into that abscess many times for a long time, and another abscess succeeded in its place, which I also cut into until it was resolved.
Therefore, I cauterized the throat of her, thus bringing relief; and then I wandered and did not know what God had done to her nearby me.
Chapter 37.
Of the incision of a boil when it is suppurated.
When a sore throat affects the uvula, and it is suppurated and long, then it is called perpendicular; and if it is in the lower part, thick and round, then it is called a boil.
However, when it is cured with what we have said in the division, and you do not confirm your cure, and sometimes a suppurating boil is already subdued and subtle, then you must incise that which is swollen and round; and if it is of that length, and is bloody, dark-colored or black, and is not sensitive to touch, then you must attempt its incision, for there is a fear for the patient in that.
And when you see it according to the way I have described, by its whiteness and length, you should have the patient sit down and press the tongue with an instrument that displays its shape and then place the lancet on the new boil, pull it downwards, and incise it with one of the two instruments that we call 'incising two boils'.
And you must not incise it except what is aligned with the natural boundary without addition, for if you incise further, you will worsen its condition.
And after the incision, use what we have described in the incision of two boils, and treat it until it is healed.
However, if the patient is frightened by the incision, you should use cauterization without fear, and the method of cauterization there is only with sharp water.  You should place the patient's head on your lap, then press their tongue with the instrument we mentioned, and then take the water, as described in the chapter on cauterization, and apply it with a burning stick.  Do not use a thick or thin layer, and fill the cavity of the instrument, which is shaped like [… (the description of the instrument's shape is missing)].
Let the extremity of it [the instrument] be concave, like the bowl of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine over the wound.  The patient should lie on their side so that saliva or mucus from the mouth flows away from the medicine, so that nothing drips down the throat and irritates it.
And then, hold your hand with the medicine, and press it on the uvula for half an hour, until you see it blackened and calmed by the medicine's action.
And if you wish, take a piece of cloth and wrap it around the end of the [medical] instrument, dip the cloth in the medicine, and insert the instrument with the cloth-covered end into a tube or canal until the cloth adheres to it.  Do this many times until you achieve the desired cauterization of the uvula. Then, leave it to dry and fall off in about three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it.  Then, after the cauterization, clean the area around the uvula with a cloth soaked in water or broth, removing any remaining medicine.  Wash with cold water and treat the outside with a soothing ointment, and internally with remedies until healed.
And this type of treatment is safer than incision and less prone to complications.
Sometimes the uvula is treated with a method more delicate than cauterization or incision, through fumigation in this way: take balm, hyssop, origanum, rue, Arabic cicuta, chamomile, rosemary, and similar herbs, and combine all of them, or some of them, in a pot, and immerse them in sour wine and bring to a boil. The pot should be properly sealed, and a hole in the center of the pot should have a concave brass instrument placed over it, be it made of silver or brass.
And the end [of the instrument] should be inserted into the patient's mouth until the vapor rises to the uvula through the tube, until the uvula is considerably softened.
And then repeat this many times until it [the uvula] is dry.  Be careful not to use this treatment at the beginning of an abscess, for it would only worsen the abscess;  you should not do this unless the abscess is already hot.
But if this instrument is not available to you, then take a reed and place a hard-boiled egg shell at its end, so that the patient's mouth is covered; for the egg shell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter 38.
Of the removal of thorns and of that which adheres to the throat, and of other similar matters.
Often thorns or fish bones or some other thing adhere to the throat.
Then you should remove what is clearly visible, focusing on it.  Press the tongue with an instrument in front of the light, so that what is in the throat is revealed to you.
And what is hidden in the throat, you must make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for a foreign object adhering to the throat will be expelled by vomiting; or have the patient swallow a piece of bread or lettuce root or a piece of dry bread; or let the patient take a piece of dry sea sponge, tightly wrapped in wool, and then the patient swallows this, for as it reaches the place where the thorn is, it will be expelled. And further, take an eel and drink it, wrapped in wool, and place it inside [the throat] and it will be drawn out; or take a leek and place it inside, and then draw it out.
But if it is not expelled by those methods we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument in this way:
And it should be slightly larger than a small finger, and at its end there should be a concavity that you can easily insert into the patient's throat.
And have the patient raise their head, and be careful not to touch their throat bone, so that it does not hurt, and with that [instrument] push on the bone or thorn, or the physician with their hand.
But the patient should, with their knowledge of the location of the cause, gently push downwards, and pull their hand with the instrument upwards, all according to the amount that is prepared until it is expelled.
Chapter 39.
Of the extraction of a bloodsucker attached to the throat.
When the leech is medicated with what we have said in the division of treatment with medicines and it doesn't work, then consider the area of the patient's throat in the sun, after you have pressed the patient's tongue with an instrument as we have described.
Therefore, if you see the leech, extract it with a small knife or with gentle, skillful movements.
And if it is not possible to remove it with that, then take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat.  Then place a heated iron in the concavity of the cannula, and do this repeatedly (until the leech is dead and detached from the throat).  Keep the patient away from water all day.
Then take a bowl filled with cold water, and open the patient's mouth into it, and wash the mouth with it; and do not allow the patient to swallow anything and hold the water for another hour with your hand, for the leech, as soon as it feels the water, will fall off.
But if it is not dislodged by what we have described, then fumigate the patient with incense or asafoetida using the instrument we described for fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it will fall off.
And another method of fumigation for that is that you take a bowl containing burning coals, and the bowl is covered with a cover in the middle of which is a hole, and the tip of the instrument is placed in that hole.  Then you must perform the fumigation above it; and place the patient's mouth on the tip of the cannula, and close their mouth to ensure the fumigation does not escape until you know that it has reached the leech, for the leech will fall off as soon as the fumigation reaches it.
And if it does not fall off, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and prevent the patient from drinking, and have them eat salty things and herbs, and do not allow them to drink water, and with this treatment it will be necessary for it to be expelled.
And this is the shape of the instrument with which the leech from the throat is treated when it is visible; and it is similar to forceps in some ways, but it has a slight curve in some places. which is placed on the throat, and its tip is similar to a stork's beak, which is smooth, on which a file is used, so that when we grasp something, it does not slip.
Chapter 40.
In which is the summary of the discourse on the appearance of abscesses and their incision.
Abscesses are of many types, in many ways, according to their appearance, each one described in this book.
And they are diverse in their puncture and incision, in two ways, of which one is according to the type of abscess itself; and due to the moisture we grasp.
And the second way is according to the places on the body where they occur, for the abscess that occurs in the head is different from the abscess that occurs in the buttocks; and the abscess in a fleshy area is different from the abscess that occurs in a joint; and for each of these, there is a specific treatment.
And some abscesses must not be incised until the pus has matured within them, or until they are fully developed.
Some other abscesses should be lanced while they are inflamed and have not matured, such as those near joints.  For, when an abscess is near a joint, its growth extends until it could damage the surrounding tissue, and the ligaments or nerves of that joint are easily corrupted, causing the limb to contract.  Or, if the abscess is near a vital part of the body, delay in lancing it until maturity could harm the vital area.  Or, if it's near the anus, lance it even if it isn't fully mature.  Do not lance an unripe, incompletely digested one, but one near the anus, so you do not rupture it and cause an extension of the problem into the internal parts of the anus and create a fistula, or cause harm to the surrounding area which won't heal. But you must know the time to lance the abscess, and that is when it is mature.
You will know it is mature when the pain subsides, the fever ceases, the swelling and throbbing decrease, the head of the abscess becomes firmer, and other similar signs appear.
And because you must lance the abscess in the pus-filled area below it, and from the swelling's drainage.
And always make the incision along the length of the flesh or body, if the abscesses are in the areas of the hands or feet, and in locations away from the tendons, nerves, and arteries; and, in all similar areas where there is no reflex action.  For, in those places that are reflex-sensitive, use a method of incision appropriate to that specific body part.
However, when the abscess is in a fleshy area, it is better to leave it until its digestion is confirmed, as we have said, for if you lance it before that, you prolong the patient's suffering, add to the dirt, and make the abscess walls hardened and deep.
But some abscesses must be lanced across the breadth of the flesh or body, depending on the need or the specific requirements of the affected limb.
And you must administer a small incision for small abscesses, and a large, wide incision, or multiple incisions, depending on the size of the abscesses.
And others are abscesses in which the boil must be hollowed out and incised when it is ready, as when dealing with swellings and boils.
And some are incised with a three-sided cut; and some are incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf, as are the abscesses of the joints.
And some are treated with a round incision and a crescent-shaped incision, and similar ones.
But those in which no such procedure is appropriate, like superficial abscesses, you should pierce with a superficial, or simple, incision.
And when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, you should pierce it and not try to remove all the matter at once; having removed a portion, then close the abscess until the next day, and then remove a larger portion, and repeat this many times according to the degree until it is entirely discharged.
And especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an elderly person in decline, for the animal spirit is often weakened by the discharge of pus, and the patient often dies quickly, and you must proceed cautiously.
Observe this chapter with great caution, and for your incision in which you pierce these abscesses, you should clean the sores and consider whether the abscess wound is small, or a simple incision, then apply ointments of lead or old linen; and if the abscess is large and the fissures of the incision are many, then you must place medicaments in each opening until they join together.
And if in the abscess a part of the skin has been separated or has been incised around the circumference, then you should fill it with old linen or cloth thread, as when you have removed bread or roots, without moisture; and keep it tight until the third day; and then you should process it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals.
But if blood flows during your procedure, then apply cold water and vinegar in which you have dipped a linen cloth, and place this over the site of the blood flow repeatedly.
But if the blood flow persists, then you must administer treatment and remedies, and powders which have been recounted in many places in this book and in various divisions.
But if it is wintertime, and the location of the abscess is a place with many nerves, then you must soak powders in warm wine and oil, and apply those to the affected areas.
And if it is summer, and the areas are fleshy, then you must apply powders soaked in water and oil, or wine and oil, and all this should remain cold until the third day, as we have said.  Then you must open the abscess, cleanse it, and apply the appropriate treatment until it heals.
And this is what is necessary for the understanding of the treatment of abscesses in general; but according to the method of distinction that I have already mentioned, the treatment of each type of abscess is described in individual chapters.
Chapter 41.
Of the lancing of abscesses which arise on the neck of the head.
Small abscesses occur on the neck of the head, and these are of the nature of lumps that contain membranes surrounding them; and the content of these lumps is pus.
And they have pus, and that pus contains a moisture resembling lime. And some contain a moisture resembling dust, and some are like them; and some are hard, like stones.
And in the lancing of all of them, and in the extraction of these, there is no fear, as the extraction of these does not cause contrary arrows.
And the surgical procedure for lancing these is that you probe them with an instrument whose shape is similar to this, which is called a lancet, until you know what they contain.
For if what it contains is moisture, then you cut it with a lengthwise incision, a superficial incision in this manner:
And begin the incision along line B to line G.  Then, once the moisture is drained, scrape away the skin that contained the moisture, and leave no remnants of it at all, for it often returns if any part remains.
And then, soak some cotton in Egyptian ointment, if available, or else in water and salt; and fill the wound with that and leave it until the next day, for this will corrode any remaining skin remnants.
And then repeat applying Egyptian ointment to the cotton a second and third time, if necessary, until you are confident that there are no remaining remnants of that skin; then treat the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodules, then you cut it in this manner: with an incision shaped like a cross, and insert hooks between the two edges of the incision and scrape it from every side, and exert your strength in extracting the membrane that contains it.
But if your arrowhead causes resistance, then do as I have first described to you.
And similarly, treat a stony abscess with incision and treatment as we have told you; and incision on a stony abscess is easier, because it has little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes there are some of these abscesses which are on the head in some men, which lack moisture;  the reason why is that I incised an abscess on the head of an elderly woman and found the abscess like a hard, rough, white stone, which could not be broken, and if someone were to strike it, it would hurt.
But in other accidental abscesses on the head, this is not the topic, like the abscesses that occur on the heads of children and near the roots of the ears, but the incision for all of these should be a superficial incision.
And make the perforation in those from the lower part, so that the body's pus is drained to the lower parts.
And then treat those with what is appropriate for their treatment.
Chapter 42.

Chapter 52.
Concerning the treatment of a swelling of the navel.
A swelling of the navel is caused by various things; either by fissures in the skin covering the abdomen, and there the tissue or intestines protrude as in other ruptures; or by blood flowing from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned, or by trapped air in the area.

But if it is due to dead flesh, then the abscess is serious, large, and hard, and is embedded within a quantity (or, localized in a specific area).
But if it is due to wind (gas), then touching it is painless.
And the procedure for it is to consider if the swelling around the navel is due to blood from an arrow wound, or a vein, or wind;  then you must avoid treating it, because there is danger and fear, just as taught in the chapter where I described abscesses from arrow wounds and vein damage.

And then, cut (or incise) the middle of the extended abscess along the ligament, and insert your finger into that incision and search for the intestine; and if you find it or have grasped it with the ligament, then you remove the obstruction and push the intestine back into the belly cavity.
And if you have found the hernia, then grasp it with a scalpel; and cut away the excess tissue.
But if it is an arrow wound or vein injury, then circle that area, and return to your procedure; and take two needles and place strong threads on them, and insert the two needles into the circumference you have made around the abscess in the form of a cross, which you will have to pierce; and then press the abscess into four parts over the needles.

And if the swelling at the navel is of flesh formed there or from moisture, then it is necessary that you drain the abscess as we have said and remove the blood or moisture that you find there, and then treat it with what is appropriate and causes the area to consolidate.

Of the cleansing of children and the treatment of what happens to them during circumcision.

The foreskin has two layers, and the inner layer remains, which forces a further incision and causes unnecessary pain. And then place your hand on its penis and blow into the sheath, and pull it in so that the head of the penis emerges, and then clean it of any dirt that is stuck to it.
The shape of the scissors for cleaning, this is the shape:

Chapter 65.
Of the treatment of intestinal hernia.
