Chapter 68.
Of softening the hardening of the testicles.
Often the hardening of the testicles in some men is softened by this, because the flesh surrounding them is softened, and it is trusted that it will work.
Therefore, it is necessary for whoever is entrusted with the cure to have the patient lie down according to their side, and to carefully remove all the hardened tissue that has softened over the natural testicle; and then to stitch together the two edges with sutures.
And if you wish, stitch the excess softened tissue at the beginning with three or four stitches, using firm sutures.
And then, drain between the sutures; and then treat with healing remedies for other sores until it is healed and the pus subsides.
Chapter 69.
Of castration.
Know that castration, in our laws, is forbidden, but by reason of necessity, it is proper for me to include it in this book; and its mention is not needed except in two ways.  One is that it falls within the knowledge of the physician to be questioned on it, so that he might know the cure for it when it occurs. The other way is that we often have need of castrating other animals for our own benefit, as with oxen, goats, pigs, and similar beasts.
Therefore, I say that castration is done in two ways: either by crushing, or by slitting and cutting.
However, in that which is done by crushing, the method is to place the animal in hot water until its genitals are softened and relaxed and hang down. And then grasp them with your hand until they are broken and no longer appear beneath the touch.
But in castration performed by slitting and cutting, you must hold the animal and press the scrotum with your left hand.
And then tie the suspensory ligament, and cut on each testicle with a cut until both testicles are separated.
And then cut them further, once you have each peeled away the skin. And do not leave any cause of the sheath, except the subtle sheath that is upon the vessels.
And this method of castration, done by crushing, is better, for a strong crush leaves no cause in the testicles for the animal to desire copulation.
And then treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
Of the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men occurs in two ways: one is when there is a visible space in the scrotum, between the testicles,  shaped like a woman's vulva, with hair on it.
And sometimes urine flows from the area between the testicles.
But in women it is a different type:  it is when the woman's vulva is, in a way, like two testicles of a man, slightly extending outward like the penis of a man, and [with] two testicles.
And the treatment is of three types: for two types in men and one type in women. And you must cut through those adhering flesh until the swelling subsides, and then treat them with the same remedies as other sores.
And in the second type of men's ailment, where there is urine in the groin area of the testicles, there is no cure nor operation for it.
Chapter 21.
Of tumors and prominent flesh due to causes related to women.
A mild tumor, if it is caused by natural reasons and is restricted and easily managed, sometimes grows significantly in some women, spreading as widely as it does in men and leading to difficulties in sexual intercourse.
Then you should hold the excess tumor with your hand or forceps and cut it.
And do not overdo the incision, especially at the root of the tumor, so that you do not cause excessive bleeding.
And then treat it with the treatment for wounds until it is healed.
But flesh that arises within the mother's womb, filling it, and then erupts through the body in the shape of a tail (and for this reason some earlier physicians called this "tail disease"); therefore, you should cut it like you would cut a tumor and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 72.
Of the treatment of dropsy.
Dropsy is when the vagina of the woman is not perforated, or the opening is small. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is due to an illness that has preceded it, and this is caused either by improperly joined flesh or by a subtle or thick membrane.
And it is located either in the mother's womb, or in her ribs, or in the upper part of her, or in the lower part of her, and you prohibit food and drink and childbirth, and strongly prohibit menstruation.
And the diagnosis of this is made by observation of the midwife, if the blockage is apparent, immediately; but if it is not apparent, then you seek it with your fingers or with the rod.
But if the blockage is due to a thin membrane, and it is close to the two lips, then you cut and break it open.
And it is that you place powders on the two lips, and then place your index fingers of both hands on top.
And the woman should be on her back, her thighs extended, and then forcefully extend the two lips until that thin membrane is broken and the blockage is opened.
Then take wool, soak it in oil, and place it over the area.  Let the person rest with a covering over it during the day to prevent the wound from healing too quickly.
But if the membrane is thick and dense, then you must cut it with a sharp knife or with a myrtle leaf.
But if the blockage is due to a clot, then suspend it with a hook and cut it.
And have with you the medicines that stop blood flow without causing a bite, such as acacia, dragon's blood, and gum arabic, mixed with egg white.
And then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not heal too quickly.
And keep it for a few days and administer poultice of the same.
And then be treated with the other healing treatment for consolidation until it is healed.
And sometimes it spreads to other tissues, and then it is necessary to cut according to this method. But if this is due to a brain abscess, a brain abscess that is in the head should not be treated with any iron in any way.
Chapter 73.
Of the treatment of hemorrhoids and of tail and red swelling that occurs in the female perineum.
Hemorrhoids are understood as the openings of many veins through which a continuous flow of blood runs; and when hemorrhoids are old and they are (or have been) made into prolapses (or have prolapsed).
And I have already discussed the types of those and their signs, and I will now say which of them receive treatment and which do not.
Therefore, I say that hemorrhoids and prolapses, when they are in the rectum and not apparent to the touch, then in those cases no treatment with iron should be performed.
But those which are in the opening of the rectum and are felt, are those that are treatable.
And it is necessary that you place the woman in a cold place and then carefully cut the prolapsed area with scissors, or with a rough cloth, and cut them at the roots.
Then, sprinkle over them the flow of blood from one of the incised powders, without biting, like acacia and similar substances.
And then you should immerse their feet in a basin of cold water for an hour, and then place the affected area in cool water.
And if the flow of blood persists, then make a poultice using a decoction of the tops and bark of pomegranates, and quinces, and similar substances.
And then take barley flour and mix it with honey and sour wine, and make a poultice with this and apply it to their back.
And if the blood has stopped, good; but if not, apply two leeches, and to the nipples without incision.
Then take wool and immerse it in coriander juice, or juice of arugula, or juice of rue mixed with diluted wine, and apply it to the affected area; and then treat the area with ointments until it is healed.
And treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
Of the accidental perforation of the eyssedura (likely meaning the eustachian tube) in the ear.
Many kinds of abscesses may occur in the ear, such as cancer, stony abscess, ulcers, boils, eruptions, fistulas, hemorrhoids, sores, and other abscesses. And I have already discussed these illnesses and their types, their signs, and their treatments in the division.
And I must discuss in this treatise the abscess that arises in the ear when it is among the abscesses that collects pus, and if you wish to know how its surgical operation is performed with an iron tool.
Then you must consider if the pain of the abscess at its beginning is sharp with throbbing, phlegm, and fever, and if it shows redness and if touching it feels hot, then do not proceed with puncturing it, and treat it with things that aid maturation until the sharpness of the symptoms subsides.
And then you must lay the woman on a table with two supports; and incline her on her back and adjust her feet to the part below her abdomen, and her thighs should be spread out, and place her arms below her elbows, and bind each with a bandage suitable for this purpose.
And then the midwife, standing to the right side, should administer the instrument with which the opening in the womb is made; the shape of which will be seen in the upcoming details.
And when you wish to administer this instrument, you should insert the tip of the instrument into the womb of the woman, and what of the instrument enters the womb should only be the tip, and ease the patient's suffering. But if the instrument is larger than the opening of the womb, then you must place powders on the tissues of the womb, so that the entrance of any instrument into the womb is prevented.
And you must place the rotating part of the instrument on the top, and the assistant should hold the instrument and turn the rotating part until the opening of the womb is made.
And when the discharge appears, and is touched with the hand and is slimy and wet, then you must dry that around the patient's head with a linen cloth.
And when the discharge is evacuated, then you should place a cloth soaked in rosemary oil or green oil (in which there is some astringent substance) on the wound, and apply the compress to the area in the womb.
And place outside the womb, and over the perineum, a clean wool soaked in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And then it should be treated for about two days with an ointment appropriate for this until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and the wound are washed in such a way that it is cleansed with water and honey, and then with water in which lily root or long aristolochia has been boiled, and then it returns to the ointment.
And if the wound is hidden inside the womb, then you should be prohibited from treating it with iron, and instead treat it with what we have described in the division.
Chapter 75.
To teach the midwives how to treat living embryos when their development is not natural.
A necessary thing is that the midwife know the natural form of childbirth by its signs.  When you see the woman pushing from below and desiring to breathe air, and this is relieved by what is upon her during delivery, and hastens the expulsion of the embryo, then know that this birth is in a natural form; and the expulsion is of the child above its head and the afterbirth either hanging or suspended from the navel.
Therefore, when you see these signs, then the abdomen should be pressed so that the embryo descends easily; for when it descends headfirst, it leaves behind the afterbirth, and it is cleaned of those superfluities with a good cleaning.
But that which is different from this birth is unnatural and undesirable, for when the embryo is expelled feet first, and its body comes out before its head and feet, or its one hand, or foot, or its head and hand, or its belly, and its head is strongly bent over its neck, and its form is of an unnatural and undesirable type.
Therefore, a necessary quality for the midwife is to be knowledgeable and astute in all these matters, and to guard against error and deception.
And I am showing all the forms of these, and what skill is within them so that it may be understood and be established upon them.
When the birth has occurred headfirst through natural expulsion, and this is difficult for the woman and taxing on her, and her strength is already weakened, then apply this poultice, or what women use, and vaporize its head with collected fenugreek and with moist oils.
And then the midwife should insert her fingers into the small space and fissure with it the afterbirth, or sever it with her fingernail until she removes whatever moisture is within it. And press on the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then the woman must be treated with a poultice of plantain and a poultice of fenugreek with cumin.
And then command him, then the attendant, to be compressed and cause him to sneeze with spices; and cover his mouth and nose for an hour, for the fetus will be expelled shortly.
And if the two hands of the fetus are expelled at first, then you must gently reduce them bit by bit. But if you do not succeed in the procedure, then place the woman on a table and raise her feet up, and then tilt the table towards the ground; and hold the woman so she does not fall during the tilting.
And if the hands are not returned and the fetus dies, then cut them open and extract the remains of the fetus, or bind the body to the patient's hand with cloth, and extract it with gentleness, for that will be expelled.
When the fetus is on its feet, then you must push them up, then gently turn the fetus, and then gently swallow it, and then take one foot and extend it slowly.
But when it has emerged into the thighs, then you command the person to cough, and make them sneeze with diligence, for it will be expelled if God wills it.
And if it is not expelled by what we have recounted, then you return the fetus slowly until you put it back in its natural position, for it will then be expelled with easy delivery.
And if it is still not expelled by all that we have described, then take marshmallow root and dissolved gum, and pound them thoroughly in a mortar, then apply this mixture to the vulva, to the lowest part of her abdomen. And then apply this compress in lukewarm water, until the hypochondria is relieved.
And when the parts below her are soothed, then apply a fumigation of myrtle and place it over her. And when the fumigation has been applied for an hour, then you make her sit on a cushion, and then make her sneeze and compress her mouth, and remove it, and press the lowest part of her abdomen gently, for the fetus will soon be expelled.
The fetus emerges lengthwise, hanging from one of her hands. Be skillful in reducing the hand of the patient, but if you cannot reduce it, then lift the woman and make her walk.
And if walking is not possible, then have her lie on the bed, and forcefully compress her thighs, and then administer bandages soaked in mucilages.
And if labor has begun, then have her sit on a stool, and then carefully study the reduction of the hand, and pull the fetus into a natural position, and make the woman sneeze and tell her to push until it is expelled.
The fetus is positioned on the mother's pubic area, its hands and face are on her abdomen. The midwife should hold the baby's hand and carefully turn it slowly and gently.
And when it is pulled out, then place on its head what we have mentioned of oil and mucilages.
And then instruct her to move slightly, and when you have done this, have her sit on a stool and tell her to lean to her right side, and then instruct her to push; and make her sneeze with moderation. The prepared fetus is positioned on its side.
When the mother stops having contractions in this manner, consider it, and then you cut the afterbirth with your fingernail until the moisture is expelled.
And if the fetus is hanging from the face and neck, then you cut that from the umbilical cord, so that it is not retained and dies.
Then push the fetus in the directions inside until you place it as it should be, and tell the woman to push herself a few times, and it will be expelled easily, if God wills it.
Delivery of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are born quite often, and sometimes three or four, but this is unusual and rarely happens; but various things are outside of nature, and not alive.
And count them, whether they are multiplied, a second, and they are separated by a cipher, distinguishing them between the ligament in the navel of each of them. And sometimes they are expelled as one fetus is by a similarly easy natural way, and sometimes it happens by an unacceptable way as we have recounted.
And if some of them are difficult during childbirth and reach the point we have mentioned about unnatural formations, then you should treat them as we have described.
And if the midwife is skillful and does this with ease and subtlety, and with patience, and is ingenious according to every figure, according to what is prepared for her by the guiding intelligence to lead to health.
A recollection of what is formed in the womb from embryos and miscarriages.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and even ten are formed in the mother's womb.  I have personally verified that one woman miscarried six, and another ten, all formed, the cause of which was determined through the measurements of the learned and glorious.
And all of this must be within the midwife's knowledge, for it is easily recognized by the small signs of it, and therefore one must act accordingly.
Chapter 76.
Of the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat the dead fetus with what we have discussed in the division of medicine, and it hasn't emerged naturally, and you are forced to use manual intervention, then you must consider if the woman is of sound constitution and not afflicted by any of the conditions that cause fear of her loss.
Then the woman must be positioned on the bed on her back, and her head should be inclined downward and her thighs raised.
And the attendant should hold either part of her or tie her to the bed so that her body doesn't move away during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you should moisten the mother's mouth with soothing oils and with mucilage of marshmallow, fenugreek, and linseed.
And then the midwife should anoint her hand with those oils and mucilages, and then gently introduce her hand into the mother, and then search with her hand for the place where she can hook her fingers into the fetus.
And suitable places for this: then consider if the fetus is positioned so its head is downward, and then hook your fingers into its neck, or into its mouth, or palate, or under the chin, or in the fork of the jaw, or in the appropriate areas of the ribs, or below the armpits.
But if the descent is towards the feet, then in the groin, or in the middle of the back.
And it is necessary to hold the finger with the right hand, and place the curve of the finger in that place which is between the fingers of the left hand, and gently introduce the hand and place the fingers in any of those places we have mentioned, until reaching the empty space.
And then place and hook another finger, or a third if necessary, so that there is a pulling inwards, and not an outward extension merely along its own path.
And it should be moved in all directions, so that the extraction of it is eased, just as is done with a tooth during its extraction.
And it is necessary in the area between them that the extension be softened; and if any part of it is retained, then the midwife should anoint some of her fingers with oil and insert those fingers and turn around that which is retained.
Therefore, if the part of the fet is forced out, then the fingers should be moved to a higher location; and do this until all the fet is expelled.
But if the hand is pulled out before the other, and it is not possible to return it by constriction, then a cloth should be wrapped around it; and then pull it out until it is all expelled, and cut it either at the joint of the hand or at the elbow.
And thus you should do for the other hand and the two feet.
But if the head of the fet is large, or constriction occurs during the expulsion, or fluid is collected at its head, then it is necessary to insert fingers between the spinal vertebrae and to break the head open and drain the fluid, or to cut it with an instrument called "alundat," the shape of which will be found with the shapes of other instruments in the chapter that follows this one.
And similarly, do so if the fet has a large head by nature:  and then it is necessary to cut it with a scalpel, or to grasp it with an alundat as we have said; and then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head is protruding and constricted towards the fork, then it should be split open until the moisture in the foot is expelled.
But if it is not complicated, nor obstructing the expulsion, then you cut the head as soon as possible.
And if the lower part of the abdomen is abscess-ridden or hydrops, then you must pierce it until the moisture within is drained.
But if the fet is on its feet, then the traction of it is gentle and its direction towards the mother's mouth is gentle.
But if it is constricted towards the belly and the foot, then you must extract it with a cloth wrapped around your hand, and cut open the belly and the foot until that which is within them runs out.
But if the other limbs are removed and the head is still there, then the midwife should insert her left hand; but if the mother's mouth is open, then she should insert her hand into the vagina, and search for the head with it, and pull it towards the mother's mouth with her fingers, and then insert a finger, or two fingers, into it and pull it with them.
And if the mother's mouth is obstructed by a hot abscess which affects the opening, then it should not be roughly treated.  Instead, a poultice of fatty, moist substances should be applied. And apply poultices; and keep the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the fetus is on its side, if possible, it should be turned: and what we have said about a living fetus should be administered.
And if that is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces and extracted.
And you must not leave any cause inside the afterbirth.
And when you have completed all your operation, then administer what we have said about the treatment of boils, which sometimes occur in the mother.
And if a flow of blood occurs, then immerse it in astringent waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have already seen a woman in whose womb a stillbirth had occurred, and shortly after that fire again became pregnant, and shortly after the stillbirth happened again. A long time ago, an abscess occurred near his navel; and it became swollen until it was opened, and it began to discharge pus.
And I was called to treat him, and I medicated him for a long time, but the wound did not heal.
Then I applied some strong-drawing plasters to him, and a bone came out of him, and after a few days another bone came out; and I was very surprised, as there are no bones in the belly.
And then I thought that they were bones from the stillborn child.
Then I cleaned the wound, and removed many bones from him.  The woman, who was in good health in other ways, lived in this condition for a long time, but only a little pus was discharged.
And I did not consider this strange, because it is knowledge and aid to what the skillful physician uses in their treatment.
Of the forms of instruments needed to extract the fetus.
Method of turning with which the mother is opened:
This is the form of a tightening device where the books are bound, to which binding are two turns at the two ends of two pieces. However, this turning needs to be more subtle than a tightening device, and should be made either of a reed or of wooden stick.
And let the width of each piece be two fingers and the margin of each be one finger, and the length of each is one and a half palms; and in the middle place of the two pieces are two attachments of the same pieces, which are already fastened, whose length is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two attachments are inserted into the mouth of the mother so that it is opened by them when you turn the two prescribed turns.
Another form similar to that, more subtle and lighter:
Let it be made of reed wood or wood, resembling forceps. However, at its extremity there are two attachments, just like before, and the length of those attachments is about one palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you want to open the mother with them, then you have the woman lie on the bed with her feet hanging down, opening the area between her thighs. And then open your hand with the instrument, just as you would with scissors,  according to the amount you want to expand the opening of the mother's passageway, until the midwife accomplishes what you wish.
The method of turning, which the ancients said:
Method of pushing by which the fetus is pushed.
Method of assisting with which the large head is delivered.
And it is similar to forceps, which has teeth at the extremity, as sometimes, and sometimes it is made long, like forceps of this form, as sometimes, which has teeth like saw teeth with which it is incised and delivered. Method of gentle assistance.
Method of using a hook having a spine (or point).
Method of using a hook having two spines (or points).
Method of a spatula with two extremities, with which the head is severed.
Method of two wide spatulas.
All those instruments, the more numerous the types and the better they are prepared by the artisans, the easier they are to use and the more beneficial they are to people.
And do not neglect these instruments, wherever they may be, and be prepared for them, for the need for these is not excusable.
Of the extraction of the second [thing/part/item].
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then you should instruct the woman to hold her breath, and then make her sneeze with some means, and firmly press your hand over her mouth and nose; and if by this it comes out, it is good.
And if not, then you take a bowl, and make a hole in its cover, and place in it herbs such as rue, anise, chamomile, Arabic cicadas, cinnamon, and centaury, placing the bowl over the mother's open mouth, as a poultice. All these herbs, or some of them, should be submerged in water in the bowl, and place that over the fire.
And then place a reed tube in the mother's mouth, and hold it there until the vapor reaches the mother's cavity.
And then make her sneeze as we have said, and the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.
But if this treatment remains ineffective and it hasn't been expelled, then you order the midwife to immerse her left hand in sesame oil or marshmallow mixture, and then insert that into the vagina and try to remove the afterbirth with it.  And when you have found it, take it and pull it out slowly.  But if it's attached to the mother's womb, then insert her hand as we have described, and when she has located the afterbirth, extract it little by little, as instructed, so that the mother does not experience a violent pull. Beforehand, it is necessary that it be moved easily from the right and left sides.
And if the mother's mouth is constricted, we have already described the treatment with sneezing and with medicinal poultices of herbs.
But if it is not expelled by all the methods we have described, then beware of exhausting it further by continued attempts to remove it. Instead, it is necessary that what has been expelled from her into the mother's mouth be placed on the woman's thigh, and then treat it with unguents and tetrapharmacon.  For this will cause it to decompose over several days and dissolve, and thus be expelled.
However, when it decomposes, a foul odor rises to the head and stomach, and this afflicts the patient; and then it is necessary to administer fumigations and remedies appropriate to this.
However, some of the ancients already tested fumigations with juniper and dried figs.
Shape of the instrument with which the woman is fumigated for the retention of menstrual blood and the afterbirth:
It should be made of glass or brass.
And place the thin end in the container and the wide end over the fire, and the fumigation material should be placed on the embers of the fire.
And keep it there until the fumigation is consumed and put in another.
Of the treatment of the unperforated anus of the child.
Many children are born with their anuses unperforated, which are already narrow and obstructed. Then it is necessary for the midwife to pierce that narrowing with her finger; and if not, pierce it with a sharp instrument, and be careful not to touch the rectum.
And then place over it wool soaked in wine and oil; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is obstructed, then place a tube in the opening for many days, and remove it when the child wants to swallow.
And blockage of the anus may occur due to the consolidation of a plague or an abscess; then it is necessary that that consolidation be incised, and then be treated with what we have said about the treatment and lead.
Of the treatment of the fistulas that arise from there.
Fistulas that form in the lower region,  owing to a swelling and thickening, which form near the anus externally or in the space between the buttocks.
And it is a fistula or abscesses.
And when that swelling is longstanding and oozes a watery, whitish fluid or a subtle pus, and sometimes these fistulas are also deep-seated.
And those deep-seated ones are recognized by what comes out of them, by the discharge and by the windiness; and sometimes the patient has a discharge, and often worms come out of them.
And sometimes these fistulas extend into the bladder, and into the urethra; and some of these deep ones extend into the joint of the thigh and into the groin area.
And how a deep-seated fistula in the anus is distinguished from a non-deep-seated one is that you insert your index finger into the anus, and gently probe for a thin rod of a ramrod or iron in the fistula if there is no obstruction.
But if there is an obstruction in it, then insert a thin probe of lead or horsehair until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
And if you feel nothing at all, and there is no discharge of any kind, either worms or windiness, as we have said, then know that it is not deep-seated, and therefore your treatment is appropriate.
And when the fistula extends into the bladder or into the urethra, then its sign is the presence of urine from there, and a prohibition of urination until the area is healed with medicine.
But if it extends into the hip joint or into the bone, and there is pain in the surrounding area, then its sign is that the probe penetrates there, if there is no obstruction into the bone, and pain in the surrounding area; and that it is likely to remain this way; and that no medication will be effective or will heal with ointment.
And in all those deep-seated fistulas, there is no complete cure, and the treatment for them is the labour and vanity of foolish doctors, upon which one should not rely.
But in those that are not deep-seated and not long-standing, healing with iron is expected, as I will tell you, and this I have already tried and proven.
And it is necessary that the patient lies on their back with their legs raised upwards, their thighs bent towards their stomach, and then carefully insert a lead probe, or an iron one if there is a bend in the fistula, until you know the exact spot where the probe penetrates.
And if the pain is around the anus, then you should insert your index finger into the anus.
And if you feel the probe already penetrating on your finger, or if you feel something between your finger and the tissue or flesh, then know for certain that it is deep-seated; and then do not work on it, for there is no cure in such a case, as we have said.
But nevertheless, some might say that it heals in some men slowly.
And the treatment by which healing is expected is that you heat a thin cautery according to the width of the fistula, as previously described, and insert that heated cautery into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat this two or three times until you know that all the inflamed flesh and fistula-like tissue which resembles a bird feather quill are now cauterized.
And then treat it with some bandages until all the cauterized flesh is healed, and then treat it with medicines that generate and solidify flesh.  If it is healed, well and good; but if not, then it will not be healed at any time by any other treatment.
And if the probe enters, but does not penetrate to your finger in the anus, and between the probe and that is a thick layer of flesh or tissue, and the fistula is on the surface of the hard skin, then cut the hard skin at the beginning of the fistula, and you should cut with the probe, and move it in the fistula until the incision reaches the place where the end of the probe reaches, and by the probe, it is freed and extracted.
And then clean those fistula-like tissues, which are like a bird feather quill, and no trace of them remains.
But if blood flow occurs between you and your procedure when you cauterize those tissues, then the best and most immediate instrument is to cauterize with fire or with a sharp medicine, for cauterization with fire has two good effects: it is the cauterization of the inflamed tissues and the excision of the blood, removing the moisture.
And then treat it with bandages or a crushed cloth until the wound is closed, and then treat the wound with ointments that generate and consolidate hard flesh.
And these medicines, which involve astringency and excision, when then restored, are healed and you need not fear a recurrence.
But if the fistula has already reached the anus and is distant from the body's surface, then insert your finger into the anus and examine it with that.
And therefore, if you feel resistance and between you and it is a layer of phlegm or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then there is no operation there, but rather a treatment through trust and hope.
This condition can be addressed in one of three ways: either cauterize it as we have said; or excise it until you achieve its cavity, so that you can insert remedies and the treatment is effective; and do not proceed too much with excision, so that you do not cut the ligament that contains the bowel, and that would lead to harming the patient unintentionally; and then treat it as we have said, for a quick cure will result; or separate the membrane and clean the pus that you dry with excision near the anus, as we have said, either with a probe or with another instrument with a sharp tip, and then clean the fistulous flesh which is in the excision, until you can treat it with a scab; and then strive to restore the entire wound with excision, and if the orifice above is not healed, and the fistula remains open in the anal region, it will be less arduous for the patient. And sometimes the fistula is cured in this way: when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is located in the folds of the anus near the body's surface, with the curve and at the margin of the anus, then take a probe with a perforated tip, which is like a needle used to prepare skin in this manner:
and insert into that a probe wrapped in five probes or around those; and then insert a probe with a probe inside the fistula until you reach its cavity.
However, if it is penetrating at the margin of the anus near the inside, then you withdraw the probe from that opening as if you were inserting your finger into the anus; and withdraw the tip of the probe and join the two ends together, and tighten each one, and leave it for one or two days. And when you further cut the probe into the flesh, tightly hold it until the flesh that is between the two ends of the probe is cut and falls away.
And then treat the wound until it is healed and sound.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, in whatever way is prepared for you, but not too much into the peritoneum, for it is not up to you to do that so as not to cut the intestines; and then treat it until it is healed.
The shape of the spatula with which the fistula is opened: the curvature of which is very sharp, and the other part is not sharp, so as not to cut what is not necessary, just as sometimes happens.
Of the suturing of hemorrhoids in which blood flows in the incision of those.
Hemorrhoids are of two kinds, for either they are between the buttocks, similar to bunches of grapes, or they are like clusters of grapes. And of those, some are small and some large, and the blood flows from them constantly, and some are one, and some are many, and some are outside the anus, and on its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are according to the measures of little moisture, from which flows yellowish water or a little blood with continuous matter, and it is according to the color of the matter.
And the treatment for those that are inside the anus is to order the patient to sit and press until the anus opens and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you grasp and lift them with tweezers or your fingernails, and then cut them away from their roots.
However, if the tweezers are not holding them because of their moisture and softening, then take them with a rough cloth or cotton or a piece of wool, and remove them with your fingers, and then cut them and sprinkle some warm powders over the incisions, so that they are there instead of cauterization; or cauterize them as is previously described in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat them with the other treatment until they are healed.
And if the anus does not open, then prick the patient with a pricker in which there is a small bite, and wash with what is in the anus, so that it opens easily when the patient presses.
However, if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then their cause is slight, and you should grasp them with your fingernail, or with tweezers, cut them, and then treat them with what we have said until they are healed.
And whoever has made the incision, must stitch them using this method: take a thin thread and thread it through a needle, then pull the hemorrhoids apart, and using the needle, pierce into the root of the other side, and wrap the two ends of the thread along the lower part of the needle, in a horizontal manner. Then tightly constrict the hemorrhoid with the stitch, and afterwards tie the thread and remove the needle.
Do this to all of them, and leave one unsewn, so that the excess blood can flow from it.
Then place a cloth soaked in rubbed oil or ointment on the anus; place it over the anus and tell the patient to rest. And then leave it until it falls out; and when it has fallen out, treat it with ointments or other remedies until it is healed.
And if they are old and do not respond to that medicine, then you should scrape them with the tip of a spatula or your fingernail until they are moist and the scab of pus above them is removed, which prevents their consolidation; then treat them until they are consolidated.
Then you scrape them with a more forceful scraping than the first until much blood is drawn and they are raw; then treat them until they are healed.
Know that elephantiasis, that is to say, a key (or important) issue, and is not otherwise known, is a reddish swelling like a nail head, and it forms on the whole body, especially in the parts below the feet and on the legs, and it is caused by that swelling.
And then it is necessary to cut that which is around the swelling, and hold it with scissors or forceps, and uproot it from its root; and then treat that.
And if you wish, cauterize it according to what is previously described in its chapter.
But a wart is a small, rough, fistulous, round protuberance on the body's surface, and often appears on the hands of children.
And its treatment is to scrape and cut it, or to squeeze it with a file or a peel until it bursts. And if you wish, cauterize this, as we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine.
But a pimple is a small, rough, or fistulous, thick protuberance on the surface of the body, often appearing on the face, and when the cold encounters it, it is felt like an ant bite, and forms around the body.
And the treatment for it is to take a hollow tube made of a bird's feather or a vulture's feather, strong, and place it over the pimple until the tube encircles the area of the pimple; then rotate your hand with the tube until it cuts through whatever is around the pimple, and press it into the flesh, eradicating the pimple from its root.
And if you wish, do this with a tube of bronze or iron in this way:
Let the part above the tube be thin, closed (meaning not perforated), wrapped so that the grip on it is shortened and its rotation is easier.
And if you wish, cauterize the pimple as previously described in the section on cauterizing boils in the chapter on cauterization.
Concerning the instruments used for administering this treatment.
Sometimes cauterization is done with silver or brass or cast or hammered bronze; and sometimes it is done with instruments small and large, according to those for which they are administered, for that which is administered in the treatment of small children should be small; and for those whose buttocks are sore or painful, the cauteries for those should be small.
The form of large and small cauteries:
The head of the cannula above, on which the bag is tied, should be wide in this manner; and there should be a clear distinction where the bag is tied, just as usual.
And the lower end, which is inserted into the buttock, should be closed (that is, not open) very tightly, gradually tapering to a thin point; and on one side of it there should be two holes, and on the other side one hole, and the size of the holes should be according to the thickness of the rays, and a little bigger than the smallest.
And the bag, in which the medicines for the bladder of an animal or sheepskin are placed, should be a palm in width.
And it should be pierced with many holes, just as it is rolled; and if the space between the holes is the width of a finger.
And then insert into those holes a strong, thick, crude (or perhaps, "raw")  bile, amounting to ten parts or roughly so; and attach that to the bag; and when the bag is placed in position, tightly bind the top of the crutch (or "stick") between two distinguishing points with strong constriction, and then bind each hole with it.  However, the technique of binding with this is that you fill the bag with medicine, which should be heated according to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is neither too hot nor too cold.
And now the patient should lie on their back, raise their legs, and place a cloth underneath them, perhaps a sheet or blanket; and then anoint the end of the cautery with oil or egg white or with mucilage of fenugreek or similar; and then insert this into the buttock with ease.  Then the attendant should take the bag and squeeze it with both hands forcefully until everything contained in the crutch has been evacuated into the bowel; then remove the crutch and let the patient lower their legs to the side.
And if the medicine causes expulsion in this way, hold it in place for as long as possible; and if they sleep with it all through the night, it is better for healing.
However, if you use this crutch in cases of loose bowels and expulsion from the body, then you repeat the cautery until it is healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or festering sores in the buttock, then the crutch should be very small and slender, like those used to treat children.
Of the treatment of plagues.
I have already said in the division of the treatment of plagues what was appropriate for the book, and I say thus about the treatment of those with hands and medicines according to the fulfillment.
Therefore, I say that plagues are diversified according to where the plagues are caused and according to the location on which the plagues fall.
And the causes why plagues are often inflicted are like throwing stones, or cutting with a sword or knife, or striking with a lance, or with a staff, or with an arrow, or similar to those from many causes.
But the plagues that are on the parts of the body are like a plague that falls on the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the belly, or the liver, and similar to those of the limbs.
And I am recounting the treatment of some plagues, so that you may apply that reasoning and rule to other plagues.
And I will begin with the simple plagues of the head, for the remembrance of the compounded ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore I say that when a simple plague occurs on the head, and it is not a fracture, then consider: if it is from throwing a stone or similar, and only the scalp is broken and it is a severe wound, and you fear the onset of a hot abscess in that patient, then you should quickly perform phlebotomy on the cephalic vein, and do not delay this; and draw blood from him according to his strength, unless some other cause prevents phlebotomy, especially if the blood that has come out of the wound is little.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the affected part; and protect the patient from repletion and from heavy foods and drinks.
But if some cause prohibits the reduction of food, then reduce the amount of food and do not allow him to eat meat or drink wine.
And if an abscess develops on the wound, apply a poultice of softened oil or wine with a styptic agent.
But if a hot abscess is certain, then apply to the wound, if it is recent, its own blood and the air has not altered the powder that I will shortly recommend.
And if the air has already altered it, then apply some ointments until it is healed.
But if the wound is large or caused by a cut or similar injury, and its edges are not brought together with sutures, then bring them together with sutures, as I will recommend for their suturing.
However, if the wound has already exposed the bone and tendons, and its only problem is a small detachment, then cut it open and then treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh in its place, and instruct the patient to consume foods that are rich in fat: head and feet, and similar things, until it is healed.
And if you find putrefaction in the bone and it does not adhere to the bone, then you cut away all the putrid material and discard it, and then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood may flow, and it has not been severed by medicines, then you investigate the artery, and if you find it uncut, then you cut it with a spatula, or tie it off; and if necessity compels you that what we have said does not work, then cauterize it until the blood is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then their cause is slight; therefore, you can powder them with this powder, for it will consolidate them before the air alters them.
And the way to make the powder is this: take one part olive oil, two parts dragon's blood, and three parts of unquenched lime; grind all of it thoroughly and sieve it, and then fill the wound with it, and bind it tightly until it adheres well and coagulates with the blood.
And then you bind it with bandages and leave it, and do not loosen it until it is no longer inflamed, for the medicine will not be eradicated from it until it is healed.
And sometimes this alone suffices, when you do not have in your presence oil of roses and dragon's blood.
And you do this same procedure on large wounds when it is appropriate to apply it and bind them tightly, and on recent wounds with their own blood, which the air has not yet altered.
And if the air has altered them, especially during summer, then this powder is not appropriate.  Instead, treat them with what we have previously described, or apply digestive ointments, or a poultice of barley flour mixed with water and honey until it hardens; and then treat them with the other methods until they are healed.
But if a small fracture occurs in the bone alongside the wound, then you extract it by manipulation.
And I will describe the treatment of head fractures in what follows.
Of wounds to the neck.
But if a wound occurs on the neck, there is no difference in treatment from a simple wound to the head.
However, if the neck's nerve or artery is cut, then if it's a nerve, it's not an emergency unless the treatment avoids causing excessive astringency, such as using zinc or verdigris (copper sulfate) and similar things, as they damage nerves and cause them to contract. And do not apply anything cold to the wound, for the substance of the nerves is cold and its continuation is with the brain, which is the highest of all the limbs.
And if this is treated with gentle medicines, such as washing with fresh water, sometimes infused in oiled or un-oiled materials, and poultices when washed with fresh water, and other softening stones in this manner, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then apply sutures, or close its edges, and then treat it with powders until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep or reaches the lower, most vulnerable part of the flesh, then pierce it in the deepest point.
But if an artery is already cut in the wound and blood is flowing, then either tie it off, or cauterize it if necessary.
But if the vertebrae of the neck or the whole neck are severed, and the veins are safeguarded, then you must join the edges of the neck wound with sutures to the neck bone and do not touch it.  First, align it and restore it to its natural shape, then firmly bind it with a suitable bandage, and leave it for a few days; and then treat it with ointments that have astringent and stimulating properties until it is healed.
But if the wound has already reached the bone, you must treat it, and if there are fragments of bone within, remove all that you can; and what you cannot remove, leave it until the wound begins to suppurate, and the separation and expulsion of the debris is eased.
And if the wound is recent, then sprinkle powder on it, as instructed. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with what the recollection will provide in its chapter.
If the wound is from a spear or knife, and you observe its depth, then consider further whether the wound is windy (full of air) when the patient breathes; if so, know that the wound is fatal.
And if it is not deep, and the wound is recent with its own blood, then you place on it, at first, a powder, and do not press on it so that the blood is not retained deep inside and returns to the heart and kills the patient, but place there an attractive ointment, and apply it twice a day.
And if the ointment is not available to you, then you place in the wound opening old cloth, so that it absorbs the moisture; and make the patient sleep on the wound, so that what is inside the wound will drain out.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound was inflicted, and you observe no spasms or tremors in the heart, nor constriction of the breathing, nor other bad conditions, then know that the wound is safe; and then treat it with poultices and other remedies until it is healed.
But if healing is impossible, and it has been open for a long time, then know that a fistula has formed; therefore treat it according to its own specific treatment.
And if the wound is from a cut with a knife or sword, and is superficial on the foot or leg, then treat it with stitches, if it is large, or with powder, if it is small.
But if the wound has penetrated the bone, and there are already fragments of bone sticking out, then you should clean the wound and try to remove those fragments if they are loose. But if they are not loose, then leave them until the wound can heal, as removing them would be painful.
And for other wounds on other limbs, the treatment is similar to what we have described.
Of wounds of the abdomen.
The ailment that occurs in the abdomen is either severe, mild, or moderate.
A small rupture is difficult to manage in a different way, and the reduction of the parts is more difficult if you don't quickly return the displaced parts to their place, for if it opens and swells, the return becomes difficult.
Therefore, the best type of rupture is a moderate one, because in that case, the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, unlike the two types mentioned before.
And know that the suturing of the abdomen is done in four ways, but two of these methods are common and applicable to the suturing of other wounds on the body.
Two other specific methods are also applicable to other wounds, yet they are even more specific to suturing the abdomen.
And I will explain each to you with an exposition and a declaration.
Therefore, I say that when the wound is small and some part of the intestine has come out through it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then this is one of two ways: either due to the smallness of the rupture as we have said, or when the intestine has become inflamed due to the coldness of the air.
And therefore, when this is the case, you should warm the area by submerging a damp sponge or cloth in lukewarm water alone, or by using water with squinant, cyperus, and spic, and apply that to the intestine until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes, doing this with a styptic is better than using water alone in resolving the inflammation.
And when you see the inflammation resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which the plant althea and mallow have been boiled, for by this the intestine's discomfort is relieved with little effort.
But if the intestine's discomfort is eased by this treatment, then carefully cut the rupture slightly with an instrument with which hemorrhoids are incised.
And the first treatment is better, when it is proper and involves no incision, and there is no need for or attempt at cutting when there is necessity and weakness.
Of the instrument, here is its shape:
One part of it is sharply curved, and the other part is not sharp, and the tip is slender, and it should not be as slender as a spatula, but rather slightly blunt.
And therefore, when the rupture is enlarged and the intestine is out, then its reduction must be according to its natural form, in its proper place, if possible, for that is better.
But when the rupture is wide and is in the lower part of the abdomen, then you must have the patient lie on their back, and raise their legs higher than their head; and if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then you raise the head and feet higher than the lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two parts of the abdomen, then constantly pay attention and consider this, so that the part where the rupture is positioned is higher than the other.
And thus you must administer this in large ruptures and in medium-sized ones.
And in small ruptures, place what you have prepared, and then have the assistant hold the rupture entirely with their hand and bring the edges together, and then uncover that area in case there is another cause to the suture.
And this is the common method of suture: one takes the needle, or several needles depending on the size of the rupture; then place the tip of the needle at the edge of the rupture on the ring finger, and hold the needle firmly so you do not pull it on the two ends of the ligament, with the two ends of the stitch under the ligament on the inside, until you have made it penetrate that area; and you have now joined the two ends of the ligament and two ends of the stitch, which is done in four stages.
Then tighten with a double thread around the needle, and sometimes from two sides together, and then bring the two edges and the rupture together in a fitting way; then leave the thickness of the finger and place another needle there, and then tie it with thread, just as you did with the first needle; and do not stop doing this if necessary with needles until you have completed the entire constriction of the rupture.
And the needles should be medium in thickness and fineness, because thin needles easily pierce the flesh, and thick ones are difficult to insert into the ligament, and therefore they should be of medium fineness and thickness.
And the needle should not be inserted too close to the two ends of the ligament, so that the flesh is not pierced easily and the wound opens before it heals.
And the sutures should not be too long, so that the wound is not prevented from healing.
Then trim the ends of the needles so that they do not disturb the patient's sleep; and place expanding powders on the sutures, covering the ends of the needles, and leave them there until you know the wound is healed.
And this method of stitching with needles is most suitable for small wounds, for one or two needles, or around that number, are enough for the suture.
But the second suture is more common: and it is that you stitch the four ends of the ligament and two ends of the cyst at once, with a needle that is well-wrapped in both thinness and thickness.
And then, when you have pierced these four ends with the needle, return the needle to the same starting point from which you began, so that the thread retracts from the most superior part of the wound, so that the suture is like a fabric stitch with which the fabric is tightened.
And place the width of a little finger between each stitch.
And this suture is known to men.
And with such a suture, I closed the wound which had befallen a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife.  The wound was a tear larger than a palm, and the intestines had protruded about two palms from the middle area of the intestine, and the rupture was in the middle of the member; and then I reduced that protrusion after the intestines had been outside for twenty-four hours.
And the wound was consolidated in fifteen days or so, and I cured him and he was healed; and afterwards he lived for many years, and recovered his former condition.
And the other physicians had judged that he would not be healed; and by miracle he was, for I did not cure him with ointments, for it was in a place where no ointment or medicine was found, but I placed linen cloths over the wound twice a day and diligently washed it with honeyed water until he was healed.
But I will tell you two kinds of sutures, proper according to the narration of the Word.
And it is that you begin with a suture of the cord, and thread the needle from the outside to the inside.
And so, as the needle penetrates the cord and the same wound, according to its length and the entire body, leave the end of the thread where you have threaded the needle; and make the needle penetrate the other end from the inside to the outside in the other end of the wound.
And when you have made that penetration, then you make that penetrate again in that same end of the wound from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the thread that is in this loop, and make the needle penetrate the other end of the wound from the inside to the outside.
And make penetration with that your penetration with which you make that penetration into the thread at the wound's end, which is on the patient's side, until all of that is penetrated.
And then begin from this same side and stitch that with the thread end on the other side, and pull the needle out of the cord which it penetrated, and then return that into that cord and stitch the thread end that is on the other side with this wound end; and pull that out of the cord which is on his side.
And do that once and again, and do that once again until you sew everything that is outside according to that example.
And the sum of this manner of suture is that you sew with a skin suture, with which you stitch the skins, in such a way that you stitch the thread one time from the end of the cord, and leave that other time until you have completed it.
However, the second type of proper suture, which is also called Gallic, and this is as he and for him recounted, is that you stitch it in a similar way to how some healers stitch it, in such a way that they add each part to its corresponding part on him, as is appropriate by nature; and they apply the end of one to the end of the wound, and this is done according to what I have recounted to you.
You must place the needle into the wound end close to the external part, and make it penetrate into the internal part of the wound, and leave two ends of the thread; and then turn the needle and make it penetrate from outside to inside in two parts of the thread.
And then turn each of those, and make each penetrate from the inside to the outside in two other ends of the wound on the opposite side.
And this manner of suture is better than the common, simple suture, and is the suture of which we previously spoke.
This is the Gallic method.
And yet some may say, the suture of the belly is rectified with two needles, which means you place a thread through each, and begin by inserting the needle from your side, and make it penetrate to the other side, according to a suture that joins the layers of skin equally.
And know that when the rupture is in the middle of the belly, then the suture there is more difficult than in other parts of the belly.
But the judgment of the treatment for plague is the same as for other plagues.
Which means that when you catch the plague early, before the air alters it, and you return the bowel and sew it up properly, then place the flesh-restoring powder on it.
And if the air has already altered the plague, then apply some ointments that are readily available to you until they form a hard scab and the pus drains, and are firm and sealed; and then treat it as you would treat other plagues until it is healed.
But if you don't have the medicines available, then apply this immediately, as soon as it begins to pus, old cloth, and change it twice a day, as you have been taught, until it is healed, for you do not need other medication in many cases when the plagues are simple.
But if you fear that the plague is spreading to the main limbs, then you should soak lion's wool in high-quality olive oil, or scented oil, and place it around the areas between the armpits and below the elbows.
And if you feel pain or putrefaction in their bowels, which often happens, then treat it with black astringent wine, and especially if the inflammation has reached the bowel and has made a penetrating exit into its cavity.
And know that that which is in the large bowel is easier to heal than that which is in the small bowels.
And it is difficult to heal, for the bowel called "jejunum" does not receive healing from any plague that affects it completely; and this is due to the large number of veins in it, and because of its large size within those, and due to the subtlety of its structure, and due to its proximity to the nature of the nerves.
But if the result of the plague is a membrane, and you find it fresh, return it according to your method of returning the bowels as well.
But if it has gone further and has already turned green or black, then you should squeeze it with a cloth on the area where it is discolored, to cause a flow of blood, for there are veins and arteries in the membrane.
And then cut whatever is attached to that ligament, and hang the two ends of the cloth from below the wound to alleviate the pulling of it away from the wound and the expulsion of the plague's branch (or limb).
A reminder of the plagues when they occur in the bowel.
When a rupture occurs in the bowel, and it is small, it is possible that it can be restored in some people.  For example, I saw a man who had been wounded in his belly with a spear thrust, and the plague was on the right side of his stomach.  The plague was old and had become a fistula, and he expelled gas and wind.
Then I considered his treatment, although it wasn't guaranteed to heal him, but I continued to refine it until he was healed and the area was consolidated.
And I, having seen the area healed, inquired about any further ailments in his stomach.  No further ill effects occurred, and he remained in good condition, eating and drinking, and then he entered a bath and was exercised, as prescribed.
And some experienced practitioners have said that when a wound occurs in the bowel, if it is small, it should be bound in this way: you should take ants with large heads;  then close the two edges of the wound, and place one of those ants with an open mouth over the edges of the wound. When it bites and closes its mouth, cut off its head, as it adheres and does not dissolve.
Then place another ant near the first, and do not cease to do this with ants, one after another, according to the size of the entire wound.
And then you compress that and hold it to the wound; and those ant heads remain attached to the bowel until the bowel is healed; and you do no harm to the patient.
And it is also possible to bind the bowel with a fine thread made from the bowel of an animal attached to it, after it is put on a needle;  you take the end of this bowel thread, it should be very thin, then bind the end to the thread, wrapped around it, then insert that thread through needles and, using that needle, compress the bowel and return it to the opening.
And this method of suturing with ants is only a way of trust and hope.
However, if the wound is large and extensive, especially if it is in one of the delicate intestines, for then there is only refinement, and not a complete healing of it.
Of the treatment of abscesses and fistulas.
Know that any wound or abscess, when it grows old and becomes an ulcer, and is not healed and continually discharges pus and is not excised, then, in any limb, it is universally called a fistula and we do not call it an abscess in that case.
And a fistula, in truth, is a hard, white, fibrous swelling, with no pain and a cavity like the cavity of a bird's quill; and therefore that ulcer is called a *rizatz* (or *rizat*), that is, a quill.
And in some people it is moist, discharging pus; and easily the moisture is excised in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is thick, and sometimes thin, and sometimes large, and sometimes small.
I have already shown all those causes, and the signs of them, and the treatment of them with medicines in the division.
And it is necessary that I make you understand the treatment of a fistula that is created through surgical intervention with the hands.
And understand that fistulas occur in all parts of the body; for among them are fistulas that reach large veins and arteries, or nerves, or the spine, or the intestines, or the bladder, or to one of the ribs, or one of the vertebrae of the spine, or to one of the joints, which are compound, such as the hand and foot; and of these are those that occur near the main limb and of similar nature.
Therefore, among these fistulas, which are of this type, are among the illnesses with difficult healing, and do not receive a cure, and dealing with them is effort and ignorance.
And if any of these fistulas does not reach the locations I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its cure, and want to gain knowledge of it, then take a probe of iron or steel, and if the fistula goes in a straight path, you will find it with it.
But if the fistula is winding, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead, for lead, due to the lightness of its body, is easily bent and guided toward the winding.
And if the fistula has many roughnesses, it is not possible for you to mark it with a probe.
Then irrigate the orifices with their orifices, for the moisture through which you irrigate it flows toward the other openings and runs through them.
And then carefully investigate, according to what method is possible for you, to know if there is bone or nerve there, and whether the fistula's cavity is distant or close, or if it is a fistula with many orifices and it is all over that, with the full force of your attention, your questioning, with which you question the patient, and the location of the pain with your hand on the spot, and similar to the meanings and causes that apply to an abscess.
And when you have considered all those points with true judgment, then proceed to treatment according to your knowledge.  Consider whether the fistula is a superficial or near-surface fistula or one in a salty area, distant from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or one of the locations I have mentioned to you.
Then, dry the fistula as previously described, removing the filtering material and corrupted flesh, and whatever has grown from the flesh's opening or from there and similar things, and treat it until healed.
But if the fistula is deep and located, as was mentioned, then it is necessary to cut it, as far as possible for you, and remove all its corrupted flesh.  Then administer some compresses with sharp medicines, and place them inside the fistula's cavity, which you have opened with an iron instrument.
And do this repeatedly until the sharp medicine dissolves everything that remains in the fistula's cavity from the corruption.
Then restore it with ointments that produce healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you cauterize it as we have described.
However, if the fistula's cause is bone, and you are sure of this, then you must incise and cauterize it as we have described, unless you believe that any vein, nerve, or major limb is in the way, as we have mentioned.
But if the bone is exposed and there is some corruption or blackness on it, then you scrape away that corruption completely; and then cure it with something that will create flesh until it is healed.
And if it is not healed and continues to fester as before, then know that you have not achieved complete eradication of the corruption; and then you must once more uncover it, thoroughly excavate and cleanse it with your strongest tools, and then restore it.
And if it is healed, well and good; but if not, then know that this corruption lies deep within the body, a condition that cannot be reached, and thus there is no remedy but to leave it be.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or if you can, you should remove it with fine forceps suitable for this purpose.
And if there are many bones, then meticulously remove all of them, and leave nothing behind with your strength.
But if any moisture causes you difficulty, and there is no fear or mistake in their incision, then incise those ligaments or flesh if present, and free the bones.
But if you are not prepared to remove the bones immediately, then place something on the wound that can keep the wound closed, and leave it until the area around those bones is free of flesh and ligaments, and keep the wound from healing and make the incision you have made firm during your operation.
However, if you fear it, place cotton soaked in oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, or any green ointment on it, because when those ligaments soften, and the bones are separated from them, their removal is easier.
But if it is a large bone, like a leg bone or thigh bone, or similar, and only the surface of it is corrupted, then scrape it with a rasp until that blackness and corruption are removed, and then repair the wound.
But if the corrupted portion is large, and the corruption has already reached the bone marrow, then it is necessary to cut the affected area entirely,  incision by incision, until the corruption is reached, and then treat it until it heals.
And I recommend to you what happened to a man in his thigh, so that you may use it as an example and help for your operation.
There was a young man, around 30 years of age, who experienced pain in his thigh.  And from the internal parts, there was an accumulation of matter within the body until it reached the thigh.  This was a matter of a large abscess, and it was not caused by an external factor.
And then, because of the doctors' error, the condition was allowed to develop over time until the abscess opened, and a large amount of matter came out.  Its treatment was delayed until the leg became fistulaed, and many openings formed which all discharged pus and bodily humours.
And so, many doctors treated him over two years, and none of them had the expertise in the art, until I was called in.
And I saw his leg and the matter flowing from those openings in great quantity; and already the man's body was all wasted away, and his complexion was yellow.
Then I inserted a probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone.
And then I probed all the openings and found them leading to various parts of the leg.
And then I incised one of those openings until I uncovered a portion of the bone, and found it decayed, already turning to earth and blackened; and a portion of the leg was already decaying, having penetrated to the marrow.
And then I closed what I had uncovered of the exposed bone.
And I judged that it was not the bone itself, but that decay which I had cut away and closed, and I had already removed it.
And then I applied restorative medicine to the wound with consolidating remedies for a long time, but it did not heal.
And then I reopened and uncovered the bone a second time, after the first uncovering, and found continuing decay along with the bone.
And I removed what appeared to me as that decay, and then I was forced to perform the healing and restoration of it, but it was not restored.
And then I uncovered it again, and did not cease to cut into the bone little by little, particularly, and forced the restoration of it, but it was not restored until I cut away about a palm's width of it, and extracted the marrow along with it.  Then I restored that area with medicines, and it was slightly consolidated and healed.
And further operations and cutting into it are not necessary, except due to the weakness of the patient, and the small amount of tolerance and fear of death, for in those hours, a harmful fainting spell occurred due to excessive evacuation.
And so, it is healed completely, and nothing is in place of the bone but hard flesh; and its placement within the body is good, and its strength returned; and it has been restored to its normal state, and there is no opposition in its movement or harm in any way.
However, if the bone is prominent in a displaced area of the body, then you should cut it in this way: take a bandage and tie it tightly to the end of that prominent bone, and tell someone to pull it outwards; and place another bandage of thicker wool next to the first bandage; and then bind that over the flesh that is beneath the bone, and extend the ends so as to draw the flesh downwards, and expose the flesh in the area you wish to cut so that the cut doesn't harm the flesh.
And place a piece of wood or a board beneath the bone properly, because when you do that, the cut is not forbidden from not cutting into the corrupted flesh.
And the cutting should be done on the affected area little by little, because if the corruption is within the cavity of the bone and does not appear upon its surface, you may need to cut it again.
And if the bone is corrupted but not protruding, and one part is still joined to the other, with the corruption located in the middle or part of it, then expose all the flesh around it; then place wood underneath, and cut along the first part where the corruption is until the cut reaches the other part.
And the cut should follow the affected area closely, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, cut through that corrupted joint and cut the bone where it is connected to the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then it is not a reasonable cause, but a trick of nature.
And if the corruption is in the flesh of the hand or foot, it is very difficult; however, you must investigate the corruption in whatever way it appears to you, and expose it, and cleanse it according to every possibility you have, and by any means possible, provided it does not damage a vein or nerve.
And know that the instruments for cutting and sawing these bones are very specific to the bones' location, preparation, thickness, subtle differences, size (large or small), hardness, and density; therefore, you must prepare appropriate instruments for every type of operation.

Of incision tips and the sawing of bones.
The extremities may become diseased either due to internal or external causes, or even if you have treated the corruption with remedies and the treatment is unsuccessful, or if the corruption spreads to a limb not initially affected, then you must make an incision on that limb where the corruption has spread so that the patient escapes death or a greater harm/deficiency in that limb.

And similarly, if it affects the hand, then you cut it at the base of the forearm, and do not allow the corruption to spread to the arm's forearm.
And if, instead of that, you find it in the arm, then you cut the arm up to the elbow at the joint.
And if the corruption passes beyond the elbow, then in that case the patient's death is imminent; and applying any other remedy to this is futile.
And similarly, for the foot, if the corruption affects the toe, then you cut it down to one of the joints.
And if it spreads to the sole of the foot, then you cut off the entire foot. But if it rises up to the knee, then you cut the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the corruption has already reached the knee, then there is no cure possible, only resignation, and leaving the patient to their fate.
And the way to cut or sever the limb is that you tighten the ligament in the part just below the place you want to cut, and tighten another ligament above the spot; and the physician stretches a ligament downward, and stretches another ligament upwards; and you separate the flesh that is between the two ligaments with a sharp instrument until all the flesh is exposed; and then it should be cut or severed.
And it is necessary to place linen cloths on all sides so that the area is not touched and that the patient receives a properly formed and pus-filled wound.
But if you encounter blood flow when working, then cauterize the cut area thoroughly, or place some powders that staunch the blood; and then return to your procedure until it is completed.
And then bind the injured limb with suitable bandages, and care for it until it is healed.
And I will now relate to you what happened to a man on his foot, the very same accident which I and you have discussed, where he suffered a dark discoloration on his foot, with a burning that resembled a fire's burning.
And that corruption first appeared on his toe, until it spread to the entire foot.
And then, the man himself, because he saw the cure for the limb with the force of the pain and burning he endured, cut that foot down to the joint, and the wound healed.
For a long time, that same corruption occurred on the finger of his index hand.
