And he urged me to amputate his hand, but I did not want to do this, hoping for the expulsion of that excess fluid, and fearing the amputation of his hand because of death, for the patient's strength was waning.
And after he left me, he returned to his homeland, and it came to my knowledge that that man had himself cut off his entire hand, and he healed.
And I have related this story only so that it might be helpful in understanding this disease, and that it might serve as a sign, by which it may be recognized and by which it may be treated.
Of the treatment of sinuses and the quality of enemas with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in any fleshy member and the abscess cavity extends until it collects pus, and then it bursts and is perforated, and the pus is discharged, and the area remains empty as if it were a vessel, and the skin covering it is like a thin cloth, and it has not progressed to further corruption, and it does not affect the bone, nerves, or ligaments, it is called a sinus and not a fistula.
However, if the cavity extends until the corruption affects a surrounding part, then it is called a fistula, and not a sinus.
And the treatment for it is to cut away that skin completely as it is turned back, especially when it is thin and formed like cloth, and to make certain that it does not adhere to the affected area because of the corruption.
But if you suspect the skin is adhering and that the corruption hasn't spread further, and there's pus draining from the skin, then treat it with an enema.
And if you consider it a sinus, and if it is large, and the discharge is foul-smelling, then administer an enema with Egyptian medicine, which is made of oil, vinegar, and honey in equal parts, mixed in a vessel and heated over a fire until the mixture becomes golden brown and resembles honey.
Then, take what is necessary from this mixture, dissolve it with water and honey; and administer this mixture as an enema to the sinus, and compress the opening, and leave the medicine there for two hours.
Then, remove that (the medicine) with care; and do this for as many days as necessary until the sinus is cleansed and the foul odor has disappeared.
And sometimes, if this medicine is not available, it is treated with water mixed with ashes; and for this, you should take the ashes of a plant, mix water with it, then filter the liquid, and administer it as an enema until you are confident the sinus is relieved.
And if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or ash water, then administer an enema of water mixed with honey and finely ground cumin, or administer an enema of wine and honey mixed, for honey has the property of cleansing and washing, while wine helps to adhere to the sinus, especially when the wine contains an excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and are sure the sinus is healed, then administer an enema that promotes the growth of flesh in that area.
And you should take diaphenolic unguents and dissolve them in rosemary oil and astringent wine; or you could administer enemas with other medications and ointments that have properties I have already written about in the treatise on ointments.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow and the enema tube cannot be inserted, then widen it gradually with a small iron implement, and apply a poultice wrapped in Egyptian or basil ointment until it is widened.
And similarly, if the opening is wide, then you should draw together the edges of the opening with sutures, leaving enough space for the enema tube to pass without difficulty.
And if the opening through which the fistula drains is elevated in the upper part, then dry that area at the lowest point to make the fistula drain downwards, because when the fistula is contained within the depth of the sinus, the flesh will grow there.
And if it is not possible for you to dry the sinus to the lowest bone as desired, then you must prepare the limb with a preparation so that the fistula drains easily as is appropriate, and so that no cause of blockage of the fistula remains.
But that which is over the sinus opening, should remain at all times, so that what is secreted from the fistula is discharged. And so that you may know if what flows from the fistula is much or little, or is digested, or is undigested.
And by this, consider the location of the sinus, if the patient feels pain there, and if there is an abscess there.
And when your treatment is prolonged in this manner, and you see that the matter discharged onto the sinus opening has already adhered, or shows signs of adherence, then on the third day, you detach it, and when you detach the scab, change the cloth to another one and renew the ointment.
But if from the sinus there is a thin, subtle discharge, then do not despair of the adherence of it, for the healing process is appropriate; for some people take a long time for flesh to grow over their wounds, and in others, the opposite occurs.
And if from the sinus a significant amount of undigested discharge has come out for many days, then know that the sinus has not adhered; and if the adherence of the sinus is delayed and the cause is prolonged, then you should adjust the drying effect of the medicines, and if the medicines are moist in their substance and in their virtue, make them dry like a diafinicon ointment, by adding a dry substance such as zegi.
The final treatment, with which it is cured, is to take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it in rosined oil, and then add old, strong wine to it, and mix it thoroughly, and use that.
And similarly in that procedure, when there is no discharge, use diafinicon ointment, honey when it is cooked until it is thickened, and use it; or take myrrh, aloe, and frankincense, grind and powder them, and mix them with honey heated on the fire until thickened, and apply this mixture to a cloth and apply that to the sinus; or apply the thickened honey to a cloth, and apply that to the sinus.
Sometimes it is administered with long, slender roots of a celestial lily (and it is a celestial lily), and the flour of eris and centaury.  Use these simple ingredients, separately or combined, grind them, sift them, and powder them and mix them with the honey previously described; and use it.
And if the sinus is prolonged and not healed by this treatment, then know that the corruption is in the bone, or other hard members or ligaments; then you treat it with fistular treatment, as mentioned above.
Of the treatment of whitlow, and of the bruised nail and the incision of the affected finger, and its sectioning.
Whitlow is an abscess that arises under the nail of the finger of the hand or foot, and commonly arises on other fingers.  When it is prolonged and its treatment is less favorable, or if it is an abscess from a deep-seated abscess or is corrupted, it emits pus until it corrodes the base of the nail, and frequently corrupts the entire nail.  Often, the corruption spreads to the bone until it has a foul odor, the end of the finger is blackened, and the color is dark.
Therefore, when you have done what we have said in the division, and your treatment does not work, then you should cut away all the excess of the nail with iron, and then cauterize the wound with that, for cautery is very helpful in this.
However, if the bone is sound and the nail is healthy, and the nail's additions have already pushed the flesh inward, and they begin to pierce and ulcerate it, then you should place a thin blade under the angle of the nail that is piercing the flesh, and lift it up and cut the flesh easily, and place on what remains of the flesh the burning and corrosive remedies until it is completely removed; and then treat that with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already reached the bone, then you must cut into the bone and remove it, for a corrupted bone will never heal.
And if the corrosion and corruption extend to the finger, then you cut it at one of the joints, according to the prognosis of its recovery, and then treat it until it is healed.
However, if the nail has suffered a blow or friction, and there is intense pain, then you should first bleed the patient, and then dry the nail with a sharp scalpel by making a transverse cut from top to bottom, and be careful not to reach the flesh beneath the nail, for you will cause the patient intense pain and might cause the birth of excess adhering flesh in the area. Then treat that area with whatever alleviates the pain.
But a swollen finger, which is often born in the hands of some people, is quite fleshy, and it is often on the bones, or often on the nails.
And some of them are born at the base of the joint, or their origin is in some of the finger joints. However, the one that is born in some joints of the finger is not harmful, and the one that is born in the joints is quite painful.
And for those that are fleshy growths, the incision should be gentle, and you should cut it along its base with a broad scalpel.
But for the one that is born at the base of the joint, the treatment is difficult; therefore, be careful with its removal.
However, for the one that is born on a finger near the joint, you should first cut through the flesh around it with a circular incision, right down to the bone, then use a suitable saw to cut through the bone, and then treat it until it is healed.
However, when the fingers stick together, either by birth, or due to a wound or burn or similar injuries, you must separate the solidified parts until the fingers return to their natural form.
Then, place cloths soaked in warmed oil between them to prevent them from sticking together easily, and separate them, or insert a thin sheet of lead between them until they have healed properly.
And similarly, if fingers stick together to the palm, then you must separate the adhesion according to what is appropriate and restores the correct shape of the limb.
Of incisions of veins, these are varicose veins.
Varicose veins are swollen, thick veins full of melancholic excess fluids that appear in various parts of the body, most commonly in the legs, and especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and those who carry heavy loads.
Therefore, you should administer purging of the body of black bile several times with strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilic vein.
However, the treatment of them with iron is in two ways: one is that they be incised and that the black blood be drawn out, and the other way is that the vein be elevated and all of it be extracted.
The sectioning of it is done in this way: first, soak the leg in very hot water until the thick, phlegmatic blood in the leg is resolved. Then, tightly bind the patient's leg from the upper thigh area down to below the knee with a bandage.  Next, cut the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision.  Then, elevate the black blood with your hand from the bottom of the leg up and down, until the desired amount of blood has been removed, and this will alleviate the patient's ailment.
And then bandage it, and order the patient to abstain from foods that engender black bile.
And repeat the treatment by phlebotomy when the veins are full and this is noticed by the patient.
But the elevation of them is done in this way: you raise the patient's leg, if it is very swollen, and then put it in a bath and soak the leg with hot water until it turns red and the veins are prominent; or if a bath is not available, it is exercised by vigorous activity until the limb is warmed.
Then cut the cord (likely a vein) directly above the vein by incision along the length, or behind it, below the knee, or further down near the heel.
Then open the cord with forceps and scrape the vein on all sides until it appears sensitive, and it is near the appearance of it becoming discolored, turning towards blackness.
And then, when you drain and from the cord, you see that white, which is the cord.
And then you place a reed underneath it so that it is elevated and pulled away from the cord, and suspend it with a curved forceps.
And then make another incision near that incision after three days, and then scrape the cord on the side above the vein until it appears.
And then lift that and cut it in a back incision near the ankle, and then extract that and lift it until it comes out through the second incision, and then extract it from the incision above it.
And do this until you extract that from the incision that is highest or most elevated of all the incisions, until everything is out; and then cut that.
And if you are unable to extract and elevate it, then insert a needle with a strong double hook and tie it and extract it, or insert a reed under it and turn your hand around until it is thrown out. And be careful not to cut it; for if it is broken, it is difficult for you to raise it much, and it has come upon the patient by that injury.
And when you have completely expelled it, then place on the wound area wool soaked in wine and rubbed oil and unrubbed oil, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the vein is constricted, having a tortuousness to which it is wrapped in parts, and is not straight as we have said, then it is necessary that you cut along that in every part of its tortuosity and in the places where it is wrapped, and then suspend it with hooks until you raise the entire thing.
And be careful in your incision not to cut the vein and not to injure it, for it will be difficult for you to raise it; and use your prudence in this.
Form of the incision with which a vein is cut:
It is not so sharp as the hooks, nor does it have a sharp tip, so as not to injure the vein; and it is of considerable flexibility, like a lion's, for if it were subtle, it would cut the vein by its subtlety; however, it has an appropriate thickness, as we have said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in warm lands, like in the land of the Arabs and in the hot, arid lands of India, where little vegetation grows.
And it is easily generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from putrefaction that comes from under the skin, like serpents, worms, and grubs that breed between the skin and the flesh.
A sign of the onset of this condition is that a vehement inflammation occurs in the leg's vein; and soon the area becomes blistered; and soon the vein begins to emerge from that blister, like the root of a plant.
And when the extremity of it appears, then you should wrap a lead weight, which is the weight of a drachma, or two drachmas, around that.  Bind that and let the lead remain hanging from the leg.
And as many times as any cause of this is found on the external parts, wrap that in lead and bind it. And if the condition is prolonged, then you should incise a part of it and bandage the remainder; and do not incise it at the root until it has come out, for if you incise it at the root, it contracts and enters the flesh, causing an abscess and suppuration at the site, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is expelled from behind it and no part of it remains in the body.
And from this vein have come out in some men to a length of five palms and ten fingers, and it has already come to my attention that one of twenty palms has emerged.

And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or on the foot, and it has many openings, and a branch emerges from each opening.
And therefore treat it in the same way as we have stated in the division and with what has been previously stated.
Of the incision of worms generated under the skin, and it is called bovine illness.
This illness is called bovine illness in some lands near us, because it often affects livestock, and is a small worm that is born between the skin and flesh, and moves throughout the body, up and down, and is felt in its movement from limb to limb until it bursts the skin and comes out.
And its generation is due to the putrefaction of certain humours, just as worms, serpents, and lombrics and ascarids generate in the belly.
And it is feared because of its malice, for when it moves through the body and ascends to the head, and reaches the eye, it often causes intense inflammation there, entering and destroying the eye.
And therefore, when you want to cure it and extract it, it can only be done by following its movement and appearance as felt, and then you must compress what is above it and what is below it with strong binding and constriction, and then cut over it and extract it.

Of a section concerning illness of the nose.
This illness is called "nafir" in our land, and it is a pain that comes to certain parts of the body, and then it moves from one part to another.
And I have already seen that, as I will tell you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I examined her arm and saw a small inflammation in the vein of the arm, and after staying there for an hour, I saw that inflammation move like a worm climbing, but more quickly than expected, as if it were living silver moving from place to place.
And then the pain moved from that location and settled in the muscle, and there it remained for an hour.
And then it moved to the other side of the body until it reached the other arm.
And then I noticed it moving throughout her entire body as I watched; and I was very amazed by its movement from one limb to another. And I had previously seen things like that with my own eyes, however, I have seen many cases where pain moves from one limb to another. But I did not see it with my eyes in such a way as in the woman.
And I did not consider it to be anything else than that because the woman was old, with dry flesh and exposed veins, and in this appearance the moving air was felt.
And it is necessary that it not appear in this way in those who are delicate, with moist flesh and hidden veins.
And if you want to cure him, and the patient feels that pain, then it does not appear to you visually in the way we have said, then you gently and slightly press over it until that compressed wind is released, and cauterize the area.
But if you do not see it with your eyes, then you treat it with expectorants and with what expels wind, and treat it with stinky pills and pills of Serapion and the like.
Of the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diverse according to their types and the body parts they are lodged in, because their diversity is that some have large points, and small ones, and concave and blunt ones, which are solid; and others have three angles, and those with tongues; and those with feathers.
And the signs of the brain when an arrow lodges in it, and pierces the bone, and is exited from the skull which is over the brain, are sudden violent pain, blindness, dizziness, redness in the eyes, inflammation and redness of the tongue, spasms and confusion of thought and vomiting of bile, and the speech is badly affected and the voice is destroyed, and white humidity resembling dust emerges from the wound site, and the area around it resembles the watery state of the flesh.
And therefore, if these signs appear to you, then you abstain from treatment and from extracting the arrow, unless it is on the surface.
But the signs of the arrow when it lodges in the heart, is that it is near the left nipple, and feels as if it were stuck in a hard thing, and not a hollow thing; and the movement of the arrow is very much like the pulse's movement, and black blood flows from the wound, and there is coldness in the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting; and therefore know that death descends without any doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung are the expulsion of foamy blood from the wound, and the vessels close to the neck are inflamed, and the patient's color has changed, and they exhale large amounts of air, and crave drawing in cool air.
And if the arrow strikes the membrane which is in the foot, the pain is then near the small ribs, and great distress is caused with violent pain and sighing, and all the muscles in the limbs are affected.
And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then soon undigested food will come from the wound.
And if the arrow strikes the belly and lodges there, a rupture of the wound, or of the tissue, or intestine, will appear.  There is no hope for its cure, even with the removal of the arrow.
And if the arrow strikes the bladder, and urine leaks from the wound, and some cause of this emission spreads to external parts, and severe pain quickly afflicts the patient, then know that it is fatal.
However, the other limbs, such as the face, neck, throat, shoulders, the supporting structures, the vertebrae of the back, the collarbone, the thigh, the leg and their counterparts among the limbs, are saved according to the greatest possible dispositions when the arrow does not hit a vein or artery, and if it is not a poisoned arrow.
And I tell you this, what I have seen concerning these arrows, so that you might understand it for your own treatment.
Which is why, when an arrow strikes a person in the tear duct of their eye, near the base of the nose, and I removed it from the other side, below the ear, he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I removed another arrow from a Jew, which struck him in the area of his eye, below the lower eyelid, and the arrow was already embedded; only a small tip, which adhered to its shaft, was retrieved. It was a large, sharp arrow, typical of the Turkish ones, with a lion's head design on the shaft, and no barb.
And the Jew was healed, and no ill effects occurred in his eye.
And I removed another arrow from a Christian man, which had lodged in his throat.  It was an Arabic arrow, with barbs; I then cut around it between two guides, as it was already embedded in his throat.  I carefully extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
And I removed another arrow from a man, which had lodged in his stomach, and I thought he would die from it.  However, after thirty days had passed, and no change in his condition had occurred, I cut around the arrow and carefully extracted it. He was healed, and no ill effects resulted.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow lodged in his back, and the wound healed.  But after nearly seven years, the arrow emerged from the root of his thigh.
And I saw a woman in whom an arrow lodged in her stomach, and the wound healed within her.  There was no change in her condition, and the woman suffered no harm in any of her natural bodily functions.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow lodged in his face, and the wound healed and the arrow remained.  Finding little pain, he resembled the others.
And I extracted the arrow from the King's prince, which had lodged in the middle of his nose, and had already slightly deviated to the right side, with the entire arrow hidden.
And I was called to treat him shortly after the arrow's lodging, within three days.
And then I found the arrow wound very tight; and then I probed it with a fine probe, and felt nothing; but he felt a prick beneath his right ear, and then I suspected that this prick was caused by the arrow's tip.
And I applied a plaster to the area, one with attractive and digestive properties, confident that the area would become suppurated so that the arrow's position would become apparent, and that the wound would dry over it.
And there was no significant subsequent wound reaction that the arrow had reached that spot; and I continued with the plaster on it for many days and there was no further incident; and meanwhile the wound healed, and the patient remained free of pain from the extraction for many days, until one day he felt the arrow within his nose and reported this to me.
And then I applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days until it opened, and with the probe I examined it, and found the fine arrowhead, which adhered to the shaft.
And then I continued treating the wound with that sharp medicine until the arrowhead became visibly apparent to me, and I spent time with this for a period of approximately four months.
And then, because the wound had become inflamed, and it was possible for me to insert the forceps into it, and it remained, and did not yield, then I continued to devise and experiment with various techniques, until one day I managed to use skillfully crafted forceps, as described in the latter part of this chapter, to extract it; and then I healed that wound.
Some physicians judged that the cartilage of the nose could not be restored, but I restored it, and the wound healed completely, and the patient was completely healthy and suffered no ill effects from it.
And I recount to you the method of extracting some arrows, so that you may regard this as a rule and significance of what I have said, because the parts of this art and the definitions of it are not contained within this narration and are not encompassed by the book.
And the skilled craftsman compares the little with the much; and with what is present with what is absent; and finds a new operation and a new instrument for removing foreign objects from the art when it happens.
Therefore, I say that arrows are not pulled from the limbs into which they are shot except in two ways: either by pulling from the location where they enter; or by pushing from the other side.
And that which is driven out from the place where it entered, or is an arrow lodged in a fleshy area, is pulled out and driven out.
And if you do not follow this procedure at the time of extraction, then you must leave it for several days until the surrounding flesh can heal, and the pulling of it and its removal is relieved.
And similarly, if it is lodged in bone and you do not follow the procedure, then leave it for several days and repeat the pulling and movement each day, and it will be driven out.
And if you do not follow the procedure for a few days, then you must pierce the area surrounding the arrow in that bone from all sides with a fine drill until the space around the arrow is enlarged, and then remove it.
But if the arrow is hidden within the body, and cannot be felt, then you must search for it with probes, and if you feel it, then pull it out using appropriate tools for extraction.
And if you cannot reach it due to the tightness of the wound and the length of the shaft, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in the way, then you must enlarge the wound until it is sufficiently widened and the arrow can be extracted.
And if it is held by its ear-like parts, then you must carefully detach the flesh it is lodged in, using every skill possible.  If you cannot release the flesh from the fracture or twisting of the ear-like parts, then manipulate them until they yield.

And if you cannot remove it immediately, then leave it for several days until the surrounding tissues can heal, and then try again once it has softened. But if you encounter excessive bleeding, then apply the treatments described in the relevant chapter on healing.

Of bloodletting from the veins.
The veins for bloodletting, of which there is a custom of bloodletting, are as follows:  from chapter XVI, there are two pulsating veins near the ears, called *alhasisain*; two veins in the two apparent temples; two veins in the two tear ducts, called *aspicientz*; the vein in the middle of the forehead; the vein at the end of the nose; two veins at the neck; two veins on the upper lip; two veins on the lower lip; these are called *algelic*; and two veins below the tongue. But the veins that are bled in the arms are five: one is the cephalic, situated towards the wild side; the people call it "the vein of the head"; it is a superficial vein, and is composed of a branch of the basilica and another branch of the cephalic; the people call that one "vein of the whole body."  The basilica is the vein located on the domestic side, called "citillar," and the common people call it "the vein of the belly."  The vein of the arm, located on the forearm, is the one from which blood is drawn, and is the one that appears on the elbow by manifest appearance.  There is also the *salvatella*, the vein located between the aforementioned radial vein and the little finger, in the two hollows.
And in the foot and leg there are three veins: one is located below the bend of the knee on the wild side; the second is *sophena*, and its location is below the leg on the domestic side; the third is the sciatic vein, and its location is below the leg on the wild side; and there are other similar veins in the leg.
The treatment of veins near the ears is for headaches, migraines, inflammation, and other bad chronic head ulcers.
And the method of drawing blood from those veins, as I recommend to you, is to shave the patient's head, and to rub the back of the patient's head, at the location of both veins, with a wide cloth using good friction; then, have the patient compress their neck with a bandage until the two points of the veins near the ears appear in two indented places on the head; then, you should find them with your finger, and if you feel the pulse of those veins with your finger, then you should bleed them with a lancet.
Then take the lancet knife, which is sharp, and carefully insert it under the vein into the groove until the bleeding reaches the bone. Then raise your hand with the vein and groove to the upper part and make a sharp incision into the vein. And make the incision about the length of two fingers joined together, or thereabouts; and let the blood flow out as much as you desire.
And then press with cloths and leave it until it is healed.
And already in the beginning of the book, the incision of these things and their cauterization has been described.
However, in the case of the two arterial veins, which are in the temples, bloodletting is beneficial for chronic migraines, persistent eye inflammation, and the expulsion of acute excesses that have accumulated in the head.
And the technique for bloodletting those veins, as I recommend to you, is to press the patient's head with your hand until the two veins become clearly visible, and the pulse of them is felt under your finger; then you should apply a tourniquet.
And then you raise the skin over the vein, along the upper parts, with your index finger, and insert the lancet under it, raising the vein up; and cut it, just as you did with the other two veins; and let the blood flow according to the amount you need.
And then loosen the patient's collar, place your finger on the vein for an hour, then apply a poultice and powders and tightly compress that area, and leave it until it is healed.
And the recollection of them, and their incision, and their extraction, has already been described at the beginning of the book.
Therefore, bloodletting from the vein of the forehead is suitable for bloodletting from the cephalic vein for old diseases of the face, migraines, stiffness, and foul ulcers.

And the benefit of the phlebotomy for him is for hip pain when it is caused by hot blood.
And the procedure for the phlebotomy is that the patient enters a bath, and tightly wraps the thigh from the wide area above the heel, for four days, with a long, thin bandage, for it only appears then.
And therefore, when it appears, then you perform phlebotomy on that vein according to the best method available to you, and in a transverse direction;  either cut it by incision, or sever it by section, since the area is inflamed.
And it is quite hidden in many men; and if you find it, and it doesn't appear on the surface, then you should phlebotomize a branch of it, which is the one that appears on the back of the foot, near the aforementioned auricular and annular veins.
And be careful of the nerves, and draw out as much blood as you desire.
And then release the constriction, and place a compress over the phlebotomy site, and bind the area tightly, for it heals quickly.
Of cupping and the qualities of using them.
Cupping is done with horns, or with earthenware, or with glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one of them is with scarification and the extraction of blood, and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping without scarification is in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And the cupping that is administered with scarification and blood extraction is applied to fourteen places on the body, of which one is at the nape of the neck, and is the back part of the head; and cupping in the middle of the two sides of the neck together; and cupping of the chin below the jaw, and below the mouth; and cupping of the two shoulder blades; and cupping of the armpits, and it is above the end of the breast; and cupping of the two elbows, and it is of the two arms; and cupping of the two thighs; and cupping of the two calves.
And cupping does not extract blood from the large veins, but from the small scattered veins in the flesh, and therefore it does not destroy the strength through them, as it does with phlebotomy; nor should cupping with or without fire be done for any illness caused by fullness, until the entire body has been emptied.
But if necessity compels cupping for illness or for a continuous condition, let it be administered at any time, at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, in the middle of it, and at any time.
And therefore, when any of these conditions occur, and especially if it is in the middle or third of the month, then we command cupping to be performed after two or three hours have passed.
And the benefit of cupping the neck is that when it relieves heaviness in the head and that which is pressing on the eyes; however, this should be done as part of the evacuation of the entire body.
And this cupping is in the location of the vein-letting of the cephalic vein.
And it is not permitted to use it on those who are cold and feverish, or in whom there is a cold, for it causes them great harm.
And therefore, it is not appropriate to use it on the elderly, and on those whose heads are afflicted with cold illnesses.
But for those on whom it is continuously applied, oblivion is caused; and therefore, you must instruct the cupper to gently lower the cupping jar to the lower parts, fearing the generation of oblivion.
But cupping with alcohol is in the location of vein-letting of the *vena fusca* and the *basilica*: and therefore, it is helpful for asthma, and the constriction of the breath and the tearing of the breath's instrument, and for coughs, and congestion.
And the alcohol cupping should be lifted slightly, for if it is placed too low, it generates weakness in the body and stomach.
And cupping with *alhagdahan* is helpful for accidental head pains, and ophthalmia, and migraine, and feverishness, and pains in the roots of the teeth; and it is a substitute for vein-letting from the *basilica*.
And you must instruct the cupper not to press their hand with excessive force during the scarification so as not to cut the artery and cause excessive bleeding.
And cupping under the chin is helpful for swelling in the mouth, and for corruption of the gums, and for similar mouth ailments, and it is a substitute for vein-letting of the *aliheric* veins found in the two lips.
But cupping done over the two shoulder blades is helpful for *caputanum* (a condition), which is caused by an excess of heat.
And cupping on the two sides within the armpits is helpful as a substitute for vein-letting from three veins, namely the basilica, the *fusca*, and the cephalic, because it draws blood from all the smaller veins in the flesh, and the smaller veins draw from the larger veins until the drawing effect is transmitted to the three main veins.
And instruct the cupper not to press too hard during the scarification, because the area is lacking in flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
And a cupping treatment applied to the buttocks is helpful for hemorrhoids of the anus and for ulcers underneath.
And it is necessary to instruct the cupper to use a large, deep cupping glass, because the area needs a strong suction; and shallow, delicate cupping glasses are ineffective and should be avoided.
However, cupping on the legs is less effective, due to its obvious limitations, because they draw blood from the entire body, and help with long-standing pains in the kidneys and the bladder; and they provoke menstruation, and are useful for aching and inflammation; and they are a substitute for bloodletting from the two *sophenas* (possibly calves), and for leeches, and for two cautery irons.
However, they greatly weaken the entire body and cause fainting in many people.
And the benefit of cupping [applied to the body in this manner] is similar to the benefit of cupping both legs.
And the method of applying the cupping is to place the empty cupping glass initially and draw it gently with moderate suction, and do not prolong the application of the cupping glasses; rather, apply them lightly, leaving them briefly, so that the humours come to the area by flowing inward. And do not stop repeating this process and continuing until you see the area become red and swollen and the redness of the blood appears.
And then you make incisions and repeat the suction gradually, and then consider the position of the body parts.
For someone with delicate flesh who is easily bruised, you should make incisions only once, and no more, so that the area is not ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to deepen the incision slightly and to moderate the suction with ease.
And if the blood is thick, then you should make incisions twice, because in the first instance, you open a path for the blood and its watery elements to drain out. But in the second, you examine the extracted thick blood.
And if the blood is very thick and foul, then a third incision should be made to remove the remaining impurities.
And finally, when we want to remove little blood gradually, a single incision is sufficient.
And if we want to extract a lot of blood, we make many incisions; and if we know the blood is thick, then it is necessary to make deep incisions.
And the depth of the incision should only be the length of the lancet's blade.
And if the skin where the cupping is to be applied is thick, hard, dark, and its pores are tight, then the places of the cupping should be anointed with softening, opening, and dissolving agents; if it is summer, like almond oil, violet oil, soft chamomile oil, or poppy seed oil; and if it is winter, then narcissus oil, lily oil, chamomile oil, or blood-root oil, and similar ones.
And if the excess is thick and cold, then the oil should be amaranth oil, balm oil, almond oil, or dill oil, and similar ones.
But if the cupping is on a surface with wide pores and tender flesh, then it is necessary to avoid oil, and the areas for cupping should be washed with lotions or cold spring water, or poppy-seed water, or water of the borage, and similar things.
And if the blood is excessively moist, wash the cupping areas with sour wine, or with water of myrtle, sumac, and similar things.
And for those with thick superfluities, wash their cupping areas with old wine, or with water of St. John's Wort (likely a reference to a specific herb), or with a decoction of dill, or chamomile, and similar things.
And the cupping should be done before a bath;  afterwards, it should be administered shortly after the bath for an hour or two, and the patient should rest/sleep soon after the cupping.
What is it that should be regulated before cupping or bloodletting?
You must consider at the outset: if the person being cupped or bled is choleric and has sharp, inflamed blood, then they should take things that cool it, such as  mild vinegar, endive in sour wine, lettuce, drying herbs, and similar remedies.
And give them foods like poultry, and lean meats, and foods seasoned with vinegar, and similar things.
And for someone whose temperament is cold, they should drink  almond syrup, or syrup of fennel seeds, and sumac, and very fragrant, slightly aged wine.  They should be advised to consume little food and drink, and given foods like poultry, hemp seeds, small birds, and pigeons.
And on the day of cupping and bloodletting, the drink should be more plentiful than the food.
And certain men should be given a drink of theriac, or musk, or other similar medicines, before cupping or bloodletting, or soon after, to strengthen the main body parts and to make the blood more refined;  however, these shouldn't be taken hot.
But cupping, done without incision, should be applied to the liver, spleen, gouty areas, the navel, the kneecaps, and the groin area, for these parts cannot withstand incisions.
And this is not done for the purpose of blood extraction from the limb, but rather to draw out cold, stagnant air from the limb.  It's like applying a cupping to a breast in cases of nosebleeds; we use it to dissolve the cold, stagnant air from a limb, just as we do on the belly and navel.  Because this makes the limb less dense, warms it, and relieves the pain by removing the trapped air.
And it is placed on the temples when they are congested or inflamed, because through its strong drawing power, it quickly relieves congestion and eradicates inflammation from the area.
And similarly, it does the same when placed on the liver and spleen to remove the stagnant air contained within them.
And these cupping vessels are administered empty, only with suction; and sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of warm water in illnesses of the spleen.
And this is so that the cupping vessel is filled and becomes larger; it is either just warm water, or water in which herbs suitable for the purpose have been boiled.
The form of cupping administered with fire: let the opening be the width of two fingers, as we have formed it; and the quantity of the opening be half a palm; and it should be on the patient's back, in the location that is on the circumference, half of it, and the hole should be small, according to the size of a needle that could enter it.
Let it be made of red or citrus wood, with thick borders, lion, eagle, or triangular shapes, so that it does not move the limb from its position on it.
And there should be a transverse rod of wood or iron in the middle, where a candle with fire is placed.
And let this cupping be larger than the one we have described; and the smaller cupping be smaller than that, according to the illnesses and age of the person using it.
And the cupping for children and the weakened are different from the cupping for young people and those with robust bodies.
And the method of placing this cupping with fire onto the limb is to gently heat the surface with wood fire in front of a small wax candle, and place that onto the middle of the transverse rod in the middle of the cupping, so that the fire is ignited around the lower part, to prevent burning the patient's body.
And then it should be placed on the patient's limb, and the finger should be on the hole we mentioned, until, when we have the cupping and don't want the finger to expose the hole and the vapor to escape, the cupping will then quickly dissolve.
And heat the surface according to its method, and repeat if you need to.
But in cupping administered for pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse rod nor hole, and it is administered only by filling it with water, and it is placed directly onto the limb.
And this is the manner of it:  The larger this cupping is, the better, so that it can receive a large quantity of water.
Chapter 97.
Leeches are not administered to many limbs, but only to those limbs where cupping is impossible due to their small size, like the foot and gums and similar parts; or to limbs deprived of flesh, like the finger and nose and their similar parts.
And the quality of using those leeches is found in the clear, still waters of muddy places.
And then they [the leeches] should be left in fresh water day and night until they are hungry and no cause remains in their bellies; and then the body should be evacuated at the start with bloodletting and cupping; and then the limb of the sick person should be rubbed until it turns red; and then they [the leeches] should be placed upon it.
And when they are full and drop off.
And if suction of the area is possible with a cupping glass, it is better in terms of healing.
And if not, then wash the area with vinegar and afterwards with plenty of water; and let it be rubbed and squeezed.
And if the body of blood around the attachment of the leeches, and it is oozing, then you immerse a linen cloth in cold water, and place it on top until the oozing is stopped.
And if the blood is excessive, then sprinkle powdered vitriol, or similar astringent substances, on it until the blood is stopped; or place roasted bean halves over the area, and leave them until the beans adhere to the area, and the bleeding is stopped.
And if necessary, repeat the application of leeches, when possible changing them.
And if the leeches are prohibited from being used, then the area should be rubbed with fresh blood or a needle should be used to prick the area until blood flows; and afterwards, poultices should be applied, for when they sense blood, they immediately attach.
And when you want them to detach, sprinkle a little powdered aloes, or salt, or ash on them, and they will detach quickly.
Thus begins the treatment of the third part: of restoration. This chapter is very often necessary in the art of medicine; and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations that accidentally occur in the bones.
O, son, understand that foolish and vulgar doctors, and those who have never looked at the books of the Ancients or read a letter from them, boast of this chapter.
And for this reason, this method of the learned ones in our land is hidden; and I have not forgotten this at any time completely; and I have not embraced this that I have learned, except through the length of reading the books of the Ancients and studying their understanding, until I extracted their knowledge.
And now I will direct myself to experiment and study for the length of my life.
And I have already described in this chapter everything that contains my knowledge and upon which my experience has been passed; since I have shortened it for you and delivered it from the occupation of elongation; and I have shortened it at the end of the shortening; and I have shown it at the end of the explanation; and I have formed in it many forms of the instruments that are administered in it, as is done by adding explanation, just as I have done in the two previous chapters.
First Chapter.
A summary and collection of the nature of bone fractures, and the prerequisites needed before we begin to remember the fractured members and their various conditions. Therefore, let us begin this chapter with a summary of the subject matter; and the chapters to which the craftsman is first obliged to pay attention, and to establish himself upon the truth of those; that is, you and those who are eager to learn or acquire this noble art, from you.
And thus I say that when a fracture, dislocation, twisting, or injury occurs, then it is necessary that you first use phlebotomy or leeches, or both together, if no cause prohibits it, such as weakness of constitution, or if the affected person is a child, or an elderly person in decline, or if it is a time of intense heat or intense cold.
And thereafter, the patient's diet should consist of fresh herbs and poultry and game meats; and avoid fatty meats and foods that fill the veins with blood, until you are certain of a mature abscess, and if you do not fear an effusion of matter into the limb.
And then, return the patient to his former regimen, which was his usual way of life.
And therefore, once the broken bone begins to be restored, then the patient should be given nourishing foods rich in nutrition, thick and heavy, in which viscosity is present, such as foods made of cooked wheat with water, and rice, and porridge, and pea soup, and veal stomachs, and eggs, and fresh fish, and thick wines and similar to these, and with this regimen, the fracture is more easily and better consolidated.
And know that broken bones in people who are in the prime of life and in the elderly, cannot be joined together and consolidated according to their nature because of the wasting away of their limbs.
But nevertheless, nature gathers and unites around the broken bone from every side some substance similar to glue, which is thickened and adheres and holds it together until it is entirely glued and joined, until it reaches the point of strength and firmness, just as with a fire at the beginning, until some factor prevents it from completing its work.
And for this reason, it is necessary that you give the patient foods that are heavy, viscous, and thick, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the variety of limbs, for a fracture of the leg bone is different from a fracture of the head bone; and a fracture of the foot bone is different from a fracture of the arm bone; and similarly, all other limbs have different types of fractures among them.
And I come with a recollection of every type of fracture, explained in its own distinct chapter.
There is a diverse range of fractures within itself.  (Or,  A diverse variety of fractures exists.) And the types of bone fractures themselves are diverse; for there is an arched fracture, and some that occur in a star-like pattern; and sometimes a fracture occurs along the bone's length: and these are fractures of the bone's shaft and splinters and resulting and non-resulting (or proceeding and non-proceeding) splinters, just as is done in a pen when it is prepared for writing, and there are fractures with a gap and a break in the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And for every type, there is a particular method or treatment in restoring it, according to what is remembered in its distinct places (or, sections/locations).
A certain sign of a bone fracture. And by this a bone fracture is known: it is the twisting of it, and the prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and this is of it when you press it with your hand.
Therefore, when there is no apparent twisting in the location, nor is there a feeling of it when you touch the bone with pressure, and the patient does not experience significant pain, then there is no fracture here.
However, it is possible that there is a twist or a small crack, so you should not try to move it with your hand and a total compression. Instead, put some medicine on the affected area, remembering the type that is appropriate for this specific moment and place; and then it should be tightly bound with a subtle bandage.
And know that when the affected area is curved but not fractured in two or otherwise separated from its original alignment,  then you must begin, as soon as possible, to straighten it, lest a painful abscess occur.
And if an abscess forms there, then leave it for several days until the abscess subsides; and then bandage it using any method that you can to provide gentle and ingenious support.
And know that the restoration and reduction of a dislocated joint is easier than a fractured bone.
And then hold it firmly according to the instructions for its repair.
However, if there is a fracture, then extension of the broken limb, be it an arm or a leg, is necessary.  Use your hand if the limb is small, or two ropes, or one rope and a hand.
And your position in which you place the limb back into its natural shape, while the fractured parts of the limb are extended, will require you to skillfully and by any means you can, return those fragments to their proper places.
And be careful, using your strength, that your treatment of the patient does not cause further pain or injury; and strive, to the best of your ability, to fit the bone with its corresponding companion according to their best forms.
And it is necessary that you, skillfully, touch the affected area with your hand, and if you find any other cause, correct it and put it back into place to the best of your ability.
Avoid excessive stretching and compression, as some foolish doctors, often, cause boils or contractions in the limb through their treatment, as we have seen happen frequently in their practices.
The appropriate binding of the still limb is what I recommend; so that you know that broken limbs vary in size, shape and form.
And for those that are small, like the arm, finger, and forearm, a very fine cloth wrap is needed.
However, for larger ones like the thigh or leg, a strong, firm bandage is necessary, because a strong bandage binds the large limb tightly and evenly, so that nothing enters it.
And then, begin your treatment by applying a suitable bandage to the fracture site with three or four wraps, according to the limb's needs; and tighten your grip on the bandage slightly.
Then, take it (the bandage) to the highest point of the fracture site, and tighten it less than you tightened the fracture site. Then, gently lengthen the bandage at the fracture site a little, and loosen the tightening gradually, until you have drawn a substantial part of the bone back into place.
And then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken place with a bandage, and then bring the wraps to the lowest part of the fracture; and let your operation be in the tightening of the bandage and in the loosening of it, just as we have said in the first bandage above.
And then place between the wraps a piece of linen or cloth, with which the twist of the fracture is covered, if there is a twist; and if not, do not place anything there.
And then place another bandage over this, and quickly cover these properly made bandages, if there is no inflammation or a painful abscess in the area. And if there is, place on it what will calm the abscess and reduce the inflammation; and leave it for a few days; and then you tighten the bandages over it.
And let the splints be of the half of a cane, carefully shaped; or of pine wood; or of palm fronds; and let the width of each splint be according to this form and this figure:
However, it is necessary that the splint placed over the fracture be slightly thicker and wider than the other splints, and the length of the splint according to the size and smallness of the limb; and then tighten the splints with another bandage, according to your first tightening; and then tie it over with thread properly, as we said about the tightening, and the tightening should be according to the location of the fracture, and the further it extends from the fracture, the looser the tightening should be.
And the threads should be medium in thickness and thinness, and they should be of flax; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen from the operation of some fools who use their thick cords, it is a great mistake, when the tightness is unequal.
And likewise, thin threads are not suitable, for you will not be able to achieve the desired tightness with those, and there should be at least a finger's width between splint and splint.
And if the patient is affected by the ends of the splints near the tight areas, then place under those some soft material, such as wool or carded wool, until it no longer causes him injury.
Sometimes a fracture is accompanied by a wound, and an accidental rupture occurs in the bone, and I will return to that in the second chapter on the subject of singular occurrences.
And know that not every broken limb should be tightly splinted on the first day; the reason for this is that when the limb is large, splints should not be applied until approximately five or seven days, or more, depending on your confidence that suppuration will not occur.
And this is the method of plasters with which the Ancients repaired fractures, dislocations, and twisting injuries.
A common method of plaster for fracture repair, suitable for many complexions, particularly women and children, because it doesn't generate heat or cold:  Take powdered flour, specifically the fine flour-dust that collects on the walls of a flour mill.
And apply this as it is, but first sift it with egg white, and make the mixture not thick or thin; then use it.
Another method of plaster for repairing fractures, dislocations, and twisting injuries: a recipe using asafoetida, ladanum, acacia, enula, and wild millet root (probably a type of ground herb), all 1/4 ounce each; myrrh and aloe, 1/8 ounce each; and aloes, 1/2 ounce. Grind and sift all these ingredients, then mix them with either honeyed water or egg white if the patient's constitution is warm.  Use this plaster, as it is well-composed and helps mend slightly broken bones, and is helpful to many people.
Another method of plaster for fractures and twisting injuries:  a recipe using wild millet root (likely a type of ground herb) and white marshmallow root, 1/4 ounce each; myrrh and aloe, 1/8 ounce each; acacia, 1/6 ounce; and Armenian bole (a type of clay), 1/2 ounce.
All are ground in a mortar with thorough grinding, then sifted, and mixed with water or egg white, and applied.
A method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places, and for pain, fissures, and fractures in those areas: take wool, soak it in vinegar and cooked olive oil, and apply it to the affected area.
However, this plaster does not have the virtue of repair; it is good for a painful abscess and effectively relieves pain.
Another method of plaster that repairs a broken bone: take leaves of prickly fig and leaves of wild poppy; grind them together and make a plaster from them.
A brief method of plaster for use in the repair of a fractured bone, and when you want to resolve the remaining abscess: take roots of marshmallow, chamomile, and violet flowers, and flour of yarrow; grind them all separately, then mix them together and add wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, then you must mix it with moist coriander water or water and use that.
Another, stronger plaster for resolving a stubborn abscess during the repair of a limb:  take roots of marshmallow, flaxseed, mullein, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, all separately ground, mix them together; all are to be mixed with willow water, or plain water, or wine,  according to the condition of the limb and the color/tone of its skin.
However, Hippocrates, in his book, does not say that a plaster should be placed on a fractured limb during its repair, but rather a cerate made from wax and oil, and nothing else; and recommends that it be of a medium thickness, neither too thick nor too thin.
However, in Gaul, it is observed that plasters are placed on fractured limbs during their repair, and those cases requiring desiccation with mild warmth, such as myrrh, frankincense, and similar to them, of those substances we have mentioned.
A plaster for injuries and pain: take equal parts of linseed, human skin (from a skinned animal, or bird skin), marshmallow, and salt; grind them together, sieve them, and make a plaster.
However, the length of time the bandage should remain in place, and when it should be removed, depends on whether pain, swelling, or displacement of the bone is evident.  If none of these occur, the bandage should not be removed for many days. And if there is intense pain, swelling, or inflammation at the site, then you must unbind or remove it immediately, and remove the plaster.
And take linen cloth and a wet marine sponge, immerse it in lukewarm water, and with this wash the affected area until the swelling subsides and the pain is relieved.  Then let the limb rest for an hour, and afterwards apply a layer of wool soaked in sour wine and oil (or olive oil), and bandage it for a night until you are certain that any inflammatory abscess is gone, the limb’s inflammation is reduced, and the pain has subsided.
And then reapply that subtle bandage and small plaster, but do not use the same amount of pressure as before.  Treat it gently until it is healed.
And if, at times, the abscess, soreness, pain, inflammation, and all other symptoms have completely disappeared, and you no longer require plaster and bandages, then you can reapply them in the same way as you did at the beginning.
And if no symptoms like those we have mentioned appear on the limb, then do not remove the dressings for three, four, five, or seven days; and sometimes all of this is left in place for twenty days, according to your assessment of the limb's condition, as we have said, until the fracture heals and the flesh around it is properly united.
And then adjust the bandage more loosely than your initial bandage, and adjust the patient's diet, according to the patient's recovery.
But if you find the fracture site has dried out and weakened more than necessary, then understand that nourishment is prevented from reaching it.  Therefore, moisten it with warm water every time you remove the dressing, and continue for three days.  Loosen the bandage slightly, and through this process, nourishment flows back to the limb, and it heals quickly.
However, what those foolish bone setters do when a limb fracture is not properly set at first, and they set it crookedly, is an error in their technique, a great danger.  If they were correct, the ancients would have described this in their books, and they would not have used such a method.
And I have not seen anyone of them do this, therefore it is more reasonable that it should not be done.
Second Chapter: Concerning Fractures When Occurring to the Head.
The types of fractures are numerous, and their shapes are diverse, and their causes are distinct, for some fractures are caused by a blow of a blunt instrument, and the blow shatters the entire bone down to the skull, just as it would a wooden log.  Thus, this type is called a comminuted fracture; or the blunt instrument cuts a part of the bone, and cuts the flesh without penetrating the bone behind it; and this type of fracture is called an incomplete fracture.
And the severity of these two types of fractures can be great or small, and there are fractures that are caused by crushing or grinding; their cause is a blow with a stone or a fall on a rock, or similar impacts.
And this fracture is either penetrating, approaching the membrane beneath the bone, or is on the surface of the bone.  The severity of these two types of fractures is either great or slight.
And there is a fracture that is hidden within the bone, in the smallness of a hair, and because of this, this type of fracture is called a hairline fracture.
And there is a fracture made either by a fall or by a blow from a stone or similar objects, which causes some part of the bone to be driven inwards.  It happens in the concave portion, like what happens to the teeth of a comb when struck, and part of their protrusion goes inwards.
This happens most often in the bones of moist bodies, such as the heads of infants.
And in all these types of fractures, the fragments are scattered like separated tiles. And I will come to reposition all of that in its place.
And we recognize all these types of fractures by probing them and by examining their surrounding areas for signs of decaying flesh.
A specific kind of fracture is recognized because a [capillary?] is exposed on the bone, and it is cleaned and probed, because the fracture appears dark/black.
But in the treatment of fractures, first consider the patient's symptoms, and if you see through the symptoms that they indicate manifest fear, such as vomiting of bile, convulsions, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, and swelling and stiffness of the limbs, and similar symptoms to those in more severe conditions, without a doubt, it is not going to be cured/healed.
And if you see non-standard symptoms, you hope for recovery, then you begin the treatment.
The reason for this is that if the patient has come to you at the beginning of the illness, as it is with the plague, and this is during the winter days, then you must examine the bone's condition before the 14th day, according to all conditions.
And if it is during the summer days, then you must strive to examine the bone before the seventh day, lest the area below the bone be corrupted by the pannicle [a skin-like covering], and the symptoms we have mentioned occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the pannicle covering the brain, and is with small pieces and friction, then you must cut away the broken and frayed parts, as I will instruct you.
And you are to shave the head of the afflicted patient, and uncover the bone according to the custom and manner possible to you, considering the nature of the plague, and to be gentle with the patient.
And if you encounter bone exposure, blood flow, or a hot abscess, then you must oppose it with what is needed, and you should fill the area with cloths infused in wine and rubbed oil, and allow the plague to rest until the abscess is calmed, and the blood flow ceases; and then begin with the surrounding bone and its removal.
And this should be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you cut the bone with a narrow incision tool, and this is its shape:  And then administer another incision tool, a little further out than the first; and this is its shape:
And then administer another incision tool further out than the second one.
And it is necessary that you have several different incision tools, some wider than others, and some shorter than others, with sharpened edges at their extremities, and made of hardened iron or steel.
And use them with ease in striking the incision tool so that you do not injure the head and damage it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then it is necessary to pierce around it before you administer the incision tools with the drills, which are called non-deepening drills.
And they are not called this way except because they do not penetrate the surface of the bone at the point near it, because the drill has a round tip or edge at that part below the pointed head, that prevents it from sinking in and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take a large number of these drills, each suited to the thickness of each bone, so that you have available in every skull a tip that is sharp, the length and shortness of which is according to the thickness of that particular skull.
And this is the method for three types of drills:  The method of piercing around the fractured bone is to place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know the bone is pierced; then change the drill to another location and place it at a distance equal to the thickness of the bone's width or approximately that.
Then you cut with the incision tools what's between each hole in the bone, making it as thin as possible, as we've said, until you separate the bone, either with your hand or with some other device such as pliers and delicate forceps.
And you must carefully ensure that the incision tool does not touch anything of the skull.
And therefore, once you have encircled the pierced bone and separated it from the skull if it is attached, and you have extracted it, you must scrape and smooth the rough surface of the bone with another tool similar to a knife, but it must be finer and more delicate than the other incision tools.
And if any small, jagged pieces of bone remain, then remove them easily with your tools; and then treat the wound with liniments and ointments as we have described.
And another method of operation is gentle, free of fear, which Gualtier and Laetius described with great praise.  And this is their teaching: it is necessary for you at the beginning to start uncovering the area of the bone where the fracture is most severe and noticeable, until, when you have uncovered that area and placed under it the end of this gentle and pleasant incision tool, which is of this form:
It is that the lenticular part is smooth and does not cut anything, and the sharp part extends in two directions along the length, as seen; so that the lenticular part is joined to the skull with the sharp cutting face against the bone.
Then strike the incision tool on one side with a small mallet until the entire bone is cut easily, as it is turned, and you are certain that the bone will not suffer the accident that you fear in any way.  And if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; truly, even if he were asleep.
Then if any piece remains attached to the bone in any part of the bone, cut that from it with the end of that lenticular incision tool, and remove it easily, for it is removed without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the flesh, and what has been cut off or removed from the bone is its surface, and roughnesses and fine jagged edges remain, then it is necessary to smooth those roughnesses and eradicate those jagged edges with fine razors for which you have prepared a number of various sizes so that you can administer the most suitable and most correct ones according to what you consider appropriate to your operation on the broken bone and its shape.
And it is necessary that in your process of smoothing the bone, you begin with the largest of those razors, and then progressively use the finer ones, and do not cease doing this in succession until you arrive at administering the finest and most subtle of all of them.
However, for each of the other small, capillary fractures and subtle fractures, it is necessary to administer care according to what you deem necessary for its repair.
And it is a cause that is not hidden from all those who have studied in that art. And what we have written about this, we have shown in large fractures.
And if the flesh is retracted when you expose the bone, then it is necessary to take a cloth corresponding to the size of the wound, and soak it in warmed oil, and place it over the wound opening; then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and soak it in warmed wine and oil, and place it over the first cloth, doing this as gently as possible so you do not press on the flesh; and then apply a thick bandage, and do not tighten it except to the point where it holds the wound together.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are sure of a feverish abscess; then remove it and then administer medicines that are good for dryness, such as flax seeds, barley flour, ashes, and powdered aristolochia, and similar things.
And you should make a powder from these medicines, ground into a powder, and apply it to the wound, as they are dry; and universally, administer any medicine that is of a nature to cleanse and not to bite.
And watch carefully, as the wound in your treatment should be clean, and should not be tainted by ointment or anything else; nor should you allow the virulence to increase there. For when it is concentrated on the flesh of the brain, it corrupts it and rots it, and causes the sick person great and malicious harm.
And problems occur in the flesh of the head, when the bone is exposed, and especially when the treatment of it is neglected, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore, if you see the patient having the symptoms we have described, know that it is dangerous without a doubt.
And if it is not blackness but from the medicine placed on it, and the nature of that medicine causes that blackness, then you should take one part of honey and three parts of olive oil, and with these, moisten a cloth, and then place that on the affected area; and then treat it with the appropriate healing spices until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Concerning the restoration of a broken nose.
Know that the nose is not broken in two separate, very serious ways, or in one of those ways occasionally, because you have two bones there, for the lower part is cartilaginous, and it is not broken, and you only see it with rubbing, twisting, and bending.
But if one of the two parts is broken, then you should insert your little finger into the nose, and use your index finger to hold that fracture inside, and your thumb on the outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this easily, and be careful not to cause the patient pain with your procedure.
And if the fracture is in the most serious part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it should be straightened with the tip of a bone tool which has a small thickened area/handle.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the same.
And try to restore it on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then approximately the 7th or 10th day, apply a poultice to the inflamed area.
And insert a piece of soft cloth into the nostril, if the fracture is in one part, or two pieces, if the fracture is in two parts, and each piece should be of sufficient thickness to fill the nasal opening.
Some ancient restorers said the pieces should be soaked in wine and changed daily, but I have not seen this.  Instead, the cloth should be dipped in beaten egg mixed with powdered lime, and leave it in place until the bone is set and the cartilage hardened.
And sometimes, instead of a cloth piece, a bird's feather wrapped in soft cloth is inserted into the nose,  especially if the nasal fracture is severe and the patient is not being prevented from breathing.
And this method is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do this, and if you wish, do it with cloth pieces.
And if a further abscess arises, plaster the nose with a cerate or with cotton soaked in sour wine and rubbed oil, or with diachylon.
However, if no further abscess arises, then you should plaster the external one with sesame flour and powdered coriander seeds mixed with egg white; then place a soft bandage over it, and do not bind the nose with anything else.
But if the nasal bones are fractured, or have small splinters, then you must cut around them and remove them with the appropriate tools; afterwards, close up the wound and heal it with what promotes healing and consolidation, and with appropriate plasters.
And if a sore appears inside the nose, then you should treat it with cloth strips and administer lead tubes until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
Of the restoration of the jaw when it is fractured.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by an open wound, observe if the fracture is only outward, and not broken in two but bent inward at the edges, then its reduction is easy.
And it is necessary, therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, that you insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert the said index finger of the right hand; and push the irregularity of the fracture inwards easily with the other hand on the outside of the bone; with that gently adjust it.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary to administer extension to both parts according to straightness until its reposition is possible.
And if you encounter a movement of teeth with disruption, then tighten those that you expect to remain with gold, silver, or silk thread; and then place a compress on the broken jaw, then place a folded cloth over it, and place a large, well-made bandage, or a strip of cloth equal to the length of the jaw; and then tie it as your binding apparatus allows, and the application is suitable, until it is no longer damaged.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and give him easy-to-digest food.
And if you estimate that the shape has been altered in any way, then you should remove and loosen it on the third day; and then correct what has been altered and plaster it with ground powder, with egg white, or with chaff flour, after you have removed the compress, and place a soft wool plaster over it.
And then, when that plaster is properly attached and the bone's position is not altered, leave it; do not remove it until it is healed and the fracture is tight; for many times this fracture tightens within three weeks.
And if another time a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have often said for its resolution until it is healed and the abscess is resolved.
And if the fracture is with a plaque, then consider if it is a detached piece of bone or splinters; then you must carefully remove those splinters with what is suitable for their removal from instruments.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you should widen it with a lancet according to your needs.
And then, when you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains of them, then you should close the wound opening if it is wide; and if not, place one of the suitable plasters over it and allow it to heal until it is sound.
Chapter 5.
Of the restoration of the collarbone when it is fractured.
Many times the collarbone is fractured on the front part towards the muscle, and that fracture is done in one of three ways.
When it is broken, or separated, or separated into two parts, other than splinters; and it is easy to restore it; or it is such that splinters are present, and it is difficult to restore it; or it is such that the fracture is with a wound.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds and steadies the fractured collarbone, and the other extends the neck towards that area.
And then mold the fracture with your fingers until its shape is correct, neither protruding nor indented.
And if you need more extension, then you should place under the patient's neck a pad of cloth or wool, and its size should be according to your needs; and extend and raise the collarbone and compress the pad with your hand until you have correctly aligned the fracture.
And if you cannot pull the collarbone's end outward, because it has become stuck, then the patient should be laid on their back, and place a slightly larger cushion on their neck muscles.
And the assistant should press the muscles beneath to raise the collarbone from its stuck position, and then you should realign and mold the fracture with your fingers.
But if you feel splinters of the collarbone and they are loose, then it is necessary to cut around them and easily remove those splinters.
And if it is lodged in the bone, then you should make an incision there with one of the scalpels you have prepared for this, after placing the instrument that aids the splint under the collarbone; and this is a wooden or iron instrument whose shape is as follows:
And it resembles a spoon, of which this is the concavity; and its width should be according to your need, according to the size of the bone or its smallness.
But its length should be as is possible during the operation; it should have two ends like a vise, and one end should be wide and the other narrow.
And if the wound, which you have incised when extracting the splinter from the bone, is wide, and you are certain of a hot abscess, then you should join the two edges of the wound with sutures.
And if the wound is small, or you fear a hot abscess, then you should fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's incision.
And if a hot abscess occurs, then soak a cloth in sour wine, and rubbed oil and wine, and place it over it.
And be careful that the splint does not move from the broken bone; therefore, you must examine the patient daily, and when you see the ligament softened or the splint has come loose, then you should correct this and tighten the bandage.
