And then place the patient's bedding over their neck, and place a small cushion under the patient's armpit beneath the bedding at night; so that with that the patient's arm is elevated away from the side, and thus the fracture of the collarbone (furcula) is elevated from the muscle; or the patient's arm is supported at the side of their neck.
And do not loosen the bandage unless there is a boil or abscess in the area, until twelve days have passed.
And then renew the plaster if you see the patient's skin reacting to it, and adjust the position and maintain it until the fracture of the collarbone is restored and healed.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the month in 28 days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Of the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The shoulder blade is fractured at its lateral aspect, and it is not the bone itself that is broken, but the edges.
And therefore when that place is fractured—and it is fractured in the middle of it—and it is not known except by touching, then, according to the fracture's appearance, you must prepare it and reduce it to its natural shape by whatever means are possible to you.
Then place on the area powder of ground stone mixed with egg white and lint; and then place a folded cloth pad; and then place on top of that a thin, flat piece of wood according to the size of the entire shoulder blade or a little more.
However, if beneath the wood there is a hollow in any part of the shoulder blade, then you fill that hollow with lint, and then raise the wood to a level.
And then hold it down firmly from above with a long bandage, with appropriate constriction, and with firmness of the wood until it is no longer displaced from its place.
And check the ligament each day; and every time it is softened, bandage it again and tighten it and adjust the wood if it has moved from its place.
And let the patient lie on their sound side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in 20 or 25 days.
And therefore, when this number of days is complete, then you dissolve the bandage, and you should know for certain that it is from the bones whose connection is not feared, nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the area of the heavy bone and place the splint underneath, then you should cut around it, remove it, and act as we have described in the fracture of the furcula, to resolve the abscess, if one has occurred there.
Chapter 7.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot sometimes breaks in the middle, and that heals slowly; but the extremities are often more easily fractured and shattered.
And the symptoms when the middle of the foot is broken are that it inclines to the inside, causing great pain, difficulty breathing, coughing, a strong fever, and vomiting blood; and the broken bone's cavity is apparent, not hidden from the sense.
And the restoration involves laying the patient on their back, placing a cushion between their shoulders; then applying pressure to their muscles and aligning their bones with the hands on both sides, proceeding carefully in the operation and adapting to all possible and appropriate ways, until the bone returns to its proper shape.
Then apply a plaster and bandage, placing a thin layer of willow or caligo (or similar lightweight material), and wrap that in a cloth; then carefully apply this bandage over the broken bone so it isn't disturbed.  Sometimes bind it in a rounded shape on the back, tightening it appropriately.  And constantly check the bandage, and when it's softened, tighten it further.
And if the bandage causes inflammation, suppuration, pain, or an abscess in the area, then you should remove and reapply it, and treat whatever has occurred with appropriate healing methods.  Then replace the plaster, if necessary, and continue adjusting the bandage until healed.
Chapter 8.
Of restoring broken bones.
Know that broken bones only occur in thick areas, like the back; and the damage to the ends and front portions of the bone happens only due to the cartilage there. And its understanding isn't hidden from the feeling/sense by investigation of it with words.
And the restoration is done by aligning the fracture with fingers in the best way possible, until it is aligned properly, and then apply a plaster and bind the broken bone with splints, if needed.
And if the fracture of the ribs is towards the inner parts, then the patient experiences intense pain and a stabbing sensation, just like the stabbing pain one experiences with pleurisy, and this is because the bone pierces the lining; and there is difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood; and this is a difficult condition to treat.
And the ancients were ingenious in that matter with many inventions.
And of what they said: it is necessary to give the patient foods that generate inflammation and gas, so that the belly is swollen and distended, and the fracture is pushed outward.
But we would avoid this, for it is not a light thing to cause a hot abscess, if it does not happen; and if it already happens, then it helps with that.
And some of them would say that a cupping glass should be placed over it and then that it should be suctioned forcefully; and this seems like a sound reasoning, but nevertheless, it is feared that the cupping glass will draw the superfluities to the area because of the weakness.
And some of them would say: the area should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is filled, that the dressing should be tightly wrapped around the area according to its roundness; and then the patient should be treated with the treatment for pleurisy, with food and medicine.
And if a severe cause afflicts the patient, which they cannot endure, and the bone pierces the skin with a painful puncture, we fear for the patient, then it is necessary to open the area, expose the broken rib, and then place an instrument over that which will hold the rib in place—the memory of which has already been made—and that we cut the bone easily and extract it.
And then we bring the two sides of the wound together, if it is large, with stitches, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if you find an abscess inside, then you mix powders in heated oil and apply it to the area, and let the patient be treated with whatever is appropriate for the internal abscess.
And let him lie on his side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
Of the restoration of a fractured vertebra of the spine and neck.
Sometimes there are fractures in the bones of the neck, and sometimes there is a grinding of them, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone, and you want to know if they will be healed or not, then consider this: if the muscles are already softened, numb, dead, and you cannot move them, stretch them, or squeeze them, and when you press them with your fingers or prick them with a needle, they feel no pain, then know that they are not healed, and in this state of affairs, death is imminent.
And if you move them and feel pressure with your fingers and pricking, then know that the bone's integrity is preserved and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see similar signs in the vertebrae of the neck and want to know if they will heal or not, then consider the patient's feet.
Then if you see that the feet are also softened, like what happens in the muscles, and when the patient lies on their back, they experience involuntary movement and urination, and sometimes are lying on their back, wanting to urinate but unable, then know that it is fatal, and do not bother with treatment.
But if you do not see any of those symptoms, the cause is better and less severe.
And the treatment for this is to apply a soothing poultice to the inflamed vertebra, either oil or crushed eggshells; apply it several times a day until the inflammation is soothed.
And then apply a comforting and stimulating plaster, and bind it firmly; and order the patient to rest and remain calm, and not sleep on the affected area until it is healed.
And if you find signs of bone fracture or any detached part, then it is necessary to cut away the skin and remove that bone; and then join the two edges of the wound, with stitches if it is large; and then treat it with a healing ointment until healed.
But if the back of the bone is broken, and it's at the end of the spine, then you should insert the index finger of your left hand into the rectum and align the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible by applying gentle pressure; and then apply a plaster and bandage if needed, and bind it tightly.
And if you find broken pieces in it, then you should remove those pieces, and treat the wound with the previous treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Of the restoration of the [broken] width/breadth.
The bones of the hips are often broken, and if they break, their fracture is only a separation at their extremities and they are cut along their length and deviate inward; and the patient experiences pain and throbbing at the site, and the leg is numb when fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until it is over the fracture, which is the figure of it.
And if the fracture is only at its extremity, then you hold it together as best as you are able by approximation until it is aligned with its natural form.
But if the fracture is along it or has already shifted downwards, then you have the patient lie on their stomach until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. And therefore, when you have set it, place a poultice on it, then place a splint of wood or bone over that, and bind tightly so that you fear neither the puncturing of the fracture nor the movement of the splint.
And hold the cavity with whatever fills it until it takes on the tightness according to equality, and order the patient to sleep on their back or on their sound side.
But if a hot abscess arises, then cease the extension and restoration until the hot abscess is calmed, and place on it whatever calms it as is previously prescribed; then return to the restoration, and bind it as is fitting.
And if splinters or fragments of bone occur, then you remove them.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the restoration of the fractured limb.
Adjutori" is that which is between the elbow and the top of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a piece of wood of medium thickness in this manner:
And having tied two bandages to its two ends, it should then be suspended from a raised position.
And have the patient lie on a stretcher; then gently place the patient's broken arm upon the piece of wood until the bone's tip is in the middle of the curved portion of the wood.
And then suspend something heavy upon it, or the assistant stretches it downwards; and then the physician holds the fracture with their hands together, until the fracture returns as it should.
The other method is that the patient be placed on their back and the hand be supported by a bandage; and then command two assistants, one of whom will hold what is above the fracture with their hands, and the other hold what is below it; and each of them should pull in their respective directions.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you bind the fracture with one bandage and another bandage over that, and each of the two assistants should pull in their respective directions.
And therefore, if the fracture is close to the muscle's extremity, it is necessary that half of the bandage be placed on the bone and the other below the fracture, near the elbow.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary that the bandage be placed on that location and around that elbow.
Apply this with ease and without effort.  Then, when it is properly bound and applied correctly, secure it tightly lest a harmful abscess arise.
However, if a harmful abscess arises, then leave the tightness until the seventh day, and apply soaked wool to the area in sour wine and warmed olive oil until the abscess subsides. Then secure it tightly.
And the manner of securing it is to place a poultice over the fracture, then wrap a clean cloth over the poultice.  Then support the arm with this support, and place their open hand over their muscle (or the affected area).
And secure the cloth and the support around the arm so that the arm is in a straight position, if no reason due to the broken bone forbids this.
And then use splints.
And you should place over the fracture a splint that is wider and stronger than the others; and place the width of a finger between each splint, and the length of the splints should be according to the break, with the addition of three fingers on each side.
And then secure the splints with the binding I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And make sure your binding around the fracture area is tight. And the longer the fracture, the less tight the binding should be.
And therefore adjust the placement of the splints and the binding as we have said at the moment when you restore the limb to its proper position, then you do... (or, then proceed).
And if you fear a suppurating abscess, then you leave the binding and splints in place until the seventh day, as we have said.
And then consider the limb in all its parts for three days so that swelling or inflammation doesn't occur there; or food is forbidden to the limb because of a superfluity of binding; and then rectify everything according to what we have said.
But if you are confident that no cause of this will occur, then do not remove the bandage until approximately three days or more.
And let the patient's rest be on their back, and their hands on their stomach.
And place on the support a poultice full of wool, and consider at all times of night or day that the shape of the broken limb is not distorted or that the bandage is not softened; therefore, rectify all this with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we previously mentioned, so that the food is subtle at the beginning until it begins to be strengthened; and then you must make the food heavier.
Because according to the custom of the support and the limb, they are to be tightened for 40 days; and then they must be loosened, and a bath administered and medicated with ointments that correct that.
And if the fracture is badly bruised, then do not loosen the bandage and splints for that injury until a few days or until two months.
Chapter 12.
Of the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, called "two braches"; of which one is small, and it is the one that sits next to the said thumb, and the other is large, and is the one that is positioned under the small finger, on the lower part.
And frequently the larger bone is broken alone, or the smaller one, and frequently they are both broken together.
And when the smaller bone above is broken, then the restoration of it is easier, and its healing is more straightforward.
And when the bone below is broken, the fracture of it is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And even worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the small bone above, then it is appropriate for the physician, in order to facilitate its restoration, to apply a small extension until it is healed.
But if the large bone is broken, then a more forceful extension is necessary.
And if both bones are broken together, then you must apply the compression very strongly.
And you must position your hand in a way that supports the restoration and extension, resting it on a cushion.  The palm of your hand should be higher than all the other parts, the thumb should be lower than the other fingers, and the patient should be lying on a smooth, hard surface (possibly a board or similar).
And there should be a cushion in front of him, at his height, so that the patient does not feel strain.
And then the assistant stretches the lower arm with their hand or a bandage, and another assistant similarly stretches the upper arm.
And then the physician manipulates the bone until it is reduced to the best possible position.
And if in the fracture of the bone, there are irregularities, then you must strive to reduce every piece to its proper place with your skill.
And if there appears a detached fragment that pinches the tendon, and you are not hopeful of restoring it, then you must operate on it and remove it according to the method we described above.
But if the fracture is with a wound, I have already written a separate chapter about that, and therefore take care of its treatment.
And if you encounter a suppurating abscess at the beginning of your treatment, then you should apply a cloth soaked in a cerot made of olive oil and white wax, and it should be medium in thickness and subtlety, and tightly bandage the cloth over it until the abscess subsides; and then remove the cerot and apply a prepared plaster of ground powder mixed with egg white, and then place a bandage; and the bandage placed over the fracture should be large and strong.
And know that the names of the bandages for the arm are varied in their arrangements, that is, whether the fracture is in one compartment or two compartments together.
And then apply your bandage to the fracture site as firmly and intensely as possible, and moreover, if the bandage is applied to the upper parts of the fracture or to its lower parts, then apply the bandage a little more loosely, according to what was previously mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
And the cloths wrapped around the fracture should be soft and damp, not hard or rough, and the bandage with which they are tied should be neither too fine nor too thick, medium as we described.
And observe the limb and the ligament for several days. And if any cause requiring correction arises, such as swelling occurring in the limb, then you should immerse the limb in warm water until the swelling subsides.
And leave the limb unconstrained until it rests, and then reapply the bandage.
And if the constriction is now softened and the bone is now rejoined and similar to the others, then correct all of this with your skill.
And consider further if the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excess constriction, for then it is necessary to soften that slightly and leave it for several days until nourishment flows to it, and then bind it firmly.
But if the patient does not exhibit any of the causes we have mentioned, then it is not necessary to unbind it for approximately twenty days or thereabouts.
And then, the patient's hand should be suspended by its own ability to move actively, and let him sleep on his side.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 days or 32 days, or quite quickly in 28 days, and all of this is according to the disposition of the complexions and the patient's constitution.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the restoration of fractures of the wrist and fingers.
Know that fractures of the wrist and the joints of the fingers often occur from forceful impact, and sometimes from repeated friction.
And when the hand suffers a fracture or injury from friction, then it is necessary that the patient lie down, and extend the injured hand in a straight line with the body.  Then place the patient's hand on that extended position.
And then the attendant stretches the broken bones, and the doctor binds them together until they are properly rejoined.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, lest an abscess arise; then place a splint over it, according to the size of the wound, and you have already wrapped this in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is in the parts inside the palm of the hand, then make a bandage in the shape of a sling, and instruct the patient to hold it with their injured palm; then tie it with a long bandage.
And let the splint be of leather, with a cloth in which it is supported, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and tie it as necessary.
But if the fracture is in the outer parts, then it is necessary to place a splint over it, and another splint below, on the palm of the hand, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply the bandage, as if the hand is turned towards the wrist between the fingers with ligatures.
And if a fracture occurs in one of the finger joints, then if it is the thumb, it should be splinted as necessary, and then it should be held with the palm and, if you wish, place a small, straight splint on it to elevate the fracture and prevent it from moving.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger, the index finger, the ring finger, or the pinky finger, then it should be splinted and bound with the finger next to it; or all should be bound together, following a continuous line, which is better; or place a small splint on it, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and afterwards, the placement of a warm poultice and its opposition – with your operation – with whatever is necessary when any such cause occurs, according to how the account of it has been repeated.
Chapter 14.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the thigh bone.
The thigh bone is frequently broken and is recognised by the symptoms as it is shifted to the front and uppermost part.
And the restoration of it is done so that the ligament is tightened over the fracture and another ligament is placed below the fracture, and the patient lies on their stomach and on their face.
And then the physician stretches all the ligament to its proper position, when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
But if it is close to the root of the thigh, then the ligament should be positioned near the knee to the groin area, so that the extension is in line with it, and the other ligament below the fracture.
And similarly, if the break is near the knee, then the ligament should be positioned near the knee, and the extension should be in the lower areas.
And then the physician aligns the bone with both their hands until it returns to its natural shape and the bone is properly joined; then it is necessary to apply a poultice and a bandage, otherwise the limb might become infected. But if it becomes infected, then you leave it for a few days until the infection subsides, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be applied like this: it is necessary to wrap a hard bandage around the fracture, fold it two or three times, and remove any excess; then wrap the leg until it reaches the ankle and the root of the thigh.
And insert a long splint between the thigh and leg near the knee in the lower areas, and let the ends of the splint extend from the most superior part of both sections, and then wrap the excess of the bandage around the leg and thigh.
And then place splints over the thigh at the location of the fracture, and place one splint on the bone of the leg.
And then fill the gaps between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the fracture is consolidated.
And then begin with a strong bandage in the middle over the fracture site using three or four wraps, and the farther you move away from the fracture site with the bandage, the weaker and lighter and softer the bandage should be.
And then extend the two ends of the splint down to the ankle of the foot, and tighten both ends of the splints on the other side so that the bandage does not shift from its position.
And then leave the bandage on it, so that no swelling, abscess, or inflammation, and similar conditions, affect the limb; and if any such thing happens, then remove it gently, and correct whatever has happened right away, as we have said many times.
But if there is a piece of bone protruding, then you must pull it back as best you can; and if not, cut over it and leave it, and treat it with whatever is previously prescribed until it heals.
And sometimes this thigh fracture is tightened with splints, just as we have said about splints for the arm.
However, in the restoration done in this manner, the patient does not develop a limp; and if it is restored on its own, or with the other leg applied, then it is necessary that the one which has always limped does so.
And know that the thigh is bandaged for forty days, or a little more or a little less, and all of this according to the diversity of the constitutions and other conditions.
Chapter 15.
Of the restoration of the fractured kneecap (or patella).
Know that a fracture of the kneecap (patella) often results, but it frequently causes considerable attrition (wear and tear).
However, if it does result in a fracture, it is then only a fissure or hole in its parts, and this is with or without a plaster, and concerning all of this, it has been with feeling.
And the restoration of it is thus, that what is broken in its parts is glued together using the said materials until it is joined and conjoined as much as possible by means of appropriate application, ease, and craftsmanship.
Then place a plaster and lay it over that round bandage, if you need it; and bind tightly over it with a suitable bandage.
And now consider all the methods we have described in other fractures, just as in an abscess or similar situations, so that it is opposed to any accident with whatever is appropriate, until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Of the restoration of a fractured leg.
Know that in the leg there are two bones, one of which is thick, and is called the thigh bone; and the other is thin, and is called the shinbone.
And the treatment for any type of fracture of these bones is similar to that for fractures of the arm, and the restoration is therefore the same, the procedure being identical.
And if both bones are broken together, the leg is dislocated in all directions.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is displaced forward.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you find it below, and it is one of those things that isn't hidden from you.
And then administer extension, reduction, and ligature of the splints accordingly.
However, if the fracture of the leg is extensive, having many splinters, the extension should be less forceful and lighter, and ease it with your skill.
And if there is any reason for operating on the arm, and when you adjust the splints and complete your operation, then you will take pieces of wood, pine being the best, which are placed under the supports that are placed on the bed, also made of palm branches; choose those with a small thickness, and none should be bulky.  Their length should correspond to the length of the leg, from the top to the very bottom.
And then wrap each with a cloth in two layers according to its length, placing one on the leg portion on one side and the other on the other side, from the top down to the lowest part of the foot.
And then tie the two splints in three places: at both ends and in the middle.  With this binding, the leg is prevented from bending to either the right or left side, and it is securely positioned.
And now a small wooden brace, corresponding to the length of the leg, is placed on it to prevent movement.
And this is especially necessary for fractures, and when there is a clear break.
And henceforth, examine the leg each day, and carefully monitor it for abscesses, inflammation, or other similar problems.  If any such problem arises, then apply the appropriate remedy until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the fracture of the ankle and bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture of the ankle doesn't occur very often; however, fractures of the foot bones do happen sometimes; and fractures of the toes are rare, and they mainly sustain friction rather than fractures in their usual positioning.
And if the foot bones experience a fracture, and those bones are displaced, then the patient should place their foot on the ground in a prepared position, as if walking.
Then place one foot on what is elevated by those bones, and gently press and push them until they return to their proper places.
Then apply a plaster and bandage over it, and place a small, flat board with two smooth ends underneath the sole of the foot.
And then tightly bind that with a bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths and other materials as needed.
And when three or four days have passed, then you loosen the bandage, for you will find the bones reduced, or broken, or dislocated, and the operation for those is as you know.
But if any bones are broken, then set them, and bring them together as I have recounted to you in the setting of the fingers of the hand. And then place a splint along the length of the broken finger, and slightly wider than it; and then beneath the foot, this board as previously explained to you, and bind it tightly with a proper bandage.
But if what is broken of the bones is two, three, or more, then you place on each finger a splint of its size, wrapped in linen, and tightly bind the board to the lowest part of the foot, and let one end of the board be outside the belly of the foot so that it has good support.
And it is fitting for you not to forget that you consider all the things that I have told you about other fractures and accidents that we have discussed; and prepare yourself for any accident with what is necessary.
Chapter 18.
Of the fracture of the thigh bone, the hip bone, and the penis of the man.
When the thigh bone of a woman is fractured, then make a splint of treated wood, and then slightly incline it towards the back, and keep it behind.
And then the midwife fills the thigh with wool, and it should be placed in the thigh just as before; and then the woman should be quieted, and lift her back slowly, since everything is displaced until the thigh is in front of the area as before and the broken bone returns.
And then place cushions on her back, and when she wants to urinate, she will remove the wool easily and urinate; and reduce and return it according to the method by which it was applied at the beginning, and then return it to its original position.
And do this for seven days or so, for that is how it is restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and insert a reed cannula into the wound; and put the entire bladder into the thigh, and then blow into the cannula with force until the bladder is inflated inside the thigh, so that the fracture returns.
And then it should be filled with wool and remain so for several days, as we have said, until it is healed.
And when the hip bone of a man or woman is broken, then administer in its restoration and setting what we have described for a broken hip bone.
And what is correct is not hidden in these nearby fractures, which heal slowly, especially for those who have some study and use and understanding of this my book's words; for that fracture signifies to you in the greatest ways a correct path in the restoration and tightening of it, so understand well.
But in the case of a broken penis, take a bird's nest, and place the penis in it; then place a bandage of cloth over it, wrapping it up, and leave it for three days or so, and it will heal.
Chapter 19.
Of the restoration of fractures of bones when it is with a plague.
It is convenient for the one who suffers a fracture with a plague, and especially if the bone is large, such as the thighbone or the shinbone, or similar to those, that you should bleed him/her immediately if the conditions for bloodletting allow you to do so as mentioned above.
And if the plague causes blood to flow, then you must apply a poultice to the area to retain it, even if you pulverize a crushed stone, if no other remedy is available.
And then begin restoring the fracture on that same day, and do not delay, unless an abscess develops.
But if a severe abscess develops, then you should delay the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides, and do not approach it in the third and fourth days at all, for you will cause harmful accidents.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the exposed skin, then it is necessary to carefully reduce it and hold it in place with your hands, with ease and minimal stretching.
However, if stretching is not suitable for you and repositioning it with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands using this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, whose length is seven or eight inches, and whose width depends on the size of the injury; and therefore it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of them, depending on the amount needed for treatment of any type of fracture.
And let it be round, and have little thickness so that it is not bent under the pressure on it during the operation.
And it should have a sharp tip, with a bevel on its edges; and its top should be thick, tapering down from the middle towards the ends.
And this is its shape:
And it is called in Greek "labre volentz atelarem", meaning a small spatula.
So that the pointed end must be placed elevated on the prominent bone end.
And you push it in one go until the bone returns, and it is secured with some binding.
And therefore, strive to bind the fractured ends together.  And if the broken end is small, and it doesn't accept the instrument well, then you must cut the end of the bone until the instrument can properly grasp it.
But if you cannot fully reduce the bone with what we have described, then cut it with whatever surgical instruments are appropriate; or saw it with one of the saws, in whatever way is possible for you.
And then remove any remaining rough edges and small splinters from the bone.
And when the bone is reduced, and the patient feels intense pain after the reduction, then know that the bone has not been returned to its natural place; and then do what you deem best to greatly benefit the patient.
When your bone repair is complete, then immerse a cloth in black astringent wine, and, specifically if it's summertime, do not apply a plaster or any other oil-based substance to the wound, lest it cause swelling and corruption.
And after, administer styptics at the time of your bone repair completion, and leave the wound uncovered, so that you cut the opening with forceps and apply dressings according to the size of the wound.
And be very careful not to constrict the wound with the fracture, for foolish physicians have often done this, causing death or a corrosive or wasting illness in their patients; that is, gangrene. And if your constriction is slight, soft, different from the constriction of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or is a bad wound, and you fear any of the bad accidents we have mentioned, and find pain in the area, then it is not appropriate to apply styptics, but make a dressing of hard cloths instead of styptics and bind it tightly.
And when, after a day or two, pus begins to form in the wound, then you remove the cloth you placed over it with wine, and then apply poultices, ointments with which, according to our custom, we treat wounds, such as tetrafarmacon ointment and similar ones.
And it is necessary for you to loosen the bandage and examine the wound each day, morning and evening, until it is healed and closed, and the limb must be prepared in such a way that the pus flows from it to the lower parts with ease.
If the wound has passed many days and is not healed nor is the pus discharged from it, then know that there are hidden complications, and you must investigate the wound with probing. And of those complications, those that are separable, remove and extract them; those that are not separable and cause pain to the limb, force you to cut into them, as far as it is possible for you.
And if the wound becomes inflamed or swollen, or other forms of corruption or putrefaction occur, then you must apply a remedy for that, as is appropriate for the treatment, of which a description precedes in its chapter.
And in this, where my discourse ends and you must apply your skill: when a large bone is broken, treat the limb as with a thighbone or similar large limb bones, and do not attempt to pull it out, or to extract it, because death often results; leave it until it can heal, for it will fall into place by itself in about 20 or 30 days.  Then treat the wound if you see fit, and if not, leave it.
Chapter 20.
Of the treatment of swelling that occurs in the area of a fracture.
Often this swelling occurs around the area of fracture healing, especially close to the joints, and it deforms the shape of the limb.  And it greatly hinders the natural function of the limb.
Then consider if the swelling is recent, and apply medicines that have a drawing or astringent effect, such as aloes, and henbane, and myrrh, and ceruse, and similar substances; use some or all of them, and mix them with astringent wine, or egg white, or vinegar, and place this mixture on the swelling over a piece of cloth, firmly bandaging it.
And maintain this bandage for several days without loosening it. Then remove it and apply another, until the swelling is gone; or apply a lead plate gently, for lead has the property of reducing any swelling in the limbs.
However, if the swelling has hardened and become tight, and it's necessary to remove it forcefully, then carefully cut it at its most prominent part, cutting away the excess and prominent material, or break it up with certain tools until it is removed.  Then treat the area until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Of the restoration of a fracture, when it is restored and afterward the limb remains weak and frail, not according to its natural state.
When a fractured bone is restored, and afterward the limb remains weak and frail, this is caused by many things: one is an excess of loosening of the ligaments and the binding done improperly; or by an excess of tight binding of the ligaments, preventing nourishment from reaching the limb; or by excessive poulticing; or by unnecessary movement not at the right time; or by lack of blood in the patient's body and weakness.
And the cure for this is to give the patient food and make their body soft until the blood is replenished; and administer baths; and introduce joy and cheerfulness upon them.
And then apply poultices to the limb, so that the poultice draws more nourishment to it, or continue applying warm water poultices, until the nourishment flows and returns to its natural form.
Chapter 22.
Of the treatment of broken bones when they are improperly set and prevent their proper operation.
When an already repaired limb shows signs of improper healing, such as crookedness and prominence at the fractured bone, and this results in a deformed limb, but the limb is nonetheless not hindered from its natural function, then you should not accept the claim of someone who asserts that the limb is newly broken.
But many foolish doctors and healers have done this in our land; and this practice is highly reprehensible, leading to great fear, a sign of which is deadly.
However, if lameness and swelling are recent, then they should be treated with warm water in which are soaked softening herbs, such as marshmallow leaves and roots, and melilot, and similar ones; and an emollient plaster should be applied, such as a well-made diachylon; or a mucilage from marshmallow root should be made, mixed with goose fat or beeswax oil, and applied as a plaster; or fatty figs should be crushed with pigeon dung, and similar medicines known as consolidating remedies.
And sometimes it is dissolved with persistent gentle rubbing with moist hands, and movement of the limb in all directions should be administered at all times.
However, if the twisting has progressed and become strong, hardened, and requires treatment with iron, then the swelling above should be released, and the continuation of the bone should be dissolved, and what is superfluous in the swelling of that bone should be cut away with delicate instruments, and ease should be administered with care and diligence.
Chapter 23.
Concerning dislocations.
Dislocation is the displacement of any joint from its proper position, preventing movement, distorting the shape of the limb, and causing significant pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation occurs, it is essential to quickly attempt reduction, and not delay it, for if delayed, the joint becomes inflamed and stiff, making dislocation reduction difficult.
And for this reason, it should not be moved or stretched during the inflammation stage, as it frequently causes spasms and worsening pain.
But when this happens, it is necessary that you apply a poultice to the affected area of the patient, and let it remain until the inflammation subsides; then, the limb should be bathed in warm water and oil, and the reduction should be attempted easily.
And every limb is thus treated, so that the memory of its place returns. (or, so that it will return to its proper place)
And I have already ordered the chapters on dislocations, following the precedent for fractures; from the most superior part of the body to the most inferior part.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the treatment of a lower jaw dislocation.
So, if both jaws are dislocated quickly; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are slightly displaced from their sockets and softened a bit; or they are completely and fully dislocated, until they are softened at the joint,  to the point where saliva drips from the patient, and the patient cannot hold their jaw shut or close their mouth, and the patient's tongue is entangled in their speech.
However, when the dislocation is slight, then in most cases it returns on its own with little effort.
But if the dislocation is complete and full, then it is necessary to administer a gentle reduction of it, and not delay at all.
And it is necessary for the attendant to hold the patient's head, and for the physician to insert the fingertip of one hand into the site of the jaw dislocation inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or to insert both thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of the hands are outside, supporting; and order the patient to relax the jaw and allow the jaw to move from each side; and the physician supports the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are completely dislocated, then administer vaporization with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both is made easier, and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And thus, when they return and are aligned, and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then place upon them cloths (made of linen) with a plaster, to be made of melted wax and oil; and afterward, be bound gently with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their head be supported between two cushions, so that it does not tilt to the right or left side; and do not force him to chew on anything; let his food be soft and bland, until the pain has subsided and the jaws are healed, then let him eat whatever he wants.
And this should be administered gently, and do not force the opening of the mouth for eating, drinking, or breathing, until the jaw is healed and sound.
And if the reduction of the two jaws, when dislocated at any time and not returned to their places, then often fever and continuous sweating occur, and the patient's stomach is easily agitated, and easily vomits pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is a sign of impending death; and often the person affected dies within a few days.
Chapter 25.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the muscle.
The collarbone is not dislocated from its connection within by its attachment to the foot; and sometimes it is dislocated outward, and this is evident to the touch.
And the restoration of it is that the patient lies on their back and extends their arms. And then apply pressure to the area with your palm with firm pressure, for it will return.
And then place a plaster and powders over that area and compress it.
However, the extremity that is attached to the elbow and continues from it is not dislocated in any way; therefore, it must be reduced and healed as we have said, and as is appropriate for your treatment.
And then place a plaster, powders, and a binding over that area, and instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until it is healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, of which one is that it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated from above the muscle, and this is done with difficulty; and it is not dislocated to the posterior parts by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated to the anterior parts by the nerves.
And most often it is dislocated and comes out from the lower parts toward the upper part, and especially in those where the flesh is scarce, it is easily dislocated; but in those in which there is much flesh, it is expelled with difficulty.
And often an accident occurs to some men through a blow or fall, and their muscle is inflamed with a hot abscess, and is believed to be dislocated because of this.
Therefore, you must examine this until you are certain about it, and then you should proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation is known when it is in the lower parts toward the upper part, so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, for you find a clear difference between them; and you find a concavity at the head of the muscle and below the upper part, near the contact point of the head of the muscle, like an egg, and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor more movement of that area.
If it is dislocated towards the feet or upwards, and you then find a clear touch, and it is not hidden.
And a mild reduction of this dislocation is made when it is fresh and the patient is young.
And the reduction of it is that the minister raises the patient's hand upwards; then gently places your hands below the upper part, and lifts the joint firmly into its place, and the minister raises the patient's hand, and extends it upwards; and then gently places it down, for it will return easily.
But if, despite what we have said, it does not return, and it has been a significant time since the dislocation occurred, then the patient must be bathed in warm water and given a poultice that softens and smoothes, such as a mixture of the roots of althea, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and it should be applied.
And then the patient should be placed on their back, and a layer of wool placed underneath their upper leg, ensuring it is neither too loose nor too tight.  Then the physician places their foot on the wool, and gently lifts the muscle head, pulling the patient's hand so it does not move downwards, and another assistant supports the patient's head, preventing it from moving downwards.  This way the dislocated joint will quickly return to its place.
And if desired, reduce it in this manner: have a man present who is taller than the patient, standing on the affected side. He should place his arm under the patient's upper leg and lift it upward until the patient is suspended in the air. Another assistant should pull the patient's hand downwards from below their abdomen. But if the patient is light, then something else should be suspended with it, to add weight, for this will cause the dislocation to return quickly.
And it is easily reduced by another method: a long wooden post, shaped like a mortar pestle, neither too thick nor too thin, is placed on the ground under the patient.  After placing the patient over the top of the post, a strong cloth is used, and the patient stands with their body aligned with the post's length, and the patient's hand is stretched downwards on the other side. The patient's body is then forcefully stretched away from the other side; the joint will return to its place quickly.
And if the reduction is difficult by all the methods we have mentioned, then use this treatment: take a piece of wood two arms long and four fingers wide, and its thickness should be moderate at the level of the upper leg, in this manner:
And strong cloth should be tied around the rounded top of the post to prevent it from injuring the patient.
Then place the post under the patient's thigh, and stretch their arm or hand along the post, down to the lower parts, and tie the post to the support, arm, and hand.
Place the arm on a support (like a ladder) that is the proper width, and the hand is extended downwards, and the other side of the body is suspended (or pulled) to the other side, for the joint will quickly return to place.
And when the reduction is complete by any means possible, you must place a layer of wool under the thigh; then apply a prepared plaster of ground powder with vinegar and egg white over all the muscles, wrapping it around; then apply a proper bandage under the thigh; the bandage is wrapped over the plaster, and the patient's hand is tied to their neck.  Leave it in place and do not move their hand for seven days.
And the patient's food should be light, until the limb is strengthened, for this will aid in their recovery.
Then the diet should be increased after seven or five days, and some movement should be encouraged.  If it is firm and not softened, then it is now healed.
However, if the joint has been dislocated multiple times due to moisture or another cause, then a cautery with three punctures should be administered, as described previously in the chapter on cauteries.
And if all of this is done, and after seven days the bandage and the joint are not fixed, and the plaster and the bandage have been readjusted multiple times, and it is not fixed, and it cannot raise that arm to its upper limits, then know that the nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, are either cut or extended or softened, and know that the joint will never return to its proper place.
For dislocations of the foot and the breast or the back, reduction is achieved by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns to its place.
And another bandage should be applied to that area, and it should be treated until it is healed.
And if after healing there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use baths frequently until that heaviness is relieved and it returns to its initial natural state.
Chapter 27.
Concerning the treatment of elbow dislocations.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, and more prominently in the front and back directions.  Its dislocation is not hidden from you, for it is evident through the examination or touch, depending on the form of dislocation.  And when you align the dislocated elbow by the arm, this is plainly evident to you, for the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm, nor can it touch its own muscle.
And you should act quickly, and reduce the dislocation before it becomes acutely inflamed, for if it becomes acutely inflamed, the reduction is difficult, and the limb is not completely healed.  And especially when it's in the posterior parts, for it is the worst of all other types of dislocations, causing the most pain, and often leads to death.
And the restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the patient's arm should be extended with two hands, and their arm should be straightened. The physician's hands should be on the elbow, below it, and they should press the joint with the tips of their fingers together, or with the base of their palm, until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the anterior parts, then it returns by bending the arm with their hand, until the physician strikes the elbow with the base of their palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer vigorous extension and strong force.
And it is that two assistants extend the arm, and hold that extension so that it is not pulled back from the extension.
And then the arm should be turned in every direction, since it is wrapped with a long folded cloth over the patient's hand, or bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with their hand, anoint it with some oil so that this helps in the lubrication of the joint with ease; and then push the joint with a forceful impulse until it returns.
And after the reduction, it is necessary to place it on a compress in which there is astringency and application with egg white, and it should be tightened with a descending constriction, and the arm be suspended from the neck, and left for several days.
And then it should be loosened; and so, if the joint is formed in its place, then you loosen the bandage from it and leave it.
And if you see the joint is not well-strengthened, then you repeat the poultice and the bandage, and leave it for several days until it is strengthened; and then loosen it.
But if there is a worsening after the reduction of the joint, and its condition is moving, then administer moistening in a bath and gentle friction, and compression, until it is relieved; or place something oily on the affected joint; and then bandage it and leave it over the bone day and night. And then remove that, and put the affected area in a bath; and when it sweats, then you rub the joint with gentle friction, and then repeat the application of something oily, once, and the second time, and the third. with the aid of the bath, until it is healed.
And if you wish, place a warm, moist cow's hide over the affected area and bandage it tightly; and do this several times, for it is moistening and restoring it to its proper condition.
Chapter 28.
Of the treatment of a dislocated shoulder, that is, of the joint between the arm and the hand.
A dislocated shoulder sometimes occurs, and the reduction of its dislocation is straightforward; the opposite is true for other joints. However, speed is required in its reduction the moment it is dislocated, before the area becomes inflamed or develops a painful abscess.
And the reduction of the dislocation is that you place the patient's arm joint on a table, and the assistant extends the arm. The physician then places their hand, or palm, over the area above the joint, and pushes until it returns to its proper place.
However, it is necessary to consider if the palm is already turned towards the stomach of the hand. In that case, the patient places the back of their hand on a table, for extension and reduction.  If the dislocation is on the back of the hand, then the hand of the assistant should be placed flat on the table, so that the physician's hand can press directly on the area above the joint.
And if it returns quickly, it is well; but if not, then gently compress the area avoiding the formation of an abscess and leave it. Do not repeat this, for otherwise you will not be able to achieve a successful reduction after several days.
However, if the joint remains in its dislocated position, and the patient feels no pain, but the hand feels numb and cannot grip, then you should know that the nerves are broken or damaged.  In this case, there is no cure for the dislocation, but it should be compressed with a cautery near the point of damage.  A strong and constant pressure will induce some effect.
Therefore, once the dislocation is reduced, apply a compress as previously described and leave it in place and compress it for five days. Afterward, the compress should be removed and the patient's hand exercised.
But if the patient's movement is restricted and some heaviness develops, then alleviate it with warm water and some rubbing several times until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
Of the treatment of finger dislocations.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in various ways.
And when any of these fingers are dislocated toward the palm side or the back of the hand, then straighten the finger and push the dislocation back with your fingers, until it returns to its place.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it towards the part to which it was dislocated, and leave it like that for two days.
Then suspend it and extend it until it is properly aligned in its socket.
And when night comes, bind it according to that description, and do not cease to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for several days until it is straightened.
And do the same if it is dislocated towards the back of the hand, and bind it to that side and perform your first operation until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated in other parts of the body.
Chapter 30.
Concerning the treatment of dislocations of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation of any vertebra in the back or neck occurs, and many vertebrae are removed from their places, then there is no cure in that case, for death awaits the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's bowel movements are involuntary and they cannot control them; and often some of their limbs, or one or both legs or arms, are paralyzed, or a vertebra is out of place, because frequently they are displaced and sometimes the removal is in four different directions.
Therefore, this has already moved to the rear and is called a dislocation.
However, the treatment for this is that you should consider if the dislocation occurred in childhood, because then there is no cure for it, nor is it completely healed.
But for those that occur through accident or impact or similar causes, the Ancients described various methods of treatment at length, of which the most beneficial are retained; however, I will briefly summarize the most essential points, avoiding the unnecessary length, due to my closeness to the understanding of my exposition, by which I have explained and formulated this from diverse sources; from which I will demonstrate and clarify this.
And of those, only the one that occurs in the back, specifically as I describe, is curable.
And it is necessary that the patient be stretched out on their face, close to a wall, and be supported under them with soft material to prevent the foot from being damaged.
And then a straight pole should be placed, driven into the ground near the head of the patient, and another pole placed at the patient's feet at the opposite end of the slope.
And one attendant should hold the pole, and it should not be fixed in a hole; and another attendant should hold the other pole in the same manner.
And then wrap a strong linen bandage around the patient's head and under the forehead; and extend the end of the bandage to the pole placed at the head, and tie it there; then, tighten the other bandage around the patient's hips, thighs, and legs; and then fasten all the bandages and tie them to the other pole positioned at the feet.
And now, each attendant should extend the bandages towards the pole, and the two poles and bandages should not be moved from their positions, but not so firmly secured as we have described.
And the physician places their palms on the spine with force until it returns, or places a board there, and then the patient should adjust their feet on the board until they return to a normal position.
But if it does not return in this way, then take a board whose width or length is approximately three arms' length, and excavate a space in the wall near the patient where the end of the board fits inside. Then place half of the board over the spine, and the physician places their feet on the other end, and presses firmly until the spine is straightened and returns to its place.
And if you wish, use a turning implement held by hand; and you should place two poles, each one arm's length long, in the ground behind the patient's head and two more behind their feet; and let the space between each pair of poles be one handspan.  Make a hole in each pole for the turning implement to fit into, and securely fix each pole in the ground to prevent it from moving.
And place a round post on which the turning implement is placed, and rotate it through the two holes in the two poles.
And at the end of it [the round post] is a hole into which the pole [the round post itself] fits, the length of which is one handspan along with what is being turned; and in the other two poles is something similar to this.
Then tighten the bandages that you have wrapped around the patient's feet, around the hole that is towards their head, and the bandages you have wrapped around their thighs, around the turning implement that is towards their feet.
And beside each turning implement, let a helper turn the implement with their hand, and the physician aligns the spinal column as we have described.
And after the spinal column is repositioned and the area is aligned, then it is necessary to apply a dried plaster with egg white, and then a bandage; and then place on the plaster a splint of wood whose width is about three fingers, and whose length is sufficient to cover the area of the deformity, and over the spinal column area.
And then bind it with the bandage we have described, and have the patient use subtle nourishment until healed.
And if any lingering swelling remains in the area at the end of healing, then medicines that soften and cleanse with the table's application, which we have described, must be administered for a long time; and a lead plate is already applied in that area.
And sometimes swelling occurs at the back of the spinal vertebrae, and it is caused by that dislocation; and it is that bone which is swelling, therefore it should not be treated with this medicine, for it will strongly likely cause death.
Chapter 31.
Of the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the muscle joint should only be dislocated, and what happens to other joints with slight displacement and concavity does not happen to them.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inward, or outward, or forward, or backward.
And it is more commonly dislocated inward, and less so forward or backward.
A sign of a dislocated hip inward is that, when you align the patient's healthy leg with the affected leg, the affected leg is longer and the knee is more prominent than on the healthy side, and the patient cannot bend their foot toward the groin.  And the area where it is unable to bend is inflamed with a hot abscess, because the head of the thigh bone has now reached there.
The sign of a dislocated hip outward is that the symptoms are opposite to this inward dislocation.
The sign of a dislocated hip forward is that the patient's leg extends to its full length, but cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and struggles to go forward and cannot move it forward; and the patient's urine is retained and the groin is abscessed, and pain is present when walking on the heel.
The sign of a dislocated hip backward is that the knee does not extend, nor can it be bent forward as it is able to bend to the groin, and the patient's leg is shorter than the other, and the groin is swollen, and the head of the thigh bone is toward that area.
However, the reduction of these types of dislocations is that you should consider, then, if the dislocation is old and long-standing, that it has already become ingrained, then you apply reduction and it remains in its position.  Then it's not a complete cure; and therefore, no presumption should be made in that regard.
But in the case of a recent dislocation, and one of the four types of dislocation, then you should attempt to return the joint, extending it inwards and outwards, and moving it to the right and left, for the dislocation will easily return and will not need further treatment.
However, if it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare a strong assistant and extend the patient's leg from the lower part, either by using their own hands, or with a bandage tied above the knee; and another assistant should extend the patient's upper hand so that they place their hand below their armpits; and then tighten a strong bandage around the root of the thigh, and a third assistant should hold the end of the bandage.
And let the extension be either from the inner part to the groin, or from the back to the buttocks.
And let the extension of all of these be done at once until the patient is lifted off the ground by their body weight and remains suspended.
And this type of extension is a common method for all four [types of dislocation].
And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then for every type, it is necessary to proceed to the specific cure.
In the specific reduction of a dislocation, when it is dislocated and the dislocation is inward, the patient should be placed on their right side; then apply bandages to the root of the thigh, in the area between the thigh's head and the region below the groin; and then extend the bandage upward from the groin to the highest part of the groin.
And then another strong assistant should take the patient's arms, and forcefully grasp the thick part of the affected thigh and extend it outward by forceful extension, for this will return it to its proper place.
And this type is easier to treat, with which this limb is reduced than the other types.
But if you are hesitant and do not follow the instructions for this type of treatment completely, then the patient's two feet must be bound together with a strong, light bandage, around both ankles and both toes.
And the length of each one should be about four fingers, and the affected leg should be extended more than the other by two fingers.
And then the patient should be suspended upside down from the top of a post, and be in a position of suspension; and the distance from the ground should be about two arm lengths.
And then instruct a strong young man to grasp the top of the thigh, and have another young man suspend the patient, while the first young man, pulling the thigh with strength, will cause the joint to return to its proper place quickly.
However, in the proper reduction, when the dislocation is to the outside, the patient should lie on a support, as we have previously described while having the knee bent; and the bandage should be tight around the affected leg and foot.
And then two posts should be placed, one at the patient's feet and the other at their head; and then posts should be placed fitted in the middle of the hill, covered with damp cloths, so that the patient is not harmed by the posts being between their thighs, so that the extension towards the lower parts is not pulled.
And then each attendant should pull from their side, and the physician with their hand guides the reduction according to the art (or skill).
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then the leg should be slightly extended, and it is in accordance with this same method on a raised surface; and the physician places the palm of their right hand on the affected limb, then with the other hand, they apply pressure, and with that pressure, they extend the lower parts towards the front.
And if the dislocation is in the rear parts, then the patient should not be extended downwards, but elevated above the ground; instead, they should be placed on a hard surface, just as is required for the reduction if their hip is dislocated outwards, in accordance with what we have said regarding their position on two cushions; and they should be on their side, and the ligaments should be extended, as we have previously described, not much.
And pressure should be applied with a compress on the area where the joint is.
Therefore, when the reduction is completed as needed – and the sign of its reduction is clear to you, which is that you extend the patient's legs, and when you see that they are equal and the patient tightens their leg and expands it without excuse, then you know that the limb has been returned correctly – and then you put the legs together and apply a bandage and tighten it so that the hip is not moved in any direction; and keep the patient at rest for three or four days.
And then loosen the bandage and the splint, and compare one leg with the other.
Now, if you see them equal in appearance, then know that the dislocation is now fixed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any cause of swelling, then you return and bandage it, and tighten it according to your initial tightening, and leave it for three days.
And then loosen it, and have the patient walk on it gradually for a few days, until it is strengthened.
Chapter 32.
On the treatment of a dislocated knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated outward, inward, and downwards, as well as backwards; and it is not dislocated forwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the patient to apply their leg against their thigh; then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration in all these ways is that you should have the patient lie down straight, so that he/she extends their leg as much as possible; place your two hands on the affected side, and place their foot between your feet; then apply your palms to the knee, and hold them firmly between your fingers on the knee; and another attendant should extend the leg until the knee returns to its place.
And a sign of its reduction is that their leg adheres to the thigh with a light, effortless movement.
Then bandage it and apply the leg to the thigh, and then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then unbind them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for several days, until they are healed and strengthened.
But if reduction by you is unnecessary, then administer strong extension with ligaments, as described in the account of hip treatments that preceded this.
Chapter 33.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the heel.
The heel is sometimes dislocated by a small movement, and sometimes dislocated according to its completeness, and its dislocation is either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that the heel often swells on the side towards which it is dislocated.
But the treatment for a small displacement is a gentle return, and it is that it be pulled easily with the hands and held until it returns to place.
However, in treating a complete dislocation, you must have the patient lie straight, and have a strong and capable assistant on the affected side behind the patient's hips.
And then, you, with your right hand, take hold of the foot's highest point, and with your left hand, take hold of the foot's lowest point, near the ankle.
And then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with the left hand twice, and then push the foot's lower part with the right hand towards the leg without effort.
And do this twice, as we have counted.
And then push the foot's lower part against the leg a third time, and you're at the ankle.
So, if it returns in one or two attempts using this method, and if you see the foot is now aligned, it's good; and if not, then repeat the procedure on it, for it has returned.
But if you are prevented from reducing it with what we have recounted, then have the patient lie on their back on the ground, and place a firm, thick piece of cloth on the ground, and have it positioned between the patient's thighs, and you have already draped a cloth over them to prevent injury to the patient.
And then the patient's attendant holds their thighs, and another attendant stretches the leg with their hands or a bandage that ties around the ankle; and each attendant pulls against the other's pull, and the cloth lies between the patient's thighs, holding it in place, so that the patient's body is not pulled apart in the areas below their stretched leg.
And then the physician realigns the dislocation with their hands, and another attendant holds the healthy thigh in the lower regions, for the dislocation returns with ease.
And so, when the dislocation returns and you are certain of the successful reduction, then apply a plaster and bind it firmly with bandages, and bind the foot to the leg with bandages.
And be careful or guard against the nerves which are behind the heel, so that the bandage over it is not forceful and damages it severely.
And then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage is softened, tighten it.
And then loosen it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the patient from movement for 40 days. But if he is forced to walk before that period, there is no guarantee that the dislocation will not be re-displaced and corrupted, and consequently not receive a cure.
And if an abscess occurs nearby, then you must apply to that abscess what we previously described, in another location for treatment and fomentation, until it is resolved.
Chapter 34.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the toes of the foot.
The dislocated part should be pulled back gently, without exertion; and this is not difficult, it is rather easy.
But if the dislocation is in one of the joints of the foot, then you must have the patient place their foot on a smooth surface, like the ground or a table, and hold it in the position as if they were walking.
And then you stand, placing your foot on the area of the affected joint. Then press gently on it with your foot until it returns to its proper position, and you see it is now aligned and no longer protruding.
And then place a board beneath the sole of their foot that covers the entire foot, having two ends.  Then secure it firmly and properly for three days.  Afterwards, release it and keep them from walking for many days, until the joint is strengthened and the return to normal function is certain.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocations that are caused by a blow, or by a fracture, or by both together.
When any of these causes occur, and you exert yourself greatly in treating and restoring them, death often results.
Therefore, one should not presume to treat such cases, but only those who are skilled in the art, having long experience, compassionate and patient, cautious, not rash or presumptuous; and who, at the beginning, should use only medicines that soothe inflamed abscesses, and leave the patient to divine judgment, except for those in whom one hopes for a cure from destruction, with alleviation of the illness, and where some hope appears. Then, strive immediately to restore it [the dislocated joint] at the onset of the condition, before it becomes a hot abscess.
And if the limb returns as desired, then administer a regimen that will soothe a hot abscess and treat the injury with appropriate desiccant ointments.
And if the dislocation is accompanied by a fracture and the bones are separated, then you must treat the fracture, and do in this case what we have said in the simple diseases of what precedes in the treatment of those locations.
And, according to your ability, avoid and escape from the trap of fear in the ways that my instruction has shown you; for this is better for you before your Creator, and more enduring for your glory, and more health to your true being in your own time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis, perfectly, for which may God be blessed. Amen.
